Rule for Conclusion Modality with Descriptive Majors
Author states rule for conclusion modality and presents a table of results for mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for determining the conclusion modality in mixed syllogisms when the major premise is a descriptive proposition, and identify when adjustments for non-permanence are needed.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality follows the major unless the major is a descriptive, then it mirrors the corrected converse of the minor.

This passage from al-Qutbi's logic text presents a precise rule for deriving the conclusion's modal status in mixed syllogisms. The rule distinguishes cases based on whether the major premise is a 'descriptive' proposition. Understanding this rule is crucial for ensuring that syllogistic arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence are valid not only in form but also in modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of major premise
Determine if the major is one of the four descriptives (or the nine remaining types). This classification is based on the modality of the major, such as being 'necessary', 'permanent', 'temporal', etc.
The four descriptives are a subset of modal propositions that predicate non-essential attributes.
2
Apply the basic rule for non-descriptive majors
If the major is not one of the four descriptives, the conclusion's modality is identical to the major's modality. This is straightforward.
This applies when the major is, for example, a necessary or permanent proposition.
3
Apply the special rule for descriptive majors
If the major is one of the four descriptives, the conclusion takes the form of the converse of the minor, but with two potential adjustments: remove the non-permanence term from the converse if present, and add the non-permanence of the major if the major is one of the two particularizing descriptives.
The converse of the minor must be constructed first, then modified.
4
Understand the justification and subtlety
The methods (reductio, conversion, hypothesis) are standard. Note the subtlety: if the minor is permanent and the major is absolute general, the rule would yield an absolute general conclusion, but the correct result is absolute temporal. This is detailed in Sharh al-Matali'.
This shows the rule is not mechanical; logical insight is needed.
5
Consult the table for verification
The provided table lists all combinations of minor and major types and their resulting conclusion modalities. Use it to verify your application of the rule.
The table covers the second section of mixtures; the first section is assumed already covered.

And the major in it is either one of the four descriptives or not. If it is one of the four descriptives — rather one of the nine remaining — then the modality of the conclusion is the modality of the major itself. And if it is one of the four, then the conclusion is like the converse of the minor, with the non-permanence removed from it — if the converse is restricted by it — and with the non-permanence of the major added to it — if it is one of the two particularizing. As for whether the conclusion is like the major or like the converse of the minor, it is by the mentioned methods of reductio, conversion, and hypothesis, as previously explained. And as for the removal of non-permanence from the converse of the minor, it is because the converse of the minor (1) is disputable, because if the minor is one of the two permanent and the major is absolute general, then according to the mentioned principle the conclusion would be absolute general, but the truth is that the conclusion is absolute temporal, and its detail is sought from Sharh al-Matali' (Sharif). And the detail of the results of mixtures of the second section are in this table: [table]
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage explains the rule for determining the conclusion's modality in mixed syllogisms when the major premise is a descriptive proposition. The author first states the general rule: if the major is one of the four descriptives (or one of the nine remaining types mentioned earlier), then the conclusion shares the modality of the major. However, if the major is specifically one of the four descriptives, the conclusion resembles the converse of the minor, but with two adjustments: the non-permanence term is removed from the converse (if it was present), and the non-permanence of the major is added (if the major is one of the two particularizing descriptives). The author then explains that the methods for deriving this conclusion are the same as those previously discussed: reductio ad absurdum (khalf), conversion (‘aks), and hypothesis (iftirad). He also addresses a subtle point regarding the removal of non-permanence from the converse of the minor, noting that if the minor is one of the two permanent types (e.g., necessary or permanent) and the major is absolute general, the principle would yield an absolute general conclusion, but the correct view is that the conclusion is absolute temporal. This is detailed in Sharh al-Matali'. Finally, the author provides a table that enumerates the results for all combinations of minors and majors in the second section of mixtures. The table lists various types of minors (e.g., common conditional, general customary, particular conditional, particular customary, etc.) and majors (necessary, permanent, absolute general, etc.), and shows the resulting conclusion modality (e.g., absolute temporal, absolute permanent, existential non-permanent, etc.). For example, when the minor is common conditional and the major is necessary, the conclusion is absolute temporal. This table serves as a comprehensive reference for the practitioner of modal logic. The examples are purely logical and relate to the classification of propositions in terms of necessity, permanence, and temporality, which are foundational in Islamic logic for analyzing arguments in theology (kalam) and jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh).

First Three Conditions for Fourth-Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author lists first three conditions for fourth-figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first three modal conditions to check whether a given fourth-figure mixed syllogism is valid.

Core Idea
Fourth-figure mixed syllogisms need actual premises, convertible negatives, and permanent minors.

In the fourth figure, converting premises and handling modalities is tricky. The author specifies five conditions for a mixed syllogism to yield a necessary conclusion. Here we examine the first three: the premises must be actual (not possible), the universal negative must be convertible, and the minor premise of the third mood must be permanently true. Understanding these conditions helps avoid modal fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall topic
Recognize that the passage is listing conditions for productive mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure. Mixed means premises have different modalities.
2
Parse the first condition: actuality of premises
The first condition says the syllogism must be from 'actualities' (fi'liyyat). This means both premises must be actual propositions, not possible or necessary. Actuality here indicates the proposition is true in real situations.
In modal logic, actuality contrasts with possibility and necessity.
3
Parse the second condition: conversion of the universal negative
The second condition requires that the universal negative proposition used in the syllogism be convertible. In the fourth figure, conversion is often needed to obtain a valid mood. A universal negative 'No A is B' converts to 'No B is A'.
Conversion must preserve truth; for universal negatives it does.
4
Parse the third condition: permanence of the minor in the third mood
The third condition states that the minor premise of the third mood (within the fourth figure) must be permanently true. 'Permanence' means the proposition holds at all times or is necessary.
The passage cuts off here; the remaining two conditions are on the next page.
5
Apply the conditions to a sample syllogism
Take a fourth-figure syllogism, e.g., 'No prayer is invalid (actual), every invalid act is forbidden (actual), therefore some forbidden act is not a prayer?' Check: premises are actual? Yes. Is there a universal negative? Yes, 'No prayer is invalid' – it converts to 'No invalid act is a prayer'. Is the minor premise of the third mood permanently true? The minor is 'every invalid act is forbidden' – if it is always true, condition satisfied.
Remember, the passage only covers the first three conditions; full validation requires all five.

Source Text

He said: As for the fourth figure, its condition for production according to modality are five matters: First, that the syllogism in it is from actualities. Second, the conversion of the universal negative used in it. Third, the truth of permanence for the minor of the third mood or the general 'arabi upon...
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the conditions for producing valid mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi'). The author enumerates five conditions that must be satisfied for the syllogism to yield a necessary conclusion when modalities are involved. The first condition is that the syllogism must be composed of actual propositions (fi'liyyat), meaning propositions that describe actual states rather than merely possible ones. The second condition is the conversion of the universal negative used in the syllogism; since the fourth figure often requires conversion, the negative must be convertible. The third condition is that the minor premise of the third mood (or the general 'arabi) must be permanently true. Unfortunately, the text cuts off before the remaining two conditions are stated. In the tradition of Islamic logic, these conditions ensure that the modal relationships between premises are correctly transferred to the conclusion, avoiding fallacies. For example, in a fourth-figure syllogism with premises like 'Every A is B necessarily' and 'No B is C actually', the conversion of the negative must be permissible to draw a valid conclusion. This passage is part of a larger commentary expanding on the concise rules of al-Risala al-Shamsiyya.

First Condition: No Possible Premises in Fourth Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author states first condition and explains with counterexamples for possible affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a possible premise in a fourth figure mixed syllogism leads to an invalid conclusion and explain the counterexample.

Core Idea
A single possible premise, affirmative or negative, destroys fourth figure validity.

The first condition for productive fourth figure mixed syllogisms is that the syllogism must consist entirely of actuality propositions. The author argues that possible negatives are disallowed because they fail convertibility (second condition), and possible affirmatives, whether minor or major, produce counterexamples. Let's examine the reasoning behind banning possible affirmatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the fourth figure
Recall that in the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate in the major premise and the subject in the minor. This structure imposes special restrictions on modality.
The fourth figure is the least productive, and modal conditions are strict.
2
Understand the first condition: only actualities
The author states that the syllogism must be composed of 'fi'liyyat' (actuality propositions), meaning no possible (mumkina) premises should be used.
Possible propositions assert that something is not impossible, e.g., 'Every brayer is possibly a mount of Zayd'.
3
Analyze the counterexample when the minor is a possible affirmative
Consider the premises: 'Every brayer is possibly a mount of Zayd' (possible affirmative minor) and 'Every donkey is necessarily a brayer' (necessity major). The conclusion 'Every donkey is a mount of Zayd' is false, yet both premises could be true. This shows invalidity.
The minor is possible affirmative, the major is necessary; the conclusion is affirmative but false.
4
Analyze the counterexample when the major is a possible affirmative
Consider: 'Every mount of Zayd is necessarily a horse' (necessity minor) and 'Every donkey is possibly a mount of Zayd' (possible affirmative major). The conclusion 'Every donkey is a horse' is false. Changing the major to another possible may yield a true conclusion, but the form is unreliable.
The major is possible affirmative; the conclusion is false, demonstrating invalidity.
5
Generalize the condition
Therefore, to ensure productivity, no possible premise—affirmative or negative—can be used. Possible negatives fail due to convertibility; possible affirmatives cause counterexamples.
This condition is specific to the fourth figure; other figures allow possible premises under certain conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما الشكل الرابع فشرط إنتاجه بحسب الجهة أمور خمسة:
الأول كون القياس فيه من الفعليات.
الثاني انعكاس السالبة المستعملة فيه.
الثالث صدق الدوام على صغرى الضرب الثالث أو العربي العام على
الرابع كون الكبرى في السادس من المنعكسة السوالب.
شرح الرسالة الشمسية
الخامس كون الصغرى في الثامن من إحدي الخاصتين والكبرى مما يصدق عليها العربي العام.
اقول: لإنتاج الشكل الرابع بحسب الجهة شرائط خمسة:
الأول: كون القياس فيه من الفعليات حتى لا تستعمل فيه الممكنة أصلا، لأن الممكنة إما أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة، وأيا ما كان لا ينتج: أما الممكنة السالبة فلما سيأتي في الشرط الثاني من وجوب انعكاس السالبة فيه. وأما الممكنة الموجبة فلأنها إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وعلى كلا التقديرين يتحقق الاختلاف: أما إذا كانت صغرى فلصدق قولنا في الفرض المذكور: كل ناهق مركوب زيد بالإمكان، وكل حمار ناهق بالضرورة مع أن الحق السلب، وصدق هذا الاختلاط مع حقيقة الإيجاب كثير. وأما إذا كانت كبرى فكقولنا: كل مركوب زيد فرس بالضرورة، وكل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان الخاص مع امتناع الإيجاب، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: وكل صاهل مركوب زيد بالإمكان كان الحق الإيجاب.
الشرط الثاني أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيه منعكسة، لأن أخص السوالب الغير المنعكسة هي السالبة الوقتية، وهي إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وأيا ما كان لم ينتج: أما إذا كانت صغرى فلصدق قولنا: «لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالتوقيت لادائماً، وكل ذي محو فهو قمر بالضرورة» والحق الإيجاب.
He said: As for the fourth figure, the condition for its productivity with respect to modality is five matters: First, that the syllogism in it is of actualities. Second, the conversion of the negative used in it. Third, the truth of persistence on the minor of the third mood or the general conventional on... Fourth, that the major in the sixth is from the converted negatives. Fifth, that the minor in the eighth is from one of the two specifics and the major is such that the general conventional applies to it. I say: For the productivity of the fourth figure with respect to modality there are five conditions: First, the syllogism in it must be of actualities so that the possible is not used at all, because the possible is either affirmative or negative, and whichever it is does not produce: As for the possible negative, it is because of what will come in the second condition of the necessity of conversion of the negative in it. As for the possible affirmative, it is because it is either minor or major, and in both cases difference occurs: For if it is minor, then our statement in the mentioned supposition is true: 'Every brayer is a mount of Zayd possibly, and every donkey is a brayer necessarily' while the truth is denial, and the truth of this mixture with the reality of affirmation is frequent. And if it is major, then like our statement: 'Every mount of Zayd is a horse necessarily, and every donkey is a mount of Zayd with specific possibility' while affirmation is impossible, and if we change the major to our statement: 'And every neighing is a mount of Zayd possibly' then the truth would be affirmation. The second condition: that the negative used in it is convertible, because the most specific of non-convertible negatives is the temporal negative, and it is either minor or major, and whichever it is does not produce: For if it is minor, then our statement is true: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed with temporariness not-permanently, and everything that has obliteration is the moon necessarily' while the truth is affirmation.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage lists and explains the five modal conditions required for mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure to produce a valid conclusion. The author first enumerates the conditions: (1) The syllogism must consist of actuality propositions (i.e., no possible premises). (2) The negative premise used must be convertible (i.e., can be simply converted). (3) The minor of the third mood must be persistently true or covered by the general conventional. (4) In the sixth mood, the major must be among convertible negatives. (5) In the eighth mood, the minor must be one of the two specific types and the major must be such that the general conventional applies to it. The commentator then expands on the first two conditions. For the first, he explains that possible propositions, whether affirmative or negative, must be avoided. Possible negatives are disallowed because they fail the second condition's requirement of convertibility. Possible affirmatives, whether minor or major, lead to counterexamples: if the minor is possible, one can have 'Every brayer is possibly a mount of Zayd' and 'Every donkey is necessarily a brayer', yet the conclusion 'Every donkey is a mount of Zayd' is false, while the premises are true. If the major is possible, a similar counterexample involving 'mount of Zayd' and 'horse' shows the invalidity. For the second condition, he focuses on the temporal negative (a non-convertible type) and shows that if it is used as minor or major, it fails to produce a valid conclusion. For example, 'Nothing of the moon is permanently eclipsed at a time' and 'Everything that undergoes obliteration is necessarily the moon' would yield a false affirmative conclusion. The commentary uses examples from everyday objects (donkeys, horses, the moon) to illustrate logical points, adhering to the tradition of using concrete, often non-religious, examples in logic. Students should note that these conditions are specific to the fourth figure and modal logic, emphasizing the need for careful selection of premises.

Key Takeaway

Second Condition: Negative Premise Must Be Convertible in Fourth Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author explains second condition and shows counterexample with temporal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a non-convertible negative premise (e.g., temporal negative) causes invalidity in a fourth figure mixed syllogism.

Core Idea
The negative premise in the fourth figure must be convertible; a temporal negative always spoils the syllogism.

The second condition requires that the negative premise used in a fourth figure mixed syllogism be convertible. The author identifies the temporal negative as the most specific non-convertible type and shows counterexamples whether it serves as minor or major. Let's examine why convertibility matters.

Guided Path
1
Recall the need for conversion in the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the conclusion is often obtained by converting one of the premises. A non-convertible negative cannot be validly converted, ruining the syllogism.
Conversion is an immediate inference where subject and predicate swap, preserving truth only under certain conditions.
2
Identify the temporal negative as the paradigm non-convertible negative
The temporal negative (saliba waqtīya) asserts that something is not the case at a particular time, e.g., 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at time t'. Such negatives are not convertible because the converse would be false.
Temporal negatives are the most specific non-convertible type.
3
Analyze the counterexample when the temporal negative is the minor
Consider: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at time t (non-permanent)' (temporal negative minor) and 'Every eclipsed object is the moon necessarily' (necessary major). The conclusion would be an affirmative 'Every eclipsed object is the moon', which is false. The minor is non-convertible, so the syllogism fails.
The minor is a temporal negative; the major is necessary; the conclusion should be negative but here it's affirmative? Actually the example shows that the conclusion 'Everything that has obliteration is the moon' is false. The point is that the form produces an invalid inference.
4
Analyze the counterexample when the temporal negative is the major
The author notes that if the temporal negative is the major, similarly it fails. (The passage implies a similar counterexample.)
The symmetry ensures that whichever premise is non-convertible, the syllogism is unproductive.
5
Generalize the condition
Therefore, the negative premise must be convertible. Convertible negatives include simple negatives (e.g., 'No S is P') which can be converted to 'No P is S'.
This condition interacts with the first: possible negatives are not convertible, hence also banned.

الثاني انعكاس السالبة المستعملة فيه.
الثالث صدق الدوام على صغرى الضرب الثالث أو العربي العام على
الرابع كون الكبرى في السادس من المنعكسة السوالب.

الخامس كون الصغرى في الثامن من إحدي الخاصتين والكبرى مما يصدق عليها العربي العام.
اقول: لإنتاج الشكل الرابع بحسب الجهة شرائط خمسة:
الأول: كون القياس فيه من الفعليات حتى لا تستعمل فيه الممكنة أصلا، لأن الممكنة إما أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة، وأيا ما كان لا ينتج: أما الممكنة السالبة فلما سيأتي في الشرط الثاني من وجوب انعكاس السالبة فيه. وأما الممكنة الموجبة فلأنها إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وعلى كلا التقديرين يتحقق الاختلاف: أما إذا كانت صغرى فلصدق قولنا في الفرض المذكور: كل ناهق مركوب زيد بالإمكان، وكل حمار ناهق بالضرورة مع أن الحق السلب، وصدق هذا الاختلاط مع حقيقة الإيجاب كثير. وأما إذا كانت كبرى فكقولنا: كل مركوب زيد فرس بالضرورة، وكل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان الخاص مع امتناع الإيجاب، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: وكل صاهل مركوب زيد بالإمكان كان الحق الإيجاب.
الشرط الثاني أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيه منعكسة، لأن أخص السوالب الغير المنعكسة هي السالبة الوقتية، وهي إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وأيا ما كان لم ينتج: أما إذا كانت صغرى فلصدق قولنا: «لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالتوقيت لادائماً، وكل ذي محو فهو قمر بالضرورة» والحق الإيجاب.
He said: As for the fourth figure, the condition for its productivity with respect to modality is five matters: First, that the syllogism in it is of actualities. Second, the conversion of the negative used in it. Third, the truth of persistence on the minor of the third mood or the general conventional on... Fourth, that the major in the sixth is from the converted negatives. Fifth, that the minor in the eighth is from one of the two specifics and the major is such that the general conventional applies to it. I say: For the productivity of the fourth figure with respect to modality there are five conditions: First, the syllogism in it must be of actualities so that the possible is not used at all, because the possible is either affirmative or negative, and whichever it is does not produce: As for the possible negative, it is because of what will come in the second condition of the necessity of conversion of the negative in it. As for the possible affirmative, it is because it is either minor or major, and in both cases difference occurs: For if it is minor, then our statement in the mentioned supposition is true: 'Every brayer is a mount of Zayd possibly, and every donkey is a brayer necessarily' while the truth is denial, and the truth of this mixture with the reality of affirmation is frequent. And if it is major, then like our statement: 'Every mount of Zayd is a horse necessarily, and every donkey is a mount of Zayd with specific possibility' while affirmation is impossible, and if we change the major to our statement: 'And every neighing is a mount of Zayd possibly' then the truth would be affirmation. The second condition: that the negative used in it is convertible, because the most specific of non-convertible negatives is the temporal negative, and it is either minor or major, and whichever it is does not produce: For if it is minor, then our statement is true: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed with temporariness not-permanently, and everything that has obliteration is the moon necessarily' while the truth is affirmation.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage lists and explains the five modal conditions required for mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure to produce a valid conclusion. The author first enumerates the conditions: (1) The syllogism must consist of actuality propositions (i.e., no possible premises). (2) The negative premise used must be convertible (i.e., can be simply converted). (3) The minor of the third mood must be persistently true or covered by the general conventional. (4) In the sixth mood, the major must be among convertible negatives. (5) In the eighth mood, the minor must be one of the two specific types and the major must be such that the general conventional applies to it. The commentator then expands on the first two conditions. For the first, he explains that possible propositions, whether affirmative or negative, must be avoided. Possible negatives are disallowed because they fail the second condition's requirement of convertibility. Possible affirmatives, whether minor or major, lead to counterexamples: if the minor is possible, one can have 'Every brayer is possibly a mount of Zayd' and 'Every donkey is necessarily a brayer', yet the conclusion 'Every donkey is a mount of Zayd' is false, while the premises are true. If the major is possible, a similar counterexample involving 'mount of Zayd' and 'horse' shows the invalidity. For the second condition, he focuses on the temporal negative (a non-convertible type) and shows that if it is used as minor or major, it fails to produce a valid conclusion. For example, 'Nothing of the moon is permanently eclipsed at a time' and 'Everything that undergoes obliteration is necessarily the moon' would yield a false affirmative conclusion. The commentary uses examples from everyday objects (donkeys, horses, the moon) to illustrate logical points, adhering to the tradition of using concrete, often non-religious, examples in logic. Students should note that these conditions are specific to the fourth figure and modal logic, emphasizing the need for careful selection of premises.

Key Terms
سالبة منعكسة
سالبة وقتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The universal negative

Key Takeaway
In the fourth figure, the negative premise must be convertible; a temporal negative, being non-convertible, always invalidates the syllogism.

Is the negative premise convertible? Does the syllogism produce a valid conclusion?

First Condition for Fourth Figure: Excluding Possible Propositions
Author explains the first condition: only actual propositions, excluding possible ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first condition for a valid fourth-figure modal syllogism and explain why possible propositions are excluded.

Core Idea
In the fourth figure, only actual propositions are allowed; possible propositions always lead to invalid conclusions.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has special modal conditions. The author here lays out the first of five: the premises must be actual (necessary or perpetual), not merely possible. He demonstrates with concrete examples why possible propositions, whether positive or negative, cannot yield a valid conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Locate the statement that the syllogism must be composed of actual propositions (فعليات). This is the first of five conditions for the fourth figure.
The Arabic phrase is 'كون القياس فيه من الفعليات'.
2
Analyze the exclusion of possible negatives
The author defers the explanation for possible negatives to the second condition about conversion. Note that he only mentions it without proof here.
Possible negatives are excluded because of the necessity of converting the negative premise in the fourth figure.
3
Analyze the exclusion of possible positives
The author gives two cases: possible positive as minor and as major. In each case, a modal conflict (اختلاف) arises, causing the conclusion to be false. Identify the conflict in the examples.
The conflict is between a necessary premise and a possible premise, leading to a conclusion that does not match reality.
4
Test with a concrete example
Take the example: 'Every braying thing is ridden by Zayd possibly' (minor) and 'Every donkey is a braying thing necessarily' (major). Predict the conclusion and check if it holds. The author says the true conclusion is negative, showing the syllogism is invalid.
The modal mismatch (necessary + possible) makes the standard categorical form invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اقول: لإنتاج الشكل الرابع بحسب الجهة شرائط خمسة : الأول : كون القياس فيه من الفعليات حتى لا تستعمل فيه الممكنة أصلا، لأن الممكنة إما أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة، وأيا ما كان لا ينتج : أما الممكنة السالبة فلما سيأتي في الشرط الثاني من وجوب انعكاس السالبة فيه. وأما الممكنة الموجبة فلأنها إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وعلى كلا التقديرين يتحقق الاختلاف : أما إذا كانت صغرى فلصدق قولنا في الفرض المذكور : كل ناهق مركوب زيد بالإمكان، وكل حمار ناهق بالضرورة مع أن الحق السلب، وصدق هذا الاختلاط مع حقيقة الإيجاب كثير. وأما إذا كانت كبرى فكقولنا : كل مركوب زيد فرس بالضرورة، وكل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان الخاص مع امتناع الإيجاب، ولوبدلنا الكبرى بقولنا : وكلّ صاهل مركوب زيد بالإمكان كان الحق الإيجاب.
I say: For the production of the fourth figure according to modality, there are five conditions: First: That the syllogism in it be from actual propositions so that the possible is not used at all, because the possible is either positive or negative, and whichever it is, it does not produce. As for the possible negative, because of what will come in the second condition regarding the necessity of the conversion of the negative in it. And as for the possible positive, because it is either minor or major, and in both estimations the conflict occurs. If it is minor, then the truth of our statement in the mentioned supposition: 'Every braying thing is ridden by Zayd possibly, and every donkey is a braying thing necessarily' while the truth is the negative, and the truth of this mixture with the reality of affirmation is frequent. And if it is major, then like our statement: 'Every ridden by Zayd is a horse necessarily, and every donkey is ridden by Zayd with a special possibility' while affirmation is impossible, and if we replace the major with our statement: 'And every neighing thing is ridden by Zayd possibly' then the affirmation would be true.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage defines the first condition for the fourth figure with respect to modality. The author states that only actual propositions (فعليات) are allowed; possible propositions are excluded entirely. He explains that possible negatives are excluded because of the need for conversion (condition 2). For possible positives, whether as minor or major, they lead to a conflict (اختلاف) where the conclusion would be false. He gives examples: If the minor is possible affirmative, e.g., 'Every braying thing is ridden by Zayd possibly' (minor), 'Every donkey is a braying thing necessarily' (major), the necessary conclusion would be 'Every donkey is ridden by Zayd possibly' but the true conclusion is the negative (i.e., no donkey is ridden by Zayd) — so the syllogism fails. Similarly, if the major is possible affirmative, the example shows impossibility of the affirmative conclusion. If we change the major to a different possible proposition, the conclusion becomes true, showing that the modality must be actual.

الممكنة
الاختلاف

Key Takeaway
In the fourth figure, possible propositions are excluded because they produce modal conflict leading to false conclusions; only actual (necessary or perpetual) premises ensure validity.

Second Condition: Negative Must Be Convertible
Author explains that the negative used in the fourth figure must be convertible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a negative proposition used in a fourth-figure syllogism is convertible and determine its effect on validity.

Core Idea
Non-convertible negatives break fourth-figure syllogisms.

Having established the first condition for the fourth figure, the author now turns to the second: the negative premise must be convertible (munʿakisa). He identifies temporal negatives (al-saliba al-waqtiyya) as the most specific non-convertible negatives and shows that whether placed in the minor or major premise, the syllogism fails. This lesson walks you through his reasoning and teaches you to test convertibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative premise
In the fourth figure, locate the premise that is negative. In the examples, the temporal negative 'No moon is eclipsed temporally' is used as either minor or major.
2
Classify the negative as perpetual or temporal
Determine if the negative holds for all time (perpetual, convertible) or only at a specific time (temporal, non-convertible). The temporal negative is true at some times but not always.
Perpetual negatives like 'No human is immortal' are convertible.
3
Apply the conversion test
Swap subject and predicate of the negative. If the truth is preserved, it is convertible. For temporal negatives, conversion fails: 'No moon is eclipsed temporally' does not imply 'Nothing eclipsed temporally is the moon' since the moon itself is temporally eclipsed.
The author's examples show the absurd results.
4
Assess the syllogism's validity
With a non-convertible negative, the conclusion will be false or impossible. For the minor-premise case, the conclusion contradicts the known fact that some eclipse-possessors (the moon) are eclipsed temporally. For the major-premise case, the conclusion 'No moon is an eclipse-possessor' is impossible.
Both cases demonstrate the necessity of convertibility.
5
Formulate the condition
Conclude that for a valid fourth-figure syllogism with a negative premise, that premise must be convertible (i.e., not temporal or any non-convertible type).
This ensures the syllogism produces a correct conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الثاني أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيه منعكسة، لأن أخص السوالب الغير المنعكسة هي السالبة الوقتية، وهي إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وأيا ما كان لم ينتج : أما إذا كانت صغرى فلصدق قولنا : «لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالتوقيت لادائماً، وكلّ ذي محو فهو قمر بالضرورة» والحق الإيجاب. وأما إذا كانت كبرى فلصدق قولنا كل منخسف فهو ذومحو بالضرورة، ولاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالتوقيت لادائماً، مع امتناع السلب.
The second condition: That the negative used in it be convertible, because the most specific of the non-convertible negatives is the temporal negative, and it is either minor or major, and whichever it is, it does not produce. If it is minor, then the truth of our statement: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed temporally, not perpetually, and everything possessing eclipse is the moon necessarily' while the truth is the affirmative. And if it is major, then the truth of our statement: 'Everything eclipsed possesses eclipse necessarily, and nothing of the moon is eclipsed temporally, not perpetually' while the negative is impossible.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage explains the second condition: the negative proposition used in the fourth figure must be convertible (منعكسة). The author identifies the most specific non-convertible negative as the temporal negative (السالبة الوقتية), which is true at some time but not perpetual. He shows that whether the temporal negative is the minor or major premise, the syllogism fails to yield a true conclusion. As minor: 'No moon is eclipsed temporally (non-perpetually)' and 'Every eclipse-possessing thing is the moon necessarily' leads to the conclusion that no eclipse-possessing thing is eclipsed temporally, but the true conclusion is affirmative (i.e., some are eclipsed). As major: 'Everything eclipsed is eclipse-possessing necessarily' and 'No moon is eclipsed temporally' leads to the conclusion that no moon is eclipse-possessing, which is impossible because the moon does have eclipse. Hence the temporal negative is invalid.

Key Terms

الانعكاس
المنعكسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the fourth figure, any negative premise must be convertible; otherwise, the syllogism yields a false or impossible conclusion.

Third Condition: Perpetuity in Minor and Convertibility in Major
Author explains the modal conditions for the third mood of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a modal categorical syllogism in the third mood of the fourth figure satisfies the third condition by checking the minor's modality and the major's convertibility.

Core Idea
Modal syllogisms require not just the right mood but also specific modal conditions like perpetuity or convertibility.

In this passage, the author details the third condition for the third mood of the fourth figure. This condition ensures that the minor premise is necessary or perpetual, or the major is from the convertible negatives. Without this condition, invalid combinations arise, as he demonstrates with a concrete example from astronomy. Understanding this condition is crucial for evaluating the validity of modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal terms
The minor must be necessary (ḍarūriyya) or perpetual (dāʾima); the major must be from the six convertible negatives (e.g., ʿurfī ʿāmm). Look for these qualifiers in the premises.
Perpetual means true at all times; convertible negatives are those that become true when converted.
2
Check the minor's modality
Is the minor premise necessary or perpetual? If not, the condition fails unless the major is convertible.
The author lists 11 non-necessary/non-perpetual minor types if condition is absent.
3
Check the major's convertibility
If the minor condition fails, the major must be from the six convertible negatives. Determine if the major can be converted without changing truth value.
Convertible negatives include propositions like the general conventional (ʿurfī ʿāmm).
4
Evaluate the combination
If neither condition holds, only a few combinations remain (descriptive minor + one of seven majors). The author shows the most specific such combination fails, using the eclipsed moon example.
The example: minor must be perpetual or major convertible; otherwise conclusion is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الثالث : أن يصدق الدوام في الضرب الثالث على صغراه بأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة، أو العرفي العام على كبراه بأن تكون من القضايا الست المنعكسة السوالب فإنه لوانتفى الأمران كانت الصغرى إحدى القضايا الغير الضرورية والدائمة وهي إحدى عشرة والكبرى إحدى السبع ، لكن لما كانت الصغرى في هذا الضرب سالبة – وقدتبين أن السالبة المستعملة في هذا الشكل يجب أن تكون منعكسة – سقط من تلك الجملة اختلاط صغرى إحدى السبع مع الكبريات السبع، فلم يبق إلا اختلاط صغرى إحدى الوصفيات الأربع مع إحدى السبع، وأخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة والكبريات الوقتية، وهي لا تنتج معها فلم تنتج البواقي. وذلك لأنه يصدق «لاشيء من المنخسف بمضيء بالإضاءة القمرية بالضرورة مادام منخسفاً لا دائماً، وكلّ قمر منخسف بالتوقيت لا دائماً» مع امتناع سلب القمر عن المضيء بالإضاءة القمرية. واعلم أنّ البيان في الشرط الثاني والثالث إنما يتم لوبين فيهما امتناع الإيجاب حتى يلزم الاختلاف، لكن لم يظفر بصورة نقض يدلّ عليه.
The third condition: That perpetuity holds true in the third mood regarding its minor, such that it is necessary or perpetual, or the general conventional [holds true] regarding its major, such that it is from the six convertible negative propositions. For if both are absent, then the minor would be one of the non-necessary and non-perpetual propositions, which are eleven, and the major would be one of the seven. But since the minor in this mood is negative – and it has been shown that the negative used in this figure must be convertible – there falls from that total the mixture of a minor from the seven with the seven majors. So nothing remains except the mixture of a minor from the four descriptive (وصفيات) categories with one of the seven. And the most specific of the minor propositions are the special conditional and the major are the temporal, and they do not produce with it, so the rest do not produce. That is because it is true: 'Nothing of the eclipsed is luminous by moonlight necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not perpetually, and every moon is eclipsed temporally not perpetually' while the negation of the moon from the luminous by moonlight is impossible. And know that the demonstration in the second and third conditions is only complete if the impossibility of affirmation is shown in them so that the conflict follows, but he did not find a counterexample that indicates it.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage explains the third condition concerning the third mood (الضرب الثالث) of the fourth figure. The author requires that either the minor premise is necessary or perpetual, or the major premise is from the six convertible negatives (the general conventional). He analyzes the case where neither condition holds: the minor would be from eleven non-necessary/non-perpetual types, and the major from seven types. However, because the minor in this mood is negative and must be convertible, many combinations are eliminated. Only mixtures of a minor from the four 'descriptive' categories (وصفيات) with a major from the seven remain. The most specific such minor is the 'special conditional' (مشروطة خاصة) and the major is the 'temporal' (وقتية), and they do not yield a valid conclusion, hence none of the remaining possibilities work. He provides an example to illustrate the conflict: 'Nothing eclipsed is luminous by moonlight necessarily while eclipsed (non-perpetually), and every moon is eclipsed temporally (non-perpetually)' leads to the impossible conclusion that the moon is not luminous by moonlight. Then he notes that the demonstration for conditions 2 and 3 would require showing impossibility of the affirmative conclusion to achieve conflict, but no clear counterexample for that is found.

الضرب الثالث
الدوام
العرفي العام
المشروطة الخاصة

Key Takeaway
The third condition for the third mood of the fourth figure requires either the minor to be necessary/perpetual or the major to be from the convertible negatives; otherwise, no valid conclusion follows.

Third Condition for the Third Mixed Mode
Third condition for third mixed mode with lunar eclipse example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given pair of modal premises satisfies the third condition for validity in the third mixed mode.

Core Idea
Not all true modal premise pairs yield a valid syllogism; modal qualifiers must align.

In modal syllogistics, the third mixed mode of the first figure has specific restrictions on the modalities of its premises. The third condition states that either the minor must be necessary or permanent, or the major must be from the six convertible negative propositions. This ensures a valid conclusion. In this lesson, we dissect this condition using an example from lunar eclipse and see why some true premises fail to produce a conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises in the lunar eclipse example
The author gives two premises: (1) 'Nothing of the eclipsed moon is illuminated by lunar illumination necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not permanently.' (2) 'Every moon is eclipsed temporally, not permanently.' Write these in standard categorical form with modal qualifiers.
The first premise is a universal negative with a conditional necessity (as long as eclipsed) but not permanent. The second is a universal affirmative with temporal modality.
2
Determine the figure and mode
The minor is affirmative (Every moon is eclipsed temporally) and the major is negative (No eclipsed moon is illuminated...). This is a first-figure syllogism (Celarent-like) but with modal modifiers. Since minor is temporal and major is necessary-conditioned, it falls under the third mixed mode.
The third mixed mode is defined by the minor being negative (here affirmative? Wait, the author says 'since the minor in this mode is negative' and 'the minor in this mode is negative' – check the source: 'لما كانت الصغرى في هذا الضرب سالبة' – indeed the minor in the third mode is negative. But in the example, the minor is 'Every moon is eclipsed...' which is affirmative. There might be a confusion: the example is for illustration but not necessarily matching the mode? Actually, the example is used to show that even if the premises are true, no conclusion follows because the condition is violated. The minor in the example is affirmative, but the third mode typically has a negative minor. The author says 'the minor in this mode is negative' so the example might be showing a case that does not fit the mode. Better to clarify in guided path that the example is not a perfect instance of the third mode but used to illustrate the condition. However, for simplicity, we can note that the example premises are given as a counterexample to show the condition's necessity. The step should guide to recognize that the minor is temporary and not necessary/permanent, and the major is not among the six convertible negatives, so the condition fails.
3
Apply the third condition
The condition requires that the minor be necessary/permanent OR the major be among the six convertible negative propositions. Here, the minor is temporal (not necessary/permanent) and the major is a negative necessary-conditioned (not among the six convertible negatives). Therefore, the condition is violated, and no conclusion follows from these premises.
The six convertible negatives are specific modal propositions that preserve truth when converted. The major in the example is not one of them.
4
Understand why the condition is needed
Without this condition, true premises could lead to false conclusions or no conclusion at all. The author notes that no counterexample was found to show the impossibility of affirmation, but the condition prevents invalid combinations.
The condition restricts the modal types to ensure that a valid syllogism can be formed.

Mixed [modal syllogisms]. The third condition: that permanence (al-dawām) holds in the third mode for its minor premise such that it is necessary or permanent, or the general customary (al-‘urfī al-‘āmm) holds for its major premise such that it is among the six convertible negative propositions. For if both [conditions] were absent, the minor would be one of the non-necessary and non-permanent propositions, which are eleven, and the major one of the seven. But since the minor in this mode is negative – and it has been shown that the negative used in this figure must be convertible – the mixing of a minor from the seven with the majors of the seven falls away from that total. So nothing remains except the mixing of a minor from one of the four descriptive (waṣfiyyāt) with one of the seven, and the most specific of the minors are the conditioned particular (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) and the majors are the temporal (al-waqtiyya), and they do not produce [a conclusion] with it, so the rest do not produce. That is because it is true: 'Nothing of the eclipsed [moon] is illuminated by lunar illumination necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not permanently; and every moon is eclipsed temporally, not permanently', together with the impossibility of negating the moon from that which is illuminated by lunar illumination. And know that the demonstration for the second and third conditions is only complete if impossibility of affirmation is shown in them so that the difference follows, but no counterexample was found to indicate it.

Commentary
This passage is from the section on mixed modal syllogisms (al-mukhtaliṭāt) and explains the third condition for the third mode (al-ḍarb al-thālith). The author begins by stating the condition: the minor premise must be either necessary (ḍarūriyya) or permanent (dā’ima), or the major premise must be from the six convertible negative propositions (al-qaḍāyā al-sitt al-mun‘akisa al-sawālib). If neither condition holds, then the minor would be one of eleven non-necessary/non-permanent propositions and the major one of seven. However, because the minor in this mode is negative and must be convertible, the combination of a minor from the seven with a major from the seven is eliminated, leaving only the combination of a minor from the four descriptive (waṣfiyyāt) with a major from the seven. The most specific minors are the conditioned particular (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) and the majors are the temporal (waqtiyya), which do not yield a conclusion. The author then provides an example about lunar eclipse: 'Nothing of the eclipsed moon is illuminated by lunar illumination necessarily as long as it is eclipsed, not permanently; and every moon is eclipsed temporally, not permanently' – despite the impossibility of negating 'moon' from 'illuminated by lunar illumination'. This shows why the condition is necessary: to avoid invalid combinations. The note at the end remarks that the demonstration for the second and third conditions would require showing the impossibility of affirmation, but no counterexample was found.

Condition Four: Major Premise in the Sixth Mixed Mode
Author states condition that major in sixth mode must be from six convertible negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for the major premise in the sixth mixed mode and explain why conversion of the minor requires the major to be from the six convertible negatives.

Core Idea
The sixth mode's validity depends on converting the minor, forcing the major to be a convertible negative.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth condition for the sixth mixed mode. The author teaches that the major premise must be from the set of six convertible negatives because the mode is proven by converting the minor to reduce to the second figure. Understanding this condition is essential for constructing valid mixed modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reduction strategy
Recognize that the sixth mode is proven by converting the minor premise to obtain a second figure syllogism. This conversion is the key to establishing the conclusion.
2
List the required conditions for conversion
The minor premise must be a particular negative (saliba khassa) because only particular negatives can be converted while preserving truth. This is a prerequisite for the reduction.
Recall that conversion of a negative requires universal or particular negative; here particular is chosen.
3
Determine the major's requirement
After conversion, the syllogism becomes a second figure argument. For the second figure, the major must satisfy specific modal conditions. If permanence (dawam) does not hold for the minor, the major must be from the six convertible negatives.
The six convertible negatives are a set of negative propositions that can be converted without loss of modality.
4
Verify the condition's necessity
If the major were not from the six convertible negatives, the conversion would not yield a valid second figure syllogism, and the conclusion would not follow. This condition ensures the logical soundness of the mode.

Fifth Condition for the Eighth Mixed Mode: Reducing to the First Figure
Condition that minor from two particulars, major from general customary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fifth condition for the eighth mixed mode by identifying the required premise types and verifying the reduction to the first figure.

Core Idea
The eighth mixed mode's conclusion is derived by reversing premises and converting the result.

The eighth mixed mode in modal syllogism has specific conditions to ensure its validity. One key condition is that the minor must be from the two particular propositions and the major from the general customary class. This passage explains why, using reduction to the first figure and conversion of the conclusion. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly applying modal syllogisms in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition statement
Find the sentence: 'الشرط الخامس كون صغرى الضرب الثامن من إحدى الخاصتين وكبراء مما يصدق عليه العرفي العام'. This explicitly states the condition: minor from the two particulars, major from the general customary.
2
Understand the reduction mechanism
The author shows that the eighth mode does not yield the conclusion directly; you must swap the premises to return to the first figure, derive a particular negative, and then convert that to the desired conclusion.
The reversal of order is key: the minor becomes the major of the first figure and vice versa.
3
Check first figure requirements
The first figure produces a particular negative only when its major is one of the two particulars and its minor from the six general customary propositions. After swapping, the condition ensures the swapped premises meet these requirements.
The two particulars are likely particular affirmative and particular negative; the six general customary propositions are specific modal types.
4
Consider special cases
The author notes that if the minor is from the four descriptive (waṣfiyyāt) or the two permanent (dā’imatayn) propositions, the condition still works, but the intermediate conclusion differs (e.g., necessary non-permanent) yet can still be converted.
These cases illustrate the robustness of the condition across modal variations.
5
Confirm necessity
Without this condition, the reduction would not produce a convertible conclusion, rendering the eighth mode invalid. Thus the condition is necessary.
This step solidifies the logical necessity of the condition.

الشرط الخامس كون صغرى الضرب الثامن من إحدى الخاصتين وكبراء مما يصدق عليه العرفي العام، لأنّ إنتاجه إنما يظهر بعكس الترتيب ليرجع إلى الأوّل ثم عكس النتيجة فلابد أن تكون مقدمتاه بحيث إذا بدلت إحداهما بالأخرى أنتجتا سالبة خاصة لتقبل الانعكاس إلى النتيجة المطلوبة، والشكل الأول إنما ينتج سالبة خاصة لـو كـان كـبـراه إحدى الخاصتين وصغراه إحدى القضايا الست التي يصدق عليها العرفي العام.

The fifth condition: the minor of the eighth mode must be from one of the two particulars (al-khāṣṣatayn) and its major from that to which the general customary (al-‘urfī al-‘āmm) applies, because its production is only shown by reversing the order to return to the first [figure], then converting the conclusion. Hence its two premises must be such that if one is exchanged for the other, they produce a particular negative that can be converted to the desired conclusion. The first figure only produces a particular negative if its major is one of the two particulars and its minor is one of the six propositions to which the general customary applies. As for if its minor is one of the four descriptive (al-waṣfiyyāt), it is obvious [that it works]. As for if it is one of the two permanent (al-dā’imatayn), then the conclusion is necessarily non-permanent or permanent non-permanent, and they are more specific than the particular customary (al-‘urfiyya al-khāṣṣa), so the particular negative customary proposition applies to the conclusion, and it converts to the desired conclusion. Hence the minor of this mode must be one of the two particulars because it is the major of the first figure, and the major must be from the six propositions because it is the minor of the first figure.

Condition for the Seventh Mixed Mode
Deriving conditions for seventh mode via conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reduction strategy for the seventh mixed mode in modal syllogisms and identify the necessary conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
The seventh mode is validated by converting the major premise to reduce to the third figure.

In this passage, the author explains how the seventh mixed mode is proven. Instead of proving it directly, logicians convert the major premise (swap subject and predicate) to reduce the syllogism to the third figure, which has known conditions. By understanding this reduction, you will see how modal syllogisms are systematically validated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seventh mixed mode
It is a mixed modal syllogism in the first figure with one necessary and one possible premise. The exact mood is not specified here, but the reduction technique is the focus.
The mode's number varies across commentaries; this refers to a specific mood in the classification.
2
Understand the reduction strategy
The author states that the mode is proven by converting the major premise (عكس الكبرى) to transform the syllogism into the third figure.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate, preserving truth for certain proposition types.
3
Determine the conditions
Two conditions are required: the negative premise must be convertible (i.e., a universal negative or particular negative that can undergo conversion without losing truth), and the affirmative premise together with its converted form must satisfy the conditions for production in the third figure.
The third figure requires the middle term to be the subject of both premises.
4
Note the incomplete text
The passage cuts off at 'فلابد فيه', so the full condition is not given. However, the logical method is clear: each mode has a unique reduction to a known figure.
Incomplete passages remind us that logic texts often require supplementation from commentaries.

Conditions for the 7th and 8th Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author explains the two conditions for the seventh and eighth moods of the fourth figure in mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions required for the seventh and eighth moods of the fourth figure in mixed modal syllogisms and explain why each condition is necessary.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's odd moods need special premise conditions because they reduce to other figures via conversion.

In this passage, the author expands on the conditions that make the seventh and eighth moods of the fourth figure productive. These moods produce conclusions only when their premises meet specific modal requirements, because they rely on conversion to reduce to other figures. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating the validity of complex syllogisms in Islamic modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two moods under discussion
The seventh and eighth moods of the fourth figure. Note that these moods involve mixed modalities (e.g., necessary, actual, customary). The passage specifies conditions for each.
The exact propositional forms are not fully stated, but the conditions imply they are particular negative and affirmative with specific modal qualifiers.
2
Recognize the reduction method for each mood
The eighth mood reduces by reversing the order of premises (aks al-tartib) to the first figure, then converting the conclusion. The seventh mood reduces by converting the major premise to the third figure.
These reduction methods dictate what conditions are needed: for conversion, the premises must be convertible.
3
Extract the conditions for the eighth mood
Two main conditions: (1) The minor must be a particular negative (saliba khassa) to accept conversion. (2) The major must be affirmative and meet the conditions of the second figure regarding modality; if perpetuity does not hold for the minor, the major must be among the six convertible negatives. Additionally, the minor must be one of the two particular propositions and the major must be a general customary proposition.
The phrase 'two particular propositions' likely refers to particular affirmative and particular negative with specific modal characters.
4
Extract the conditions for the seventh mood
Two conditions: (1) The negative premise must be a particular proposition (convertible). (2) The affirmative premise must be actual (fi'liyya), not possible, because a possible minor is barren in the third figure.
The author notes these conditions are implied by earlier rules.
5
Test with an example
Construct a concrete example: For the eighth mood, take minor 'Some A is not B' (particular negative, general customary) and major 'Every B is C' (affirmative, general customary). Verify the conditions, then reduce to the first figure by swapping premises to get 'Every B is C' and 'Some A is not B', which in the first figure yields 'Some A is not C', then convert to the desired conclusion.
This step reinforces how the conditions ensure the reduction works.

أحدهما أن تكون الصغرى سالبة خاصة لتقبل الانعكاس كما عرفت فيما سبق. وثانيهما أن تكون الكبرى الموجبة معها على الشرائط المعتبرة بحسب الجهة في الشكل الثاني لتحصل النتيجة، وشرطه أنه إذا لم يصدق الدوام على صغراه تكون كبراه من الست المنعكسة السوالب، فيجب أن تكون كبرى الضرب السادس كذلك. الشرط الخامس كون صغرى الضرب الثامن من إحدى الخاصتين وكبراء مما يصدق عليه العرفي العام، لأنّ إنتاجه إنما يظهر بعكس الترتيب ليرجع إلى الأوّل ثم عكس النتيجة فلابد أن تكون مقدمتاه بحيث إذا بدلت إحداهما بالأخرى أنتجتا سالبة خاصة لتقبل الانعكاس إلى النتيجة المطلوبة، والشكل الأول إنما ينتج سالبة خاصة لـو كـان كـبـراه إحدى الخاصتين وصغراه إحدى القضايا الست التي يصدق عليها العرفي العام. أما إذا كانت صغراه إحدى الوصفيات الأربع فظاهر. وأما إذا كانت إحدى الدائمتين فلأن النتيجة حينئذ ضرورية لادائمة أو دائمة لادائمة، وهما أخص من العرفية الخاصة فيصدق على النتيجة السالبة الجزئية العرفية الخاصة، وهي تنعكس إلى النتيجة المطلوبة فيجب أن تكون صغرى هذا الضرب إحدى الخاصتين لأنها كبرى الشكل الأول، وكبراء من القضايا الست لأنها صغرى الشكل الأول، ومن هاهنا يظهر أن الضرب السابع لما كان إنتاجه إنما يتبين بعكس الكبرى ليرجع إلى الشكل الثالث وجب أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيه قابلة للانعكاس، وأن تكون الموجبة مع عكسها على شرط إنتاج الشكل الثالث؛ فلابد فيه أيضاً من شرطين : أحدهما أن تكون السالبة إحدى الخاصتين. وثانيهما أن تكون الموجبة فعليّة، لأنّ الصغرى الممكنة عقيمة في الشكل الثالث. وإنما لم يذكر ذلك في الكتاب لأنّ الشرط الأول قد علم في القياس، والشرط الثاني قد علم من أوّل الشروط، وهو عدم استعمال الممكنة في هذا الشكل.
One [condition] is that the minor premise be a particular negative to accept conversion, as you have learned previously. The second is that the affirmative major premise with it be under the considered conditions according to modality in the second figure for the conclusion to be obtained, and its condition is that if perpetuity is not true of its minor, then its major be among the six convertible negative propositions, so it is necessary that the major of the sixth mood be likewise. The fifth condition: that the minor of the eighth mood be one of the two particular [propositions] and its major be among that to which the general customary applies, because its production only appears by reversing the order to return to the first [figure], then conversion of the conclusion, so it is necessary that its two premises be such that if one of them is substituted for the other, they produce a particular negative to accept conversion to the required conclusion, and the first figure only produces a particular negative if its major is one of the two particular [propositions] and its minor is one of the six propositions to which the general customary applies. As for if its minor is one of the four descriptives, it is apparent. And if it is one of the two perpetuals, then because the conclusion then is necessary-for-perpetual or perpetual-for-necessary, and they are more specific than the particular customary, so the particular negative particular customary is true of the conclusion, and it converts to the required conclusion, so it is necessary that the minor of this mood be one of the two particular [propositions] because it is the major of the first figure, and its major be among the six propositions because it is the minor of the first figure. From this it appears that the seventh mood—since its production is only clarified by conversion of the major to return to the third figure—it is necessary that the negative used in it be convertible, and that the affirmative with its conversion be on the condition of the third figure's production; so there are also two conditions: one is that the negative be one of the two particular [propositions], and the second is that the affirmative be actual, because the possible minor is barren in the third figure. And the author did not mention this in the book because the first condition is known from the syllogism, and the second condition is known from the beginning of the conditions, which is the non-use of the possible in this figure.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the conditions for the seventh and eighth moods of the fourth figure in mixed modal syllogisms. The author refers back to earlier conditions. First, for the eighth mood, the condition is that the first premise (minor) must be a particular negative so that it can be converted, and the second premise (major) must be affirmative and meet the conditions of the second figure regarding modality. If perpetuity does not hold for the minor, then the major must be among the six convertible negative propositions—this also applies to the sixth mood. Then the fifth condition specifically for the eighth mood: the minor must be one of the two particular propositions (probably the particular affirmative or particular negative with specific modality), and the major must be a general customary proposition. This is because the mood's conclusion is derived by first converting the order of premises to get back to the first figure, then converting the resulting conclusion. Therefore, the premises must be such that swapping them yields a particular negative that can be converted to the desired conclusion. The first figure only produces a particular negative if its major is a particular proposition and its minor is one of the six propositions that are general customary. If the minor is a descriptive proposition, it's clear. If it is a perpetual proposition, then the conclusion is a necessary-for-perpetual or perpetual-for-necessary, which are more specific than the particular customary, so the particular negative particular customary is true of the conclusion and converts correctly. Thus the minor must be a particular proposition, and the major must be from the six. The author then shows that the seventh mood requires two conditions: the negative must be a particular proposition to be convertible, and the affirmative must be actual (not possible) because a possible minor is barren in the third figure, to which this mood reduces by converting the major. The author notes that these conditions are not explicitly mentioned in the original text because the first condition is known from the general theory of syllogism and the second from the initial rule that possible premises are not used in this figure.

Modal conclusions for the eight moods of the fourth figure
Author lists modal conclusions for the eight moods of the fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal conclusion for each mood of the fourth figure given the premises' modalities.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's conclusions depend on conversion and perpetuity conditions.

In this passage, the author specifies how to derive the modal conclusion for each of the eight moods of the fourth figure. Understanding these rules is crucial for constructing valid syllogisms in modal logic, which is used in Islamic theology to reason about necessary, perpetual, and contingent propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood number and premise order
The fourth figure has eight moods. Each mood has a specific arrangement of premises. Determine which mood you are dealing with (first, second, etc.).
The passage refers to moods 1-8 with distinct rules.
2
Check perpetuity conditions
For each mood, the rule for the conclusion depends on whether the minor or major premise is perpetual (دائمة). Perpetuity means the proposition is true at all times.
If perpetuity is true of a premise, the conclusion can be stronger.
3
Apply the rule for the mood
For first two moods: if perpetuity holds on the minor or the syllogism uses convertible negatives, conclusion by conversion of the minor; else absolute general. For third: if either premise perpetual, conclusion perpetual; else by conversion of minor. For fourth and fifth: if major perpetual, conclusion perpetual; else conversion of minor with removal of non-perpetuity. For sixth: as second figure after minor conversion. For seventh: as third figure after major conversion. For eighth: as conversion of conclusion after reversal of order.
Each mood's rule is distinct and must be applied stepwise.
4
Determine the modal conclusion
Based on the rule, the conclusion will be either perpetual (دائمة), absolute general (مطلقة عامة), or obtained via conversion with possible modal adjustments.
Ensure you follow the exact conditions for each mood.

قال: والنتيجة في الضربين الأولين بعكس الصغرى إن صدق الدوام عليها أوكان القياس من الست المنعكسة السوالب وإلا فمطلقة عامة، وفي الضرب الثالث دائمة إن صدق الدوام على إحدى مقدمتية وإلا فبعكس الصغرى، وفي الضرب الرابع والخامس دائمة إن صدق الدوام على الكبرى والأفعكس الصغرى محذوفاً عنها اللادوام وفي السادس كمافي الشكل الثاني بعد عكس الصغرى، وفي السابع كما في الشكل الثالث بعد عكس الكبرى، وفي الثامن كعكس النتيجة بعد عكس الترتيب. القول: المنتج من الاختلاطات بحسب الشرائط المذكورة في كل واحد من الضربين الأولين مائة وأحد وعشرون، وهي الحاصلة من ضرب الموجهات من الفعلية الإحدى عشرة في نفسها. وفي الضرب الثالث سنة وأربعون وهي الحاصلة من الصغريين الدائمتين مع الفعليات الإحدى عشرة، ومن الصغريات المشروطتين والعرفيتين مع الست المنعكسة السوالب.
He said: The conclusion in the first two moods [is obtained] by conversion of the minor if perpetuity is true of it or the syllogism is from the six convertible negative [propositions]; otherwise, [it is] absolute general. In the third mood, [it is] perpetual if perpetuity is true of one of its two premises; otherwise, [it is] by conversion of the minor. In the fourth and fifth moods, [it is] perpetual if perpetuity is true of the major, and [otherwise] by conversion of the minor with the removal of non-perpetuity. In the sixth, as in the second figure after conversion of the minor. In the seventh, as in the third figure after conversion of the major. In the eighth, as the conversion of the conclusion after the reversal of order. The statement: The product of the mixed [syllogisms] according to the mentioned conditions in each of the first two moods is one hundred twenty-one, which result from multiplying the eleven actual modal propositions by themselves. In the third mood, [it is] forty-six, which result from the two perpetual minors with the eleven actual [majors?] and from the conditional and customary minors with the six convertible negative [majors?].
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the modal conclusions for the eight moods of the fourth figure, followed by counts of valid mixed modal syllogisms for the first three moods. The passage begins by quoting the original text ('He said'), then the commentator adds clarification. For the first two moods, the conclusion is obtained by converting the minor premise if perpetuity applies to it, or if the syllogism uses one of the six convertible negative propositions; otherwise the conclusion is absolute general. For the third mood, the conclusion is perpetual if either premise is perpetual; otherwise conversion of the minor yields the conclusion. For the fourth and fifth moods, the conclusion is perpetual if the major is perpetual; otherwise conversion of the minor works but with the removal of non-perpetuity (i.e., the conclusion is not perpetual). For the sixth mood, the process is like the second figure after converting the minor. For the seventh, like the third figure after converting the major. For the eighth, like converting the conclusion after reversing the order of premises. Then the commentator gives the number of valid mixed syllogisms: for the first two moods, 121 each, obtained by multiplying the 11 actual modal propositions (the possible moods of a single proposition?) by themselves. For the third mood, 46 valid combinations, coming from two perpetual minors with the 11 actual majors (2×11=22), plus conditional and customary minors with the six convertible negative majors (2×6=12? but it says 46, so perhaps more combinations). The exact calculation is not fully detailed here, but it shows the systematic enumeration of modal syllogisms.

Counting valid mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure
Author gives counts of valid mixed syllogisms for first three moods of fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of valid mixed syllogisms for the first three moods of the fourth figure using the given method.

Core Idea
The number of valid mixed syllogisms is derived by multiplying modal combinations.

After listing the modal conclusions, the author gives exact counts of valid mixed syllogisms for the first three moods. This enumeration reflects a systematic approach to modal logic, showing how many combinations of premise modalities yield valid conclusions. Understanding this helps us appreciate the completeness of modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the set of modal premise types
The author refers to 'the eleven actual modal propositions' (الفعليات الإحدى عشرة). This is the set of possible modalities for a single categorical statement (e.g., necessary, perpetual, possible, etc.).
These eleven include various combinations of modality and quantity.
2
Understand the multiplication for the first two moods
For each of the first two moods, the number of valid mixed syllogisms is 121. This is obtained by multiplying the 11 actual modal propositions for the minor by the 11 for the major (11×11=121). Thus, any combination of modal premises from the 11 types yields a valid syllogism in these moods.
This implies that in these moods, all modal premise pairs are valid.
3
Analyze the third mood count (46)
For the third mood, the count is 46. The author breaks it down: from the two perpetual minors with the 11 actual majors (2×11=22), plus from the conditional (مشروطة) and customary (عرفية) minors with the six convertible negative majors (2×6=12). That sums to 34, but the text says 46, so there must be additional combinations, perhaps including other minors or majors. The exact breakdown is not fully detailed, but the principle is that only certain modal combinations are valid.
The count 46 indicates a more restrictive set of valid combinations for the third mood.
4
Grasp the systematic enumeration
The counts show a systematic approach to modal syllogistic: each mood has a specific number of valid premise-modal combinations. This enumeration helps ensure completeness and avoids overcounting or undercounting.
Such counting is common in later Islamic logical works to map out the entire modal syllogistic.

Modal Conclusions in the First Five Moods of the Fourth Figure
Sets out the modal rules for conclusions of the first five moods of fourth figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion for each of the first five moods of the fourth figure based on the modalities of the premises and the six convertible negatives.

Core Idea
In the fourth figure, the conclusion's modality is perpetual or the converse of the minor, depending on the premises.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms yields conclusions with specific modalities when the premises are modalized. This passage from the commentary on the Shamsiyya details the rules for the first five moods. Understanding these rules is essential for evaluating the certainty of arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where modal distinctions between necessary, perpetual, and absolute propositions are crucial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood of the syllogism
Determine which of the first five moods you are dealing with. The first two moods have the same rule; the third, fourth, and fifth have distinct rules.
The moods are labeled by their premise types (e.g., first two are those with minor affirmative).
2
Check if the minor premise is perpetual or necessary
If the minor premise is perpetual (دائمة) or necessary, then the conclusion is the converse of the minor premise. Also, if the syllogism uses any of the six convertible negatives (الست المنعكسة السوالب), the conclusion is also the converse of the minor.
The six convertible negatives are modal propositions that convert to a true universal negative.
3
If not, assess other conditions for the first two moods
If the minor is not perpetual and the syllogism is not from the six convertible negatives, then the conclusion is absolute universal (مطلقة عامة).
This only applies to moods 1 and 2.
4
For mood 3, check both premises
If either premise is perpetual or necessary, the conclusion is perpetual. Otherwise, it is the converse of the minor premise.
The condition is on either premise, not just the minor.
5
For moods 4 and 5, check the major premise
If the major premise is perpetual or necessary, the conclusion is perpetual. Otherwise, it is the converse of the minor premise with non-perpetuity removed (i.e., the temporal restriction stripped).
Non-perpetuity removed means the conclusion becomes absolute or factual, not perpetual.
