الجهة في نتائج الضروب الناتجة من الشكل الرابع قال: والنتيجة في الضربين الأولين بعكس الصغرى إن صدق الدوام عليها أوكان القياس من الست المنعكسة السوالب وإلا فمطلقة عامة، وفي الضرب الثالث دائمة إن صدق الدوام على إحدى مقدمتية وإلا فبعكس الصغرى، وفي الضرب الرابع والخامس دائمة إن صدق الدوام على الكبرى والأفعكس الصغرى محذوفاً عنها اللادوام وفي السادس كمافي الشكل الثاني بعد عكس الصغرى، وفي السابع كما في الشكل الثالث بعد عكس الكبرى، وفي الثامن كعكس النتيجة بعد عكس الترتيب. القول: المنتج من الاختلاطات بحسب الشرائط المذكورة في كل واحد من الضربين الأولين مائة وأحد وعشرون، وهي الحاصلة من ضرب الموجهات من الفعلية الإحدى عشرة في نفسها. وفي الضرب الثالث سنة وأربعون وهي الحاصلة من الصغريين الدائمتين مع الفعليات الإحدى عشرة، ومن الصغريات المشروطتين والعرفيتين مع الست المنعكسة السوالب. وفي الرابع والخامس ستة وستون، وهي التي تحصل من الصغريات الإحدى عشرة مع المنعكسة السوالب. وفي السادس والثامن اثناعشر تحصل من الصغريين الخاصتين الست المنعكسة السوالب. وفي السابع اثنان وعشرون تحصل من الكبريين الخاصتين مع الفعليات الإحدى عشرة. والنتيجة في الضربين الأولين عكس الصغرى إن كانت ضرورية أو دائمة أوكان القياس من الست المنعكسة السوالب والا فمطلقة عامة. وفي الضرب الثالث دائمة إن كانت إحدى المقدمتين ضرورية أو دائمة، وإلا فعكس الصغرى. وفي الرابع والخامس دائمة إن كانت الكبرى ضرورية أو دائمة وإلاّ فعكس الصغرى محذوفاً عنه اللادوام. وبيان الكل بالبراهين المذكورة في المطلقات. وفي السادس كما في الشكل الثاني بعد عكس الصغرى. وفي السابع كما في الشكل الثالث بعد عكس الكبرى. وفي الثامن كما في الشكل الأول بعكس النتيجة بعد عكس الترتيب. وبالجملة لما كانت هذه الضروب الثلاثة الأخيرة ترتد إلى الأشكال الثلاثة المذكورة لما ذكرنا من الطرق كانت نتائجها نتائج تلك الأشكال بعينها في السادس والسابع وبعكسها في الثامن، وعليك بمطالعة هذا الجدول.
The modality in the conclusions of the moods resulting from the fourth figure. He said: The conclusion in the first two moods is by conversion of the minor premise if perpetuity is true of it or if the syllogism is from the six convertible negatives, otherwise it is absolute universal. In the third mood, [it is] perpetual if perpetuity is true of one of its two premises, otherwise by conversion of the minor premise. In the fourth and fifth moods, [it is] perpetual if perpetuity is true of the major premise, or by conversion of the minor premise with non-perpetuity removed from it. In the sixth, as in the second figure after conversion of the minor premise. In the seventh, as in the third figure after conversion of the major premise. In the eighth, as the conversion of the conclusion after reversal of the order. Statement: The productive [syllogisms] from the mixed [modalities] according to the mentioned conditions in each of the first two moods are one hundred and twenty-one, which result from multiplying the eleven factual modal propositions by themselves. In the third mood, forty-six, which result from the two perpetual minor premises with the eleven factuals, and from the conditional and customary minor premises with the six convertible negatives. In the fourth and fifth, sixty-six, which result from the eleven minor premises with the convertible negatives. In the sixth and eighth, twelve, result from the two special minor premises [with] the six convertible negatives. In the seventh, twenty-two, result from the two special major premises with the eleven factuals. The conclusion in the first two moods is conversion of the minor premise if it is necessary or perpetual or if the syllogism is from the six convertible negatives, otherwise it is absolute universal. In the third mood, [it is] perpetual if one of the two premises is necessary or perpetual, otherwise conversion of the minor premise. In the fourth and fifth, [it is] perpetual if the major is necessary or perpetual, otherwise conversion of the minor premise with non-perpetuity removed from it. The explanation of all is by the proofs mentioned in the absolutes. In the sixth, as in the second figure after conversion of the minor premise. In the seventh, as in the third figure after conversion of the major premise. In the eighth, as in the first figure after conversion of the conclusion after reversal of the order. In sum, since these last three moods revert to the three mentioned figures by the methods we mentioned, their conclusions are the very conclusions of those figures in the sixth and seventh, and their conversion in the eighth. And upon you is to study this table.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the Shamsiyya explains the modal results for each productive mixed syllogism in the fourth figure. The author begins by stating the modality of the conclusion for the first two moods: it is the converse of the minor premise if that premise is perpetual or if the syllogism is from the six convertible negatives (i.e., the six modal propositions that can be converted to true universal negatives). Otherwise, the conclusion is absolute universal (i.e., unrestricted in time). For the third mood, the conclusion is perpetual if one premise is perpetual, otherwise it is the converse of the minor premise. For the fourth and fifth moods, it is perpetual if the major premise is perpetual, otherwise it is the converse of the minor premise with non-perpetuity removed (i.e., the temporal restriction is stripped). The sixth, seventh, and eighth moods reduce to earlier figures after appropriate conversions. The passage then counts the number of productive mixed syllogisms under these conditions: 121 for the first two moods (11 factual propositions squared), 46 for the third (combinations of perpetual minors with factuals and conditional/customary minors with convertible negatives), 66 for the fourth and fifth (11 minors with convertible negatives), 12 for the sixth and eighth (two special minors with convertible negatives), and 22 for the seventh (two special majors with factuals). The author reiterates the modal conclusions with a slightly different formulation: in the first two moods, if the minor is necessary or perpetual, or if the syllogism uses convertible negatives, the conclusion is the converse of the minor; otherwise absolute universal. In the third, if one premise is necessary or perpetual, conclusion is perpetual; otherwise converse of minor. In the fourth and fifth, if major is necessary or perpetual, perpetual; otherwise converse of minor with non-perpetuity removed. All these are proven by the proofs given for absolute propositions. The last three moods (sixth, seventh, eighth) reduce to the first three figures: the sixth reduces to the second after converting the minor, the seventh to the third after converting the major, and the eighth to the first after converting the conclusion and reversing the order. Thus their conclusions are the same as those figures (or their converse in the eighth). The passage ends by advising the student to study the accompanying table.

Task 1: Given the mood is the first mood of the fourth figure, and the premises are: minor premise 'Perpetually, every intoxicant is forbidden' (perpetual), major premise 'Absolutely, every nabidh is an intoxicant' (absolute universal). Determine the modality of the conclusion according to the passage. Answer: Since the minor premise is perpetual, the conclusion is the converse of the minor, i.e., 'Perpetually, some forbidden things are intoxicants' (or 'Perpetually, some forbidden things are intoxicants').

What is the modality of the conclusion? Answer: Since the major is perpetual, the conclusion is perpetual, i.e., 'Perpetually, some temporally originated things are bodies'.

Determining Conclusions in Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author enumerates conclusion types for mixed modal syllogisms across figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type (necessary, perpetual, absolute general, or converse of the minor) for any mixed modal categorical syllogism based on the modalities of its premises and its figure, using the rules given in the text.

Core Idea
The conclusion’s modality in mixed modal syllogisms is determined by the premises’ modalities and the figure, with specific rules for each mood group.

This passage from a commentary on modal logic presents the rules for the conclusions of mixed modal syllogisms across eight figures. The author specifies how the modality of the conclusion—whether necessary, perpetual, absolute general, or the converse of the minor—depends on the premises. Understanding these rules is essential for constructing valid modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where premises often carry modal force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood groups
The author divides the moods into groups: first two, third, fourth/fifth, and sixth/seventh/eighth. Locate these in the passage.
The group numbers refer to moods within a figure, not figures themselves.
2
Extract the rule for the first two moods
Find the sentence: 'The result in the first two moods is the converse of the minor if it is necessary or perpetual, or if the syllogism is from the six converted negatives; otherwise it is absolute general.' Apply this to an example.
3
Extract the rule for the third mood
Find: 'In the third mood, it is perpetual if one of the two premises is necessary or perpetual; otherwise it is the converse of the minor.'
4
Extract the rule for the fourth and fifth moods
Find: 'In the fourth and fifth, it is perpetual if the major is necessary or perpetual; otherwise it is the converse of the minor with perpetuity removed.'
5
Understand the reduction of the last three moods
The author states that the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods reduce to the second, third, and first figures respectively after conversion. The conclusion then follows the result of the reduced figure, sometimes with conversion.
This shows how complex modal syllogisms can be simplified.

In the fourth and fifth (figures) there are sixty-six, which are obtained from the eleven minor premises with the converted negatives. In the sixth and eighth there are twelve, obtained from the six specific minor premises that are converted negatives. In the seventh there are twenty-two, obtained from the two specific major premises with the eleven actuals. The result in the first two moods is the converse of the minor if it is necessary or perpetual, or if the syllogism is from the six converted negatives; otherwise it is absolute general. In the third mood, it is perpetual if one of the two premises is necessary or perpetual; otherwise it is the converse of the minor. In the fourth and fifth, it is perpetual if the major is necessary or perpetual; otherwise it is the converse of the minor with perpetuity removed. The explanation of all is by the proofs mentioned in the absolutes. In the sixth, it is like in the second figure after converting the minor. In the seventh, it is like in the third figure after converting the major. In the eighth, it is like in the first figure after converting the conclusion after reversing the order. Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of the results of mixed modal syllogisms across the different figures and moods. The author, commenting on a prior work, enumerates the number of valid moods and specifies how the conclusion's modality is determined. The text first lists the number of valid conclusions for the fourth and fifth figures (66), the sixth and eighth (12), and the seventh (22), deriving these from specific combinations of premises. Then, the author provides rules for the conclusion's modality in each mood group: for the first two moods, the conclusion is the converse of the minor if the minor is necessary or perpetual, or if the premises come from the six converted negatives; otherwise it is an absolute general. For the third mood, the conclusion is perpetual if one premise is necessary or perpetual; otherwise it is the converse of the minor. For the fourth and fifth, it is perpetual if the major is necessary or perpetual; otherwise it is the converse of the minor with 'non-perpetuity' removed. The author states that these rules are proven by the arguments already given for absolute syllogisms. Then, for the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods, the author relates them to the earlier figures after certain conversions: the sixth is like the second figure after converting the minor premise; the seventh is like the third figure after converting the major; the eighth is like the first figure after converting the conclusion and reversing the order. Finally, the author summarizes that these last three moods reduce to the three previously mentioned figures, so their conclusions are the same (in the sixth and seventh) or the converses (in the eighth). The passage ends by directing the student to study the accompanying table, which lists the results for the first two moods from two universal affirmatives and from two affirmatives with a particular major.

Using Tables for Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author presents tables for three modes of mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal status of the conclusion in any mixed modal syllogism by applying the appropriate table from the Shamsiyya commentary.

Core Idea
The tables encode the conclusion modality for every combination of premise modalities.

When premises differ in modality (e.g., necessary, absolute, conditional), the conclusion's modality is not arbitrary—it follows a pattern captured in tables. The Shamsiyya tradition provides three such tables for the main modes of mixed syllogisms. This lesson teaches you to read these tables and derive the correct conclusion modality, a key skill for evaluating modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mode of the syllogism
Look at the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises. The first mode has two affirmative universals; the second has two affirmatives with a particular major; the third has two universals with a negative minor.
2
Locate the relevant table
Use the table corresponding to the mode you identified. The tables are typically organized with minor premise modalities as rows and major premise modalities as columns.
3
Find the intersection of premise modalities
Read the row for your minor premise's modality (e.g., ضرورية 'necessary') and the column for your major's modality. The cell gives the conclusion's modality.
For example, in the third mode, if the major is necessary and the minor is conventional (عرفية), the conclusion is often absolute (مطلقة).
4
Apply the result to the conclusion
Write the conclusion with the obtained modality. Ensure the categorical form (subject-predicate) is preserved; only the modal qualifier changes.

Commentary
This passage presents the summary tables for mixed modal syllogisms (المختلطات) in the context of a commentary on the Shamsiyya treatise on logic. The author first introduces the table for the first two modes (الضربين الأولين) of mixed syllogisms. The first mode consists of two affirmative universal premises, while the second mode has two affirmative premises but with a particular major premise. Then he presents the table for the third mode, which has two universal premises with a negative minor premise. These tables show the modality of the conclusion (e.g., necessary, permanent, conditional, conventional, general, specific, absolute, etc.) based on the modalities of the premises. For example, if both premises are necessary (ضرورية), the conclusion is necessary. The tables serve as a quick reference for determining the validity and modal status of conclusions in mixed modal syllogisms, which is a complex topic in Arabic logic. The phrase 'شرح الرسالة الشمسية' indicates that this is an explanation of the Shamsiyya, a well-known text on logic by Najm al-Din al-Katibi.

Reading the Third Figure Modal Syllogism Results Table
Table of valid moods for third figure mixed syllogisms with negative minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the third figure results table to determine whether a given modal syllogism with a negative minor premise yields a valid necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
The third figure table reveals which modal blends produce necessary conclusions and which are sterile.

This passage presents a comprehensive table for the third figure of mixed modal syllogisms where both premises are universal and the minor premise is negative. The table lists various modalities for the minor and major premises, and for each combination shows whether the conclusion is perpetual (da'ima), sterile ('aqima, i.e., no necessary conclusion), or another modality. Understanding this table allows you to quickly validate modal arguments used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise's modality and quantity
The minor premise is listed under 'صغريات' (minors). Modalities include ضرورية (necessary), دائمة (perpetual), مشروطة (conditional), عرفية (conventional). The quantity is either عامة (universal) or خاصة (particular). For example, 'ضرورية عامة' means a universal necessary minor.
The minor is always negative in this table, but the negation is not explicitly marked; it is given condition.
2
Identify the major premise's modality and quantity
The major premise is listed under 'كبريات' (majors). Similarly, modalities are ضرورية, دائمة, مشروطة, عرفية, and quantities عامة or خاصة. For instance, 'دائمة عامة' is a universal perpetual major.
3
Locate the intersection in the table
The table is structured with minors in rows and majors in columns. Find the cell where the row of your minor meets the column of your major. For example, minor 'ضرورية خاصة' and major 'عرفية عامة' gives a result.
The passage lists the results in a dense sequence; mentally align the rows and columns.
4
Read the result: valid or sterile
The result in the cell tells you the modality of the conclusion if the syllogism is valid. If it says 'عقيمة' (sterile), the mood yields no necessary conclusion. If it says 'دائمة' or another modality, the conclusion has that modality and the syllogism is valid.
For example, many combinations yield 'عقيمة', so not all modal blends produce a valid inference.

Reading the Fourth and Fifth Figure Mixed Syllogisms Table
Table of valid moods for fourth and fifth figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the table of valid modal conclusions for fourth and fifth figure mixed syllogisms and identify the resulting modal proposition given a specific combination of minor and major modal premises.

Core Idea
The fourth and fifth figures produce different modal conclusions depending on the modality of premises.

The table presented here is a reference for determining the valid conclusions when mixing modal premises in the fourth and fifth figures. Understanding this table is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where temporal and modal distinctions matter, such as in arguments about divine attributes or legal rulings with temporal conditions. In this lesson, we will walk through the structure of the table and practice extracting conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Determine if the syllogism belongs to the fourth or fifth figure based on the premise quantifiers and quality. Fourth figure: two universal premises with the minor affirmative. Fifth figure: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major.
2
Locate the minor premise modality
The table lists minor premises by modality (e.g., ضرورية, دائمة, etc.). Find the row for the given minor premise modality.
3
Locate the major premise modality
The table lists major premises by modality and specificity (عامة/خاصة). Find the column.
4
Read the intersection
The cell at the intersection gives the resulting conclusion modality (e.g., دائمة, حينية, مطلقة). That is the conclusion of the syllogism.
If the cell is empty, the syllogism is sterile (yields no conclusion).
5
Verify the mood
Check if the resulting modal proposition matches the allowed moods for that figure. The table only lists valid combinations.

Reading the Tables of Mixed Modal Syllogisms (Moods 4,6,7,8)
Tables for mixed syllogism moods 4,6,7,8

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to read the tables for mixed modal syllogisms and determine the conclusion modality for any combination of premise modalities in the given moods.

Core Idea
Tables of mixed syllogisms codify strict rules for combining premises of different modal strengths.

In this passage, Qutb al-Din al-Razi presents tables for four moods of mixed modal syllogisms (المختلطات). These tables are practical tools for quickly determining the modality of the conclusion when the premises have different modal qualifiers. Understanding these tables is essential for constructing valid arguments in modal logic, particularly in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
The table is labeled with the mood number (e.g., ضرب رابع). Note the quantity and quality of premises characteristic of that mood: for the fourth mood, both premises are universal and the minor is affirmative.
Mood numbers here refer to standard classifications in mixed modal syllogisms.
2
Locate the premise modalities
Find the row corresponding to the minor premise modality (e.g., ضرورية, دائمة, مشروطة, عرفية) and the column for the major premise modality (e.g., عامة, خاصة).
The modalities are listed in the table headers.
3
Read the conclusion modality
The cell at the intersection gives the modality of the conclusion. For example, if minor is ضرورية and major is عامة, the conclusion may be ضرورية or another modality depending on the rules.
This is the key operation: the table summarizes the modal inference.
4
Apply the rule to any combination
Once you understand the pattern for one mood, you can extrapolate to other moods with similar tables. The principle is that the conclusion modality is determined by the specific combination, often following the weaker modality but with exceptions.
The actual entries are verified by the author from the rules of mixed syllogisms.

Figures of Conditional Syllogisms and the Scope of the Term
Qutbi explains the composition of conditional syllogisms and their four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of a conditional syllogism based on the position of the common proposition and distinguish between pure conditional and mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms also have four figures—the common proposition acts as the middle term.

Qutbi now turns to conjunctive syllogisms made from conditionals. He explains that such syllogisms share the same four figures as categorical ones, using the position of the antecedent and consequent as the key. He also warns that 'conditional syllogism' is broader than pure conditionals—it includes mixtures with categoricals. Mastering this will help you handle complex arguments in Islamic theology where conditional and categorical premises combine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional premise
The passage focuses on 'muttasilat' (connected conditionals) like 'If A then B'. Note that the premises may be purely conditional or mixed with categoricals.
Later divisions include other types like disjunctives.
2
Locate the 'common part' (الشركة)
The common part is a complete proposition shared between the two premises—it can be the antecedent or consequent of each conditional.
Unlike categoricals where the middle term is a term, here it is a whole proposition.
3
Determine the figure using the four rules
Apply Qutbi's rules: If the common part is the consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major = first figure; consequent in both = second; antecedent in both = third; antecedent in minor and consequent in major = fourth.
4
Test the example given
The example: 'Whenever A is B, C is D' (minor) and 'Whenever C is D, E is Z' (major). The common part 'C is D' is consequent in minor and antecedent in major → first figure.
This mirrors the Barbara mood of categoricals.
5
Understand the scope of 'conditional syllogism'
Qutbi clarifies that a 'conditional syllogism' need not consist only of conditional premises—it can include categoricals. This expands the applicability of the rules.
For example, a premise could be 'Every A is B' (categorical) and the other 'If A is B then C is D' (conditional).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: الفصل الثالث في الاقترانيات الكائنة من الشرطيات وهي خمسة أقسام: القسم الأول ما يتركب من المتصلات والمطبوع منه ما كانت الشركة في جزء تام من المقدمتين. وتنعقد الأشكال الأربعة فيه لأنه إن كان تالياً في الصغرى مقدماً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الأوّل، وإن كان تالياً فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني، و إن كان مقدماً فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث وإن كان مقدّماً في الصغرى وتالياً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الرابع. وشرائط الإنتاج وعدد الضروب والنتيجة في الكمية والكيفية في كل شكل كما في الحمليات من غير فرق. مثال الضرب الأوّل من الشكل الأوّل: «كلما كان (اب) فرج د) وكلما كان (ج) (د) فـرهـ ز ينتج «كلما كان (اب) فـرهـ ز)». القول: ليس المراد بالقياس الشرطي هو المركب من الشرطيات المحضة، بل هو ما لا يتركب من الحمليات سواء تركب من الشرطيات المحضة أو من الشرطيات والحمليات.
He said: The third chapter on the conjunctive (syllogisms) composed of conditionals, and they are five divisions. The first division is what is composed of connected conditionals, and its natural (form) is when the common (term) is a complete part of the two premises. And the four figures occur in it: if the consequent is in the minor (premise) and the antecedent in the major, then it is the first figure; if the consequent is in both, then it is the second figure; if the antecedent is in both, then it is the third figure; if the antecedent is in the minor and the consequent in the major, then it is the fourth figure. And the conditions of productivity, the number of moods, and the result in quantity and quality in each figure are as in categoricals without difference. An example of the first mood of the first figure: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D, and whenever C is D, then E is Z, yields: Whenever A is B, then E is Z.' Statement: By 'conditional syllogism' is not meant solely what is composed of pure conditionals, but rather what is not composed of categoricals, whether it is composed of pure conditionals or of conditionals and categoricals.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage from Qutbi's work on logic (Shaykh Muhammad b. Husayn al-Halabi) is an exposition of the third chapter concerning conjunctive syllogisms formed from conditional propositions. The author begins by stating that such syllogisms are divided into five types, and here he treats the first type: those composed of connected conditionals (muttasilat). He notes that naturally the common term between the two premises is a complete part (i.e., a complete proposition) rather than a part of a proposition. He then explains how the four figures of categorical syllogisms also arise in conditional syllogisms based on the position of the antecedent and consequent in the premises. Specifically, if the consequent appears in the minor premise and the antecedent in the major, it is the first figure; if the consequent is in both, the second; if the antecedent is in both, the third; and if the antecedent is in the minor and the consequent in the major, the fourth figure. Importantly, the conditions for a valid conclusion (productivity), the number of valid moods (darb, pl. durub), and the resulting conclusion in terms of quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) are exactly the same as in categorical syllogisms. He provides a concrete example: 'Whenever A is B then C is D, and whenever C is D then E is Z' yields 'Whenever A is B then E is Z' – this is analogous to the first mood (Barbara) of the first figure in categorical logic. Finally, he clarifies a potential misconception: the term 'conditional syllogism' does not refer only to syllogisms composed entirely of conditional propositions; rather, it includes any syllogism that does not contain categorical propositions, whether it be purely conditional or a mix of conditional and categorical propositions. This distinction is important because some logicians might restrict the term to pure conditionals, but the author broadens it to include mixed forms.

Key Terms
الاقترانيات الكائنة من الشرطيات
المتصلات
الأشكال الأربعة

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms use the same four figures as categoricals, with the common proposition acting as the middle term, and the term 'conditional syllogism' covers mixed forms as well.

The Four Figures of Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Author classifies compound conditional syllogisms into five divisions and explains the first division's four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of compound conditional syllogisms by locating the middle term's position in the premises and verify the conclusion's type.

Core Idea
Compound conditional syllogisms mirror categorical figures when the shared part is a complete proposition.

In this passage, the author expands on compound syllogisms composed of conditional premises, focusing on the first division where the common part is a complete proposition. We will explore how the four figures of categorical syllogisms apply directly to these conditionals, with the middle term defined as the shared complete part. By mastering these figures, you will be able to analyze and construct conditional arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Find the complete proposition (either the entire antecedent or entire consequent) that appears in both conditional premises. This is the shared part, analogous to the middle term in categorical syllogisms.
In the examples, the middle term is marked by the variable pair (ج د) or similar.
2
Determine its position in each premise
Check whether the middle term appears as the consequent (tālin) or antecedent (muqaddam) in the minor premise (first conditional) and the major premise (second conditional).
Remember: the minor premise is the first stated, major the second.
3
Apply the figure classification
Use the position pattern to assign the figure: (1) consequent in minor, antecedent in major → first figure; (2) consequent in both → second; (3) antecedent in both → third; (4) antecedent in minor, consequent in major → fourth.
This mirrors the categorical figures exactly.
4
Read the conclusion and verify quantity/quality
Following categorical rules, first figure yields a universal affirmative conclusion; second figure yields universal negative; third and fourth yield particular affirmative. Check if the example's conclusion matches.
The author confirms: 'The conditions of production, number of moods, and the result in quantity and quality in each figure are as in categoricals without difference.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: الفصل الثالث في الاقترانيات الكائنة من الشرطيات وهي خمسة أقسام: القسم الأول ما يتركب من المتصلات والمطبوع منه ماكانت الشركة في جزء تام من المقدمتين. وتنعقد الأشكال الأربعة فيه لأنه إن كان تالياً في الصغرى مقدماً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الأوّل، وإن كان تالياً فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني، و إن كان مقدماً فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث وإن كان مقدّماً في الصغرى وتالياً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الرابع. وشرائط الإنتاج وعدد الضروب والنتيجة في الكمية والكيفية في كل شكل كما في الحمليات من غير فرق. مثال الضرب الأوّل من الشكل الأوّل: «كلما كان (اب) فرج د) وكلما كان (ج) (د) فـرهـ ز ينتج «كلما كان (اب) فـرهـ ز)». القول: ليس المراد بالقياس الشرطي هو المركب من الشرطيات المحضة، بل هو ما لا يتركب من الحمليات سواء تركب من الشرطيات المحضة أو من الشرطيات والحمليات. وأقسامه خمسة : لأنه أما أن يتركب من المتصلتين أو منفصلتين، أو حملية ومتصلة، أو حمليّة ومنفصلة، أو متصلة ومنفصلة : القسم الأول ما يتركب من المتصلتين، والشركة بينهما إما في جزء تام من كل واحدة منهما وهو المقدّم بكماله أو التالي بكماله، وإما في جزء غير تام منهما : أي جزء من المقدّم أو التالي ، وإما في جزء تام من إحداهما غير تام من الأخرى، فهذه ثلاثة أقسام، لكن القريب بالطبع منها الأول وهو ما يكون الشركة في جزء تام من المقدمتين، وتنعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة : لأن الأوسط - وهو المشترك بينهما - إن كان تالياً في الصغرى مقدماً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الأوّل كقولنا : «كلما كان (اب) فـ(ج د) وكلما كان (ج) د) فه ز) فكلما كان (اب) فرهـ ز)». وإن كان تالياً فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني، كقولنا : «كلما كان (اب) فرج (د) وليس البنّة إذا كان ه ز (فـ(ج) (د) فليس ألبتة إذا كان (ا ب فه ز)». وإن كان مقدّماً فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث، كقولنا : «كلماكان (ج د) ف (اب) وكلّما كان (ج (د) فـه ز) فقد يكون إذا كان (اب) فه ز). وإن كان مقدّماً في الصغرى وتالياً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الرابع كقولنا : كلما كان (ج) د) فرا (ب) وكلماكان (ه ز) فرج د) فقد يكون إذا كان (اب) فره ز). وشرائط إنتاج هذه الأشكال كما في الحمليّات من غير فرق
He said: The third chapter on compound syllogisms composed of conditionals. They are five divisions. The first division is what is composed of two conditionals, and its natural [form] is that the common part is a complete part in both premises. And the four figures occur in it because if [the middle] is the consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major, it is the first figure; if it is the consequent in both, it is the second figure; if it is antecedent in both, it is the third figure; and if it is antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major, it is the fourth figure. The conditions of production, number of moods, and the result in quantity and quality in each figure are as in categoricals without difference. Example of the first mood of the first figure: 'Whenever A is B then C is D, and whenever C is D then E is Z' yields 'Whenever A is B then E is Z'. Statement: What is meant by conditional syllogism is not that which is composed solely of conditionals, but rather that which is not composed of categoricals, whether it is composed solely of conditionals or of conditionals and categoricals. And its divisions are five: because it is either composed of two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. The first division is that composed of two conditionals, and the common part between them is either a complete part of each of them, i.e., the entire antecedent or the entire consequent, or an incomplete part of them: i.e., a part of the antecedent or consequent, or a complete part of one and incomplete of the other; so these are three divisions. But the naturally primary among them is the first, which is that the common part is a complete part in both premises, and the four figures occur in it: because the middle – which is the shared term – if it is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major, it is the first figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B then C is D, and whenever C is D then E is Z, so whenever A is B then E is Z'. And if it is consequent in both, it is the second figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B then C is D, and never if E is Z then C is D, so never if A is B then E is Z'. And if it is antecedent in both, it is the third figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever C is D then A is B, and whenever C is D then E is Z, so possibly if A is B then E is Z'. And if it is antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major, it is the fourth figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever C is D then A is B, and whenever E is Z then C is D, so possibly if A is B then E is Z'. And the conditions of production of these figures are as in categoricals without difference.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the Shamsiyya's categorization of compound syllogisms (al-iqtiranat) composed of conditional premises (shartiyyat). The commentator clarifies that 'conditional syllogism' does not mean a syllogism consisting solely of conditionals; rather, it includes any syllogism not composed solely of categoricals—purely conditional or mixed. He then enumerates five divisions: composed of two conditionals, two disjunctives, a categorical and a conditional, a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. The focus is the first division: two conditionals. Within these, the shared part (al-mushtarak) can be a complete part (entire antecedent or consequent), an incomplete part (part of antecedent or consequent), or a complete part in one and incomplete in the other. The naturally primary (al-qarib bi al-tab') is when the common part is a complete part in both premises. In this case, the four figures of categorical syllogism apply directly, with the middle term being the shared complete part. The author gives examples for each figure: first figure (universal affirmative conclusion), second figure (universal negative), third figure (particular affirmative), fourth figure (particular affirmative). The conditions of validity, number of moods, and resulting quantity/quality are exactly as in categorical syllogisms. Note that the letters (a, b, c, d, e, z) are term variables representing propositions or terms within conditionals.

Key Terms
الاقترانيات
الشرطيات
الجزء التام
الأوسط
الأشكال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a compound conditional syllogism of the first figure (middle is consequent in minor and antecedent in major) with two universal affirmative conditional premises, what type of conclusion results?
A) Universal affirmative (e.g., 'Whenever A is B then E is Z')
B) Particular affirmative (e.g., 'Possibly if A is B then E is Z')
C) Universal negative (e.g., 'Never if A is B then E is Z')
D) Particular negative (e.g., 'Possibly not if A is B then E is Z')

Key Takeaway
The four figures of compound conditional syllogisms mirror those of categoricals when the middle term is a complete proposition, providing a powerful tool for extended reasoning in Islamic sciences.

5 min
The Five Types of Conditional Syllogisms
Author classifies conditional syllogisms into five types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the five types of conditional syllogisms and identify which premises each type uses.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms are classified by premise types: two conditionals, two disjunctives, or mixes.

In this passage, the author expands on the types of conditional syllogisms, which go beyond simple categoricals. Understanding this classification is crucial for applying syllogistic reasoning to conditional and disjunctive propositions found in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of classification
Note that the author divides conditional syllogisms based on the kinds of premises: two conditionals (متصلتان), two disjunctives (منفصلتان), or a mix with a categorical (حمليّة).
2
List all five combinations
The five types are: 1) two conditionals, 2) two disjunctives, 3) categorical and conditional, 4) categorical and disjunctive, 5) conditional and disjunctive.
3
Recognize the primary focus
The author then focuses on the first type (two conditionals) as the most natural, which will be detailed in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأقسامه خمسة : لأنه أما أن يتركب من المتصلتين أو منفصلتين، أو حملية ومتصلة، أو حمليّة ،ومنفصلة، أو متصلة ومنفصلة : القسم الأول ما يتركب من المتصلتين، والشركة بينهما إما في جزء تام من كل واحدة منهما وهو المقدّم بكماله أو التالي بكماله، وإما في جزء غير تام منهما : أي جزء من المقدّم أو التالي ، وإما في جزء تام من إحداهما غير تام من الأخرى، فهذه ثلاثة أقسام، لكن القريب بالطبع منها الأول وهو ما يكون الشركة في جزء تام من المقدمتين، وتنعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة : لأن الأوسط - وهو المشترك بينهما - إن كان تالياً في الصغرى مقدماً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الأوّل كقولنا : «كلما كان (اب) فـ(ج د) وكلما كان (ج) د) فه ز) فكلما كان (اب) فرهـ ز)». وإن كان تالياً فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني، كقولنا : «كلما كان (اب) فرج (د) وليس البنّة إذا كان ه ز (فـ(ج) (د) فليس ألبتة إذا كان (ا ب فه ز)». : وإن كان مقدّماً فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث، كقولنا : «كلماكان (ج د) ف (اب) وكلّما كان (ج (د) فـه ز) فقد يكون إذا كان (اب) فه ز). وإن كان مقدّماً في الصغرى وتالياً في الكبرى فهو الشكل الرابع كقولنا : كلما كان (ج) د) فرا (ب) وكلماكان (ه ز) فرج د) فقد يكون إذا كان (اب) فره ز). وشرائط إنتاج هذه الأشكال كما في الحمليّات من غير فرق حتى يشترط في الأول إيجاب الصغرى وكلّية الكبرى وفي الثاني اختلاف مقدمتية بالكيف وكلّية الكبرى - إلى غير ذلك. وكذلك عدد ضروبها إلا في الشكل الرابع، فإن ضروبه هاهنا خمسة، لأن إنتاج الضروب الثلاثة الأخيرة بحسب تركيب السالبة، وهو غير معتبر في الشرطيات. وكذلك حال النتيجة في الكميّة والكيفية : فتكون نتيجة الضرب الأول من الشكل الأوّل موجبة كلّية، ومن الشكل الثاني سالبة كلية - وعلى هذا القياس.
And its divisions are five: because it either is composed of two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. The first division is what is composed of two conditionals, and the common part between them is either a complete part of each one of them—which is the whole antecedent or the whole consequent—or an incomplete part of them both: that is, a part of the antecedent or the consequent, or a complete part of one of them and an incomplete part of the other. So these are three divisions, but the natural one among them is the first, which is the common part being a complete part of the two premises, and in it the four figures arise: because the middle—which is the shared part between them—if it is the consequent in the minor and the antecedent in the major, then it is the first figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (ab) is, then (cd) is, and whenever (cd) is, then (ez) is, so whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And if it is the consequent in both, then it is the second figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (ab) is, then (cd) is, and it is not the case that whenever (ez) is, then (cd) is, so it is not the case that whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And if it is the antecedent in both, then it is the third figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (cd) is, then (ab) is, and whenever (cd) is, then (ez) is, so it may be that whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And if it is the antecedent in the minor and the consequent in the major, then it is the fourth figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (cd) is, then (ab) is, and whenever (ez) is, then (cd) is, so it may be that whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And the conditions for productivity of these figures are as in categoricals without any difference, such that the first requires the major to be affirmative and the minor universal, and the second requires the two premises to differ in quality and the major to be universal, and so on. Similarly, the number of their moods is the same except in the fourth figure, for its moods here are five, because the productivity of the last three moods depends on the composition of the negative, which is not considered in conditionals. Likewise, the situation of the conclusion in quantity and quality: so the conclusion of the first mood of the first figure is universal affirmative, and of the second figure is universal negative—and so on.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, explaining the types of conditional syllogisms (اقترانيات شرطية). The author begins by stating that such syllogisms have five divisions based on the types of premises: two conditionals (متصلتان), two disjunctives (منفصلتان), a categorical and a conditional, a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. He then focuses on the first division: syllogisms composed of two conditionals. Within this division, the common part (المشترك) between the two premises—that is, the middle term in the categorical sense—can be either a complete part of each premise (the whole antecedent or whole consequent), an incomplete part (a part of the antecedent or consequent), or a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other. The author notes that the natural (المطبوع) kind is when the common part is a complete part of both premises, and it is in this kind that the four figures arise. He then describes each figure using the position of the middle term (الأوسط) in the minor and major premises. For example, in the first figure, the middle is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major, and he provides a sample syllogism using logical placeholders (اب, ج د, ه ز). The second figure has the middle as consequent in both premises, yielding a negative conclusion. The third figure has the middle as antecedent in both, yielding a particular conclusion (قد يكون). The fourth figure has the middle as antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major, also yielding a particular conclusion. The author then states that the conditions for productivity (شرائط الإنتاج) are the same as in categorical syllogisms: e.g., the first figure requires the minor to be affirmative and the major universal, the second requires differing quality and universal major, etc. The number of moods is also generally the same, except the fourth figure has five moods here because the last three moods involve the negative in a way not applicable to conditionals. Finally, the conclusion's quantity and quality follow the categorical counterparts: the first mood of the first figure gives a universal affirmative, the second figure gives a universal negative, and so on. This passage is a detailed expansion of the matn's compressed content, showing how conditional syllogisms mirror categorical ones structurally.

Key Terms
الاقترانيات الشرطية
المتصلتان
المنفصلتان
حمليّة

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms are divided into five types based on the combination of premise types.

The First Type: Two Conditionals and the Common Part
Author explains three kinds of common part in the first division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term (common part) in a conditional syllogism and classify it as a complete or incomplete part.

Core Idea
The middle term in conditional syllogisms is a clause, and its completeness determines naturalness.

The author now focuses on the first type: syllogisms composed of two conditionals. The key element is the common part (المشترك) between the premises, which can be a complete clause, an incomplete part, or mixed. Understanding this sets the stage for the four figures.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the common part
In a conditional syllogism of two conditionals, the 'middle term' is the clause shared between the two premises. For example, if both premises contain 'if X then Y', the shared clause might be X or Y.
2
Determine completeness
The common part can be a complete part (the whole antecedent or whole consequent), an incomplete part (a part of the antecedent or consequent), or a complete part of one and incomplete of the other.
3
Identify the natural kind
The author notes that the natural (القريب بالطبع) kind is when the common part is a complete part of both premises, as it gives rise to the four figures.

The Four Figures of Conditional Syllogisms
Author describes the four figures of the first division conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of a conditional syllogism based on the position of the middle term and verify its conditions for productivity.

Core Idea
The four figures of conditional syllogisms mirror categorical figures, with the middle term being a clause.

The author now presents the four figures that arise when the common part is a complete clause in both premises. He provides examples using Arabic variables, shows the position of the middle term, and then states that the conditions for productivity are the same as in categorical syllogisms. This lesson will help you recognize these figures and apply the conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In each example, locate the shared clause between the two premises. This is the middle term (الأوسط). For instance, in the first figure example, the middle is '(cd)'.
2
Determine its position in each premise
Check whether the middle term appears as the antecedent (مقدّم) or consequent (تالي) in the minor (first) and major (second) premises.
3
Match to figure
Use the author's rule: If middle is consequent in minor and antecedent in major → first figure; consequent in both → second; antecedent in both → third; antecedent in minor and consequent in major → fourth.
4
Verify the conclusion
The author gives the conclusion for each figure. Compare with the premises to see the logical relationship.
5
Apply the productivity conditions
The author states conditions are like categoricals: first figure requires affirmative minor and universal major; second requires differing quality and universal major; etc. Check these against the examples.

The first division is what is composed of two conditionals, and the common part between them is either a complete part of each one of them—which is the whole antecedent or the whole consequent—or an incomplete part of them both: that is, a part of the antecedent or the consequent, or a complete part of one of them and an incomplete part of the other. So these are three divisions, but the natural one among them is the first, which is the common part being a complete part of the two premises, and in it the four figures arise: because the middle—which is the shared part between them—if it is the consequent in the minor and the antecedent in the major, then it is the first figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (ab) is, then (cd) is, and whenever (cd) is, then (ez) is, so whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And if it is the consequent in both, then it is the second figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (ab) is, then (cd) is, and it is not the case that whenever (ez) is, then (cd) is, so it is not the case that whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And if it is the antecedent in both, then it is the third figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (cd) is, then (ab) is, and whenever (cd) is, then (ez) is, so it may be that whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And if it is the antecedent in the minor and the consequent in the major, then it is the fourth figure, as in our saying: 'Whenever (cd) is, then (ab) is, and whenever (ez) is, then (cd) is, so it may be that whenever (ab) is, then (ez) is.' And the conditions for productivity of these figures are as in categoricals without any difference, such that the first requires the major to be affirmative and the minor universal, and the second requires the two premises to differ in quality and the major to be universal, and so on. Similarly, the number of their moods is the same except in the fourth figure, for its moods here are five, because the productivity of the last three moods depends on the composition of the negative, which is not considered in conditionals. Likewise, the situation of the conclusion in quantity and quality: so the conclusion of the first mood of the first figure is universal affirmative, and of the second figure is universal negative—and so on.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, explaining the types of conditional syllogisms (اقترانيات شرطية). The author begins by stating that such syllogisms have five divisions based on the types of premises: two conditionals (متصلتان), two disjunctives (منفصلتان), a categorical and a conditional, a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. He then focuses on the first division: syllogisms composed of two conditionals. Within this division, the common part (المشترك) between the two premises—that is, the middle term in the categorical sense—can be either a complete part of each premise (the whole antecedent or whole consequent), an incomplete part (a part of the antecedent or consequent), or a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other. The author notes that the natural (المطبوع) kind is when the common part is a complete part of both premises, and it is in this kind that the four figures arise. He then describes each figure using the position of the middle term (الأوسط) in the minor and major premises. For example, in the first figure, the middle is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major, and he provides a sample syllogism using logical placeholders (اب, ج د, ه ز). The second figure has the middle as consequent in both premises, yielding a negative conclusion. The third figure has the middle as antecedent in both, yielding a particular conclusion (قد يكون). The fourth figure has the middle as antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major, also yielding a particular conclusion. The author then states that the conditions for productivity (شرائط الإنتاج) are the same as in categorical syllogisms: e.g., the first figure requires the minor to be affirmative and the major universal, the second requires differing quality and universal major, etc. The number of moods is also generally the same, except the fourth figure has five moods here because the last three moods involve the negative in a way not applicable to conditionals. Finally, the conclusion's quantity and quality follow the categorical counterparts: the first mood of the first figure gives a universal affirmative, the second figure gives a universal negative, and so on. This passage is a detailed expansion of the matn's compressed content, showing how conditional syllogisms mirror categorical ones structurally.

The Natural Form of a Disjunctive-Disjunctive Syllogism
Author introduces natural disjunctive-disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the natural form of a disjunctive-disjunctive syllogism and locate the common part in the premises.

Core Idea
The natural disjunctive syllogism shares an incomplete part between premises.

In this passage, the author introduces a special type of conditional syllogism where both premises are disjunctive. The natural (or primary) form is when the two premises share a part that is not the whole disjunction—an incomplete part. Understanding where this common part lies is essential for constructing valid conclusions. We will walk through the example given and see how the four figures of categorical syllogisms apply here as well.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjunctive premises
Locate the two 'منفصلتین' (disjunctives) in the example. Each is a compound disjunction using 'إما' and 'أو'.
The first premise: 'دائماً إما كل (ا) (ب) أو كل (ج) (د)'; the second: 'دائماً إما كل (د) (ه) أو كل (و) (ز)'.
2
Find the common part (الشركة) between them
Look for a term or clause that appears in both premises. Here, 'كل (د) (ه)' appears as the first disjunct of the second premise and as part of the first premise's second disjunct (the subject 'ج' is different; actually the common part is 'كل (د) (ه)'? Wait: In the first premise, the second disjunct is 'كل (ج) (د)' which is a universal affirmative with subject ج and predicate د. In the second premise, the first disjunct is 'كل (د) (ه)' with subject د and predicate ه. So the term د appears in both. The common part is not the whole disjunct but the term د (the subject/predicate overlap). The text says 'الشركة في جزء غير تامّ من المقدمتين' meaning the common part is an incomplete part (a term, not the whole proposition).
The common part is the element that allows the syllogism to connect, analogous to the middle term in categoricals.
3
Determine whether the common part is complete or incomplete
Check if the shared element is a complete disjunct (a full 'جزيء') or just a component. Here, it is only a term (د), so it is an incomplete part.
The text explicitly states that the natural form has the common part in an incomplete part.
4
Recognize the natural form
When the common part is incomplete in both premises, we have the natural (المطبوع) form. This is the most straightforward and productive way to combine two disjunctive premises.
The commentator will later mention three subdivisions; this is one of them.
5
Construct and verify the conclusion
The conclusion combines all possibilities from both premises, excluding only those ruled out. In the example, the conclusion is: 'دائماً إما كل (ا) (ب) أو كل (ج) (د) أو كل (و) (ز)'. Verify that this follows because reality cannot be devoid of the two premises and one of the latter two.
This form yields multiple disjuncts in the conclusion.

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on conditional syllogisms, now addressing the second division: syllogisms composed of two disjunctive premises (منفصلتان). The author first states that the natural (المطبوع) form is when the common part (الشركة) lies in an incomplete part of both premises—meaning not the whole disjunction, but a component part. He provides an example: 'Always either every A is B or every J is D, and always either every D is H or every W is Z, so always either every A is B or every J is D or every W is Z.' The author notes that in this natural form, the four figures arise, and the same conditions as in categorical syllogisms apply between the shared parts. Then the commentator (أقول: = 'I say') further explains that this second division itself has three subdivisions based on whether the common part is a complete part of both premises, an incomplete part of both, or a complete part of one and incomplete of the other. However, the text breaks off before completing the discussion of which is natural. This passage is a typical expansion of the matn, showing how disjunctive syllogisms mirror the structure of conditional ones but with their own specifics.

Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms with Two Disjunctives
Definition and conditions of disjunctive-disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the natural type of conditional conjunctive syllogism composed of two disjunctives and verify whether it meets the conditions for production.

Core Idea
The shared part between two disjunctive premises functions like the middle term in categorical syllogisms.

This passage introduces the second section of conditional conjunctive syllogisms, where both premises are disjunctive. The author defines the natural form, the conditions for a valid conclusion, and how the four figures apply. Understanding this allows you to analyze compound arguments in Islamic sciences that involve disjunctive propositions, such as legal reasoning with alternative rulings.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two disjunctive premises
In the example, each premise is a disjunction (munfasila) of the type man' al-khuluww (prevention of emptiness). Identify the parts of each disjunction: first premise has parts 'every A is B' and 'every J is D'; second premise has 'every D is H' and 'every W is Z'.
2
Find the commonality (sharika)
Determine which part appears in both premises. Here, the term 'D' appears in 'every J is D' and 'every D is H'. The commonality is a partial part (incomplete) of both premises.
The shared part is not a full proposition but a term (J, D, H, etc.).
3
Check the conditions for production
Ensure both premises are affirmative (ījāb), one is universal (kulliyya), and both are of the man' al-khuluww type. In the example, both are affirmative and universal, satisfying the conditions.
4
Derive the three-part conclusion
The conclusion combines the non-shared parts from each premise and the result of composition (the shared parts combined). The text shows that if the shared part is true from the first premise, then from the second premise either the shared or non-shared part is true, leading to three possibilities that cover all cases.
The result of composition is a categorical syllogism: every J is D, every D is H, so every J is H.
5
Verify using the impossibility of emptiness
Since each disjunctive is man' al-khuluww (cannot both be false), exactly one part from each is true. This forces the truth of the conclusion: reality cannot be empty of the result of composition and the two non-shared parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: القسم الثاني ما يتركب من المنفصلتين: والمطبوع منه ماكانت الشركة في جزء غير تامّ من المقدمتين، كقولنا: دائماً إما كلّ (ا ب) أوكل (ج د)، ودائماً إما كل (د ه) أوكل (و ز)» دائماً إما كلّ ا (ب) أو كلّ (ج) (٥) أوكل (و ز)» لامتناع خلو الواقع عن مقدمتي التأليف وعن إحدى الأخيرين، فينعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة، والشرائط المعتبرة بين الحمليتين معتبرة هاهنا بين المشاركين. أقول: القسم الثاني من الاقترانيات الشرطية ما يتركب من منفصلتين وهو أيضاً ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام : لأن الشركة بينهما إما في جزء تام منهما أو في جزء غيرتام منهما، أو في جزء تام من إحداهما غير تام من الأخرى، إلا أن المطبوع من هذه الأقسام ماتكون الشركة في جزء غيرتام من المقدمتين. وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المقدمتين، وكلّية إحداهما وصدق منع الخلو عليهما، كقولنا : دائماً إما كلّ (اب) أوكل (ج د) ودائماً إما كل (ده) أوكل (و ز) » ينتج : «دائماً إمـا كـل (اب) أوكـل (ج ) أوكـل (و ز)» لامتناع خلوّ الواقع عن مقدّمتي التأليف - وهماكل (ج د) وكل (د ه)- وعن إحدى الأخريين : أي كلّ (اب) وكلّ (و ز) فإنه لما كانت المقدمتان مانعتي الخلو وجب أن يكون أحد طرفي كل واحدة منهما واقعاً في الواقع والآخر غير واقع، فالواقع من المنفصلة الأولى، إما الطرف الغير المشارك أو الطرف المشارك؛ فإن كان الطرف الغير المشارك فهو أحد أجزاء النتيجة، وإن كان الطرف المشارك، فالواقع معه من المنفصلة الثانية إما الطرف المشارك فيجتمع الطرفان المشاركان على الصدق، وتصدق نتيجة التأليف - وهي الجزء الأخير من النتيجة - أو الطرف الغير المشارك وهو الجزء الثالث، فالواقع لا يخلو عن نتيجة التأليف وعن الطرفين الغير المشاركين. و تنعقد الأشكال الأربعة في هذا القسم أيضاً بحسب الطرفين المشاركين ويعتبر فيهما أن يكونا على شرائط الإنتاج المعتبرة بين الحمليتين
He said: The second section is what is composed of two disjunctives. The natural type of it is that the commonality is in a partial part of the two premises, like our saying: Always every (A is B) or every (J is D), and always every (D is H) or every (W is Z); therefore always every (A is B) or every (J is ?) or every (W is Z), because it is impossible for reality to be empty of the two premises of composition and of one of the two latter. In it the four figures are formed, and the conditions considered between categoricals are considered here between the shared parts. I say: The second section of conditional conjunctive syllogisms is what is composed of two disjunctives, and it also is divided into three parts: because commonality between them is either in a complete part of both, or in an incomplete part of both, or in a complete part of one and incomplete part of the other, except that the natural of these sections is that the commonality is in an incomplete part of the two premises. And the condition of its production is that both premises be affirmative, and one of them be universal, and the truth of the prevention of emptiness (man' al-khuluww) be applicable to them, like our saying: Always every (A is B) or every (J is D), and always every (D is H) or every (W is Z); it produces: Always every (A is B) or every (J is ?) or every (W is Z), because it is impossible for reality to be empty of the two premises of composition – which are every (J is D) and every (D is H) – and of one of the two others, i.e., every (A is B) and every (W is Z). For when the two premises are preventers of emptiness (man'at al-khuluww), it is necessary that one of the two parts of each of them be realized in reality and the other unrealized. So what is realized from the first disjunctive is either the non-shared part or the shared part. If it is the non-shared part, it is one of the parts of the conclusion. If it is the shared part, then what is realized with it from the second disjunctive is either the shared part, so the two shared parts are together true, and the result of composition – which is the last part of the conclusion – is true; or the non-shared part, which is the third part. So reality is not empty of the result of composition and of the two non-shared parts. And the four figures are formed in this section as well according to the two shared parts, and it is considered between them that they be upon the conditions of production considered between categoricals.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the second section of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (al-iqtiraniāt al-sharṭiyya). It opens by quoting the original text: the second section is composed of two disjunctive premises (munfaṣilatān). The most natural form of this section has commonality (sharika) in a partial part of the two premises, meaning that the two disjunctive premises share a term that appears only partially in each. An example is given: "Always every A is B or every J is D; always every D is H or every W is Z" produces "Always every A is B or every J is ? or every W is Z". The reasoning is that reality cannot be empty of the two premises of composition (the two shared parts: "every J is D" and "every D is H") and also cannot be empty of one of the other two parts ("every A is B" or "every W is Z"). Then the author (presumably al-Qutb al-Taḥtānī) comments: this section divides into three types based on whether the commonality is in a complete part of both premises, an incomplete part of both, or a complete part of one and incomplete part of the other. The natural type is the second. The condition for production (intāj) is that both premises be affirmative (ījāb), one of them be universal (kulliyya), and the truth of the condition "prevention of emptiness" (man' al-khuluww) apply—meaning that the two disjunctive premises are of the mutually exclusive type where both parts cannot be false. The author then elaborates on why the conclusion follows: since each disjunctive has one true part, we consider cases. The four figures of the syllogism (ashkāl arba'a) are formed here as well, based on the two shared terms, and the conditions of production between categoricals apply to these shared terms.

The Syllogism

Third Section: Categorical and Conditional Syllogism
Defines mixed categorical-conditional syllogism with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate a mixed syllogism composed of a categorical premise and a conditional premise in the natural form.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism with a conditional minor and categorical major yields a conditional conclusion by composing the consequent with the categorical.

This passage introduces a third section of conditional conjunctive syllogisms, where the premises are a categorical and a conditional. The natural form places the categorical as the major premise and the conditional as the minor, with the common term in the consequent. By composing, we derive a new conditional conclusion. This type of reasoning is essential for extending categorical knowledge to conditional contexts in Islamic sciences, such as deriving rulings from conditional legal principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the categorical proposition (simple attribution) and the conditional proposition (if-then). In the example: 'Every D is H' (categorical) and 'Whenever A is B, then J is D' (conditional).
2
Determine major and minor
In the natural form, the categorical is the major premise (kubrā) and the conditional is the minor. The common term (sharika) is the consequent of the conditional.
The common term here is D.
3
Verify the commonality
Check that the common term appears in the consequent of the conditional and in the categorical premise. Here, D is in the consequent of the conditional and the subject of the categorical.
4
Perform the composition
Compose (ta'līf) the consequent (J is D) with the categorical (every D is H) to produce a new categorical proposition: every J is H.
This is like a first-figure categorical syllogism applied to the consequent.
5
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the minor premise (A is B) and the new categorical as the consequent (every J is H). Result: 'Whenever A is B, then every J is H'.
This conclusion is a conditional conjunctive proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: القسم الثالث ما يتركب من الحملية والمتصلة: والمطبوع منه ماكانت الحملية كبرى والشركة مع تالي المتصلة، ونتيجته متصلة مقدّمها مقدّم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين التالي والحملية، كقولنا: كلما كان (ا ب) فرج د) وكل (د هـ)» ينتج «كلما كان (ا ب) فكـل (ج هـ)
He said: The third section is what is composed of the categorical and the conditional. The natural type of it is that the categorical is the major and the commonality is with the consequent of the conditional, and its conclusion is a conditional of which the antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and the consequent is the result of composition between the consequent and the categorical, like our saying: Whenever (A is B) then (J is D), and every (D is H); it produces: Whenever (A is B) then every (J is H).
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage defines the third section of conditional conjunctive syllogisms: those composed of a categorical premise (ḥamliyya) and a conditional premise (muttaṣila). The natural and most common type is when the categorical premise is the major premise (kubrā) and the commonality (sharika) is with the consequent (tālī) of the conditional. In other words, the conditional is the minor premise, and its consequent shares a term with the categorical premise. The conclusion is a new conditional proposition whose antecedent is the same as the antecedent of the minor premise, and whose consequent is the result of composing (ta'līf) the consequent of the minor with the categorical major. An example is given: 'Whenever A is B, then J is D; and every D is H; therefore whenever A is B, then every J is H.' This is analogous to a first-figure categorical syllogism, but with a conditional minor and categorical major, producing a conditional conclusion. The 'composition' refers to applying the categorical premise (every D is H) to the consequent (J is D) to produce a new categorical (every J is H), which then becomes the consequent of the conclusion.

Key Terms
حملیة
متصلہ
تالی
تأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a third-section conditional conjunctive syllogism, if the conditional premise is 'Whenever alcohol is consumed, it causes intoxication' and the categorical is 'Every intoxication is haram', what is the conclusion?
A) Whenever alcohol is consumed, it causes intoxication.
B) Whenever alcohol is consumed, it is haram.
C) Some intoxication is haram.
D) Every alcohol is haram.

Key Takeaway
In a mixed categorical-conditional syllogism, the conclusion is a conditional whose consequent combines the categorical with the original consequent.

The Third Division of Conditional Syllogisms: The Standard Form
Defines the third division and its standard form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the standard form of a conditional-categorical composite syllogism and construct its conclusion correctly.

Core Idea
When a categorical premise shares its term with the consequent of a conditional, the conclusion is a conditional linking the antecedent to the combined result.

In this lesson, we explore the third division of conditional syllogisms, where one premise is categorical and the other is conditional. The author presents the most common form, where the categorical is the major premise and the common term is the consequent of the conditional. Understanding this structure is essential for constructing valid arguments that mix proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the categorical premise (e.g., 'Every D is H') and the conditional premise (e.g., 'Whenever A is B, every C is D').
2
Find the common term (al-sharika)
The term that appears in both premises. In the example, it is D, which is the consequent of the conditional and the subject of the categorical.
3
Determine the standard form
Check if the categorical is major and the common term is the consequent. If yes, this is the matbu' (commonly used) form.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: antecedent = antecedent of the conditional, consequent = the result of composing the categorical with the consequent (i.e., the predicate of the categorical applied to the subject of the consequent). In the example: 'Whenever A is B, every C is H.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : القسم الثالث ما يتركب من الحملية والمتصلة: والمطبوع منه ماكانت الحملية كبرى والشركة مع تالي المتصلة، ونتيجته متصلة مقدّمها مقدّم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين التالي والحملية، كقولنا: كلما كان (ا ب) فكل (ج د) وكلّ (د هـ)» ينتج «كلما كان (ا ب) فكل (ج هـ» وينعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة، والشرائط المعتبرة بين الحمليتين معتبرة هاهنا بين التالي والحملية. أقول: القسم الثالث من الأقيسة الشرطية ما يتركب من الحملية والمتصلة والحمليّة فيه إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وأيا ماكان فالمشارك لها إما تالي المتصلة أو مقدّمها، فهذه أربعة أقسام، إلا أن المطبوع منها ماكانت الحملية كبرى والشركة مع تالي المتصلة. وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المتصلة. ونتيجته متصلة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين التالي والحمليّة، كقولنا : «كلما كان (ا ب) فكل (ج د) وكلّ (د هـ) » ينتج كلّما كان (ا ب) فكل (ج هـ) » ؛ لأنه كلما صدق مقدم المتصلة صدق التالي مع الحمليّة : أما صدق التالي، فظاهر ؛ وأما صدق الحملية فلأنها صادقة في نفس الأمر، فتكون صادقة على ذلك التقدير، وكلما صدق التالي مع الحملية صدق نتيجة التأليف، فكلّما صدق المقدم صدق نتيجة التأليف وهو المطلوب. وتنعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة باعتبار مشاركة التالي والحملية. والشرائط المعتبرة بين الحمليتين معتبرة هاهنا بين التالي والحملية.
He said: The third division is what is composed of the categorical and the conditional. The commonly used among it is when the categorical is the major premise and the common part is with the consequent of the conditional, and its conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of composition between the consequent and the categorical, as in our saying: 'Whenever (A is B), every (C is D), and every (D is H),' it produces 'Whenever (A is B), every (C is H).' And the four figures are formed in it, and the conditions considered between two categoricals are considered here between the consequent and the categorical. I say: The third division of conditional syllogisms is what is composed of the categorical and the conditional, and the categorical in it is either minor or major, and whichever it is, the part shared with it is either the consequent of the conditional or its antecedent, so these are four subdivisions, except that the commonly used among them is when the categorical is the major and the common part is with the consequent of the conditional. Its condition for production is the affirmation of the conditional. And its conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of composition between the consequent and the categorical, as in our saying: 'Whenever (A is B), every (C is D), and every (D is H),' it produces 'Whenever (A is B), every (C is H).' This is because whenever the antecedent of the conditional is true, the consequent along with the categorical is true: as for the truth of the consequent, it is obvious; and as for the truth of the categorical, it is because it is true in itself, so it is true under that estimation; and whenever the consequent along with the categorical is true, the result of composition is true; so whenever the antecedent is true, the result of composition is true, which is what is sought. And the four figures are formed in it on account of the sharing of the consequent and the categorical. And the conditions considered between two categoricals are considered here between the consequent and the categorical.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the third division of conditional syllogisms, which combines a categorical (ḥamliyya) premise with a conditional (muttaṣila) premise. The author first states the typical form: the categorical is the major premise, and the common term (the part shared between the premises) is the consequent of the conditional. The conclusion is itself a conditional: its antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the original consequent with the categorical premise (i.e., a syllogistic conclusion between them). An example is given with terms: 'Whenever A is B, every C is D; and every D is H; therefore, whenever A is B, every C is H.' This mirrors a first-figure categorical syllogism inside the consequent. The author notes that the four figures of categorical syllogism apply here, with the same conditions (e.g., universality, affirmation) now applied to the relationship between the consequent and the categorical premise. Then the commentary expands: there are four possible combinations (categorical as minor/major, shared part as consequent/antecedent), but the most common is the one described. The condition for production is that the conditional must be affirmative. The reasoning is explained: whenever the antecedent holds, the consequent holds (by the conditional), and the categorical is always true, so the combination holds. Thus the conclusion follows. This division is foundational for syllogisms that mix different types of premises.

Conditions and Variations in Composite Conditional Syllogisms
Expands on subdivisions and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four possible subdivisions of the third division and explain the condition for its validity.

Core Idea
The third division has four possible arrangements, but only one is commonly used; validity requires an affirmative conditional and the same conditions as categorical syllogisms.

The author's commentary expands on the initial statement, revealing that the third division is not monolithic. There are four subdivisions based on which premise is major/minor and which part of the conditional is shared. Additionally, the condition of affirmation and the requirement that the categorical conditions apply between the consequent and the categorical are crucial for validity.

Guided Path
1
List the four subdivisions
The commentary states: categorical can be minor or major, shared part can be consequent or antecedent. So 2x2 = 4.
2
Identify the commonly used subdivision
The matbu' is when the categorical is major and shared part is the consequent.
3
Note the condition for production
The conditional must be affirmative (positive/affirmative) for the syllogism to work.
4
Apply categorical conditions
The usual conditions for validity between two categoricals (e.g., universality, figure conditions) apply here between the consequent and the categorical premise.
5
Understand the truth reasoning
When the antecedent of the conditional is true, the consequent is true (by the conditional), and the categorical is always true, so the combination (the result of composition) is true; thus the conclusion follows.
