Fourth Division: Mixed Categorical-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author introduces the fourth division and its first subdivision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first subdivision of mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogisms and determine the required number of categorical premises based on the number of disjuncts.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, match categorical premises one-to-one with disjuncts.

So far we have studied conditional syllogisms composed entirely of conditionals. The author now introduces a fourth division that mixes categorical (ḥamliyya) and disjunctive (munfaṣila) premises. This is a crucial step in expanding the toolkit of logical inference. The first subdivision establishes a foundational rule: the number of categorical premises must equal the number of disjuncts, and each categorical must share a term with its corresponding disjunct. Understanding this structure prepares you for more complex compound syllogisms used in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Locate the disjunctive premise (منفصلة) and the categorical premises (حمليات) in the passage. The disjunctive premise contains multiple parts (أجزاء الانفصال).
2
Count the disjuncts
Determine how many parts (disjuncts) the disjunctive premise has. The text states that the number of categorical premises should equal this number.
Example: if the disjunction is 'Either A is B or C is D', there are two disjuncts.
3
Match categoricals to disjuncts
Each categorical premise must share a term with one of the disjuncts. For instance, if a disjunct is 'A is B', the matching categorical premise should involve A and B.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a fiqh example: 'Either this liquid is khamr or it is nabidh. If it is khamr, it is forbidden (categorical premise 1). If it is nabidh, it is permissible unless intoxicating (categorical premise 2).' This illustrates the first subdivision.

Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage begins the exposition of the fourth division of conditional syllogisms, which combines a categorical (ḥamliyya) premise with a disjunctive (munfaṣila) premise. The author states that there are two subdivisions. The first subdivision is when the number of categorical premises is equal to the number of disjuncts in the disjunction, and each categorical premise shares a term with one of the disjuncts. For example, if the disjunction is 'Either A is B or C is D', and there are two categorical premises like 'Every A is B' and 'Every C is E', then the categorical premises correspond to each disjunct. The purpose is to produce a conclusion that combines the categorical conclusions with the disjunctive structure. This is a preliminary definition; the text cuts off before explaining the second subdivision or giving an example.

Mixed Categorical-Conjunctive Syllogisms: Structure and Conditions
Author defines the mixed categorical-conjunctive syllogism, its subdivisions, and validity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a mixed categorical-conjunctive syllogism, determine its most common arrangement, and verify the condition for validity.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism combines a categorical and a conjunctive, with categorical conditions applied to the consequent.

In this passage from the Qutbi, the author explains the third type of conditional syllogism, which blends a categorical proposition with a conjunctive conditional. Understanding this mixed form is crucial for handling compound proofs in Islamic theology and law, where a conditional chain is often combined with a universal categorical truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
One premise is a categorical proposition (حمليّة), the other is a conjunctive conditional (متصلة).
The categorical may be minor or major; the conjunctive has an antecedent and consequent.
2
Determine the role and partner of the categorical
The categorical can be minor or major, and it partners either the antecedent or consequent of the conjunctive. There are four possible subdivisions.
The most common arrangement is the categorical as major partnering the consequent.
3
Apply the condition for validity
The conjunctive must be affirmative (positive). This is stated as 'the condition for its production is the affirmation of the conjunctive'.
Negation would prevent the inference.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a new conjunctive: its antecedent is the same as the original conjunctive's antecedent, and its consequent is the result of combining (تأليف) the original consequent with the categorical premise.
For example: 'Whenever A is B, then J is D; and every D is H' yields 'Whenever A is B, then H'.
5
Observe the transfer of conditions
The same conditions that hold for a valid categorical syllogism (e.g., distribution, figure rules) now apply between the consequent and the categorical premise.
Thus, the four figures occur depending on how the consequent and categorical are arranged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث من الأقيسة الشرطية ما يتركب من الحملية والمتصلة وينعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة، والشرائط المعتبرة بين الحمليتين معتبرة هاهنا بين التالي والحملية. أقول: القسم الثالث من الأقيسة الشرطية ما يتركب من الحملية والمتصلة. والحمليّة فيه إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، وأيا ما كان فالمشارك لها إما تالي المتصلة أو مقدمها، فهذه أربعة أقسام، إلا أن المطبوع منها ما كانت الحملية كبرى والشركة مع تالي المتصلة. وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المتصلة. ونتيجته متصلة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين التالي والحمليّة، كقولنا: «كلما كان (اب) ف (ج د) وكلّ (د) هـ» ينتج «كلما كان (اب) هـ»؛ لأنه كلما صدق مقدم المتصلة صدق التالي مع الحمليّة: أما صدق التالي، فظاهر؛ وأما صدق الحملية فلأنها صادقة في نفس الأمر، فتكون صادقة على ذلك التقدير، وكلما صدق التالي مع الحملية صدق نتيجة التأليف، فكلما صدق المقدم صدق نتيجة التأليف وهو المطلوب. وتنعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة باعتبار مشاركة التالي والحملية. والشرائط المعتبرة بين الحمليتين معتبرة هاهنا بين التالي والحملية.
The third division of conditional syllogisms is what is composed of a categorical and a conjunctive [conditional]. And the four figures occur in it, and the conditions considered between two categoricals are considered here between the consequent and the categorical. I say: The third division of conditional syllogisms is what is composed of a categorical and a conjunctive. The categorical in it is either minor or major, and whatever it is, the partner to it is either the consequent of the conjunctive or its antecedent. So these are four subdivisions, except that the common one is when the categorical is major and the partnership is with the consequent of the conjunctive. The condition for its production is the affirmation of the conjunctive. Its result is a conjunctive whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conjunctive and whose consequent is the result of the composition between the consequent and the categorical, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then J is D, and every D is H' yields 'Whenever A is B, then H'; because whenever the antecedent of the conjunctive is true, the consequent together with the categorical is true: as for the truth of the consequent, it is obvious; as for the truth of the categorical, it is true in itself, so it is true on that estimation. And whenever the consequent together with the categorical is true, the result of the composition is true, so whenever the antecedent is true, the result of the composition is true, which is the desired. And the four figures occur in it according to the partnership of the consequent and the categorical. And the conditions considered between two categoricals are considered here between the consequent and the categorical.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage from the Qutbi defines the third division of conditional syllogisms (al-aqyisa al-shartiyya). The author distinguishes it as composed of a categorical premise (hamliyya) and a conjunctive conditional (muttaṣila). He first presents the original text (matn) stating that the four figures apply, and the conditions that hold between two categoricals now hold between the consequent (tālī) of the conjunctive and the categorical premise. Then the commentator expands: the categorical can be either the minor or major premise, and it can partner either the consequent or the antecedent of the conjunctive. Among these four possible arrangements, the most common is the one where the categorical is the major and partners the consequent. The syllogism's condition is that the conjunctive must be affirmative (positive). The conclusion is itself a conjunctive: its antecedent is the same as the antecedent of the original conjunctive, and its consequent is the result of combining (ta'līf) the original consequent with the categorical. The example illustrates: 'Whenever A is B, then J is D; and every D is H' yields 'Whenever A is B, then H'. The reasoning: whenever the antecedent (A is B) is true, the consequent (J is D) is true, and the categorical (every D is H) is also true because it holds in itself; thus the composition (J is D and every D is H) yields J is H; so whenever A is B, J is H. The commentator notes that the four figures apply here depending on the position of the partnership between the consequent and the categorical, and the same validity conditions as for categorical syllogisms are transferred to this context.

متصلة
حملیة
تالی
مقدم

C) The conjunctive must be affirmative (positive)
D) The partnership must be with the antecedent

Key Takeaway
Mixed categorical-conjunctive syllogisms require the conjunctive to be affirmative; the standard form uses the categorical as major partnering the consequent, and the conclusion is a new conjunctive.

Therefore, if the world is created, the creator is powerful.' Specify the categorical premise, its role (major/minor), and the partner.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Fourth Type of Conditional Syllogism: Categorical and Disjunctive
Author defines the fourth division of conditional syllogisms with disjunctive premises, detailing two subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two subdivisions of the fourth type of conditional syllogism (categoricals equal to or fewer than disjuncts) and construct a valid divided syllogism (qiyās muqassam) and a syllogism with fewer categoricals using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The number of categorical premises relative to disjuncts determines the conclusion's form.

This passage from al-Qutbī's logic text introduces the fourth division of conditional syllogisms: those combining a categorical with a disjunctive. The author divides it into two main kinds: when the categorical premises equal the number of disjuncts, and when they are fewer. In the first kind, if all categoricals share the same predicate, the conclusion is a single universal categorical (the divided syllogism); if not, the conclusion is a disjunctive. In the second kind, the categorical premise partners with one disjunct, and the conclusion replaces that disjunct with the result of the partnership. Understanding these forms is essential for advanced reasoning in Islamic sciences like uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subdivision
Determine whether the number of categorical premises equals the number of disjuncts or is fewer. The passage describes two cases: 'الأول: أن يكون عدد الحمليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال' and 'الثاني: أن تكون الحمليات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال'.
The commentary notes the division is not exhaustive; categoricals could also be more numerous.
2
For equal number: check the composition
If the categoricals all share the same predicate (unified composition), the syllogism is a 'divided syllogism' (qiyās muqassam) and yields a single categorical conclusion. If they have different predicates (different composition), the conclusion is a disjunctive with those predicates.
Examples: unified: 'كل ج إما ب أو د أو ه, كل ب ط, كل د ط, كل ه ط' yields 'كل ج ط'. Different: same disjunctive but categoricals with different predicates yields a disjunctive conclusion.
3
For the divided syllogism: verify the condition
The disjunctive must be affirmative, universal, and exclusive of emptiness (māni'a al-khuluw) or real (ḥaqīqiyya). This ensures at least one disjunct is true, so the conclusion follows from any case.
The commentary states: 'وشرطه أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة كلية مانعة الخلو أو حقيقية'.
4
For fewer categoricals: identify the partnered disjunct
The categorical premise partners with one disjunct (shares a term). Compose them to generate a new predicate for that disjunct. The conclusion is a disjunction of the unpartnered disjunct and the new composition result.
Example: 'إما كل أ ط أو كل ج ب, وكل ب د' yields 'إما كل أ ط أو كل ج د' because the categorical composes with the second disjunct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The fourth division is what is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive [conditional], and it is of two kinds: First, the number of categoricals equals the number of disjuncts so that each categorical partners one of the disjuncts, either with unification of the composition in the conclusion, as in our saying: 'Every J is either B or D or H, and every B is T, and every D is T, and every H is T' yields 'Every J is T' because of the truth of one of the disjuncts with its partnered categorical. Or with difference of composition in the conclusion, as in our saying: 'Every J is either B or D or H, and every B is J, and every D is T, and every H is Z' yields 'Every J is either J or T or Z' as before. Second, the categoricals are fewer than the disjuncts, and let the categorical be one part and the disjunctive two parts, and the partnership with one of them, as in our saying: 'Either every A is T or every J is B, and every B is D' yields 'Either every A is T or every J is D' because it is impossible for reality to be empty of both premises of composition and the unpartnered part. I say: The fourth of the divisions is what is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive, and it is of two kinds, because the categoricals are either equal to the number of disjuncts or fewer than them, and this division is not exhaustive because it is possible they are more numerous than the disjuncts. First, that the categoricals are equal to the number of disjuncts, and we suppose that each of the categoricals partners one of the disjuncts, and then the compositions between the categoricals and the disjuncts are either unified in the conclusion or different. As for when the results of the compositions are one, it is the divided syllogism (al-qiyās al-muqassam), and its condition is that the disjunctive is affirmative, universal, exclusive of emptiness (māni‘a al-khuluw) or real (ḥaqīqiyya), as in our saying: 'Every J is either B or D or H, and every B is T, and every D is T, and every H is T' yields 'Every J is T' because one of the disjuncts must be true and the categoricals are true in themselves, so whichever disjunct is assumed true,
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth division of conditional syllogisms, where a categorical premise is combined with a disjunctive conditional (munfaṣila). The author presents two main subdivisions based on whether the number of categorical premises equals the number of disjuncts or is fewer. (He later notes in the commentary that the division is not exhaustive; categoricals could also be more numerous.) The first subdivision (equal number) has two subcases: (a) unified composition – all categoricals share the same predicate, producing a universal categorical conclusion using a divided syllogism (al-qiyās al-muqassam); (b) different composition – the categoricals have different predicates, producing a disjunctive conclusion with those predicates as disjuncts. Example for (a): 'Every J is either B or D or H; all B, D, H are T' yields 'Every J is T'. Example for (b): 'Every J is either B or D or H; B is J, D is T, H is Z' yields 'Every J is either J or T or Z'. The second subdivision (fewer categoricals) uses a disjunctive with two disjuncts and one categorical partnered with one disjunct; the conclusion is a disjunctive with the other disjunct unchanged and the partnered disjunct replaced by the result of composition. Example: 'Either every A is T or every J is B; every B is D' yields 'Either every A is T or every J is D'. The reasoning invokes impossibility of both premises and the unpartnered part being false. The commentary then begins to elaborate on the first subdivision, noting the condition that the disjunctive must be affirmative, universal, and exclusive of emptiness (māni‘a al-khuluw) or real (ḥaqīqiyya), but the text cuts off.

Compound Syllogisms from Categorical and Disjunctives: Divided and Undivided
Author enumerates and exemplifies two cases of compound syllogisms with categoricals and disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between divided (al-qiyās al-muqassam) and undivided (al-qiyās ghayr al-muqassam) compound syllogisms, and construct valid examples in Islamic reasoning.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms with categoricals and a disjunctive yield a single conclusion when categoricals share a predicate, and a disjunctive conclusion otherwise.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth type of compound syllogism, which mixes categorical (ḥamliyya) premises with a disjunctive (munfaṣila) premise. The author divides this into two cases based on the number of categoricals relative to the disjuncts. Within the first case (equal number), we further distinguish divided syllogisms, where all the categoricals share a common predicate, from undivided syllogisms, where the categoricals lead to different predicates. You will learn the conditions for validity and see examples with variables that you can apply to Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunctive proposition in the argument. It must be universal affirmative and exhaustive (māni‘at al-khuluw) for the divided syllogism.
The author specifies: 'شرطه أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة كلّية مانعة الخلو أو حقيقية'.
2
Count the categoricals
Compare the number of categorical premises to the number of disjuncts. The two main cases are equal or less.
The division is not exhaustive; categoricals could also be more numerous, but that case is not treated.
3
Check for common predicate
For the equal case, see if all categoricals have the same predicate. If yes, it is divided (muqassam); if different predicates, it is undivided (ghayr muqassam).
The common predicate is what makes the conclusion a single categorical statement.
4
Trace the reasoning
Since the disjunctive is exhaustive, one disjunct must be true. That disjunct, together with its categorical, yields a statement about the predicate(s). For divided, all yield the same; for undivided, each yields a different predicate.
The author says: 'فأي جزء يفرض صدقه من أجزاء المنفصلة يصدق ما يشاركه من الحمليات'.
5
Formulate the conclusion
For divided, state the common predicate for the subject of the disjunctive. For undivided, state a new disjunctive combining the predicates.
Example: 'كل ج إما ب أو د أو هـ; كل ب ط; كل د ط; كل هـ ط' yields 'كل ج ط'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: رابع الأقسام مايتركب من الحملية والمنفصلة، وهو قسمان، لأن الحمليات إما أن تكون بعدد أجزاء الانفصال أو تكون أقل منها، وهذه القسمة ليست بحاصرة الجواز كونها أكثر عدداً من أجزاء الانفصال: الأول أن تكون الحمليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال، ولنفرض أن كل واحدة من الحمليّات يشارك جزء واحداً من أجزاء الانفصال، وحينئذ إما أن تكون التأليفات بين الحمليات وأجزاء الانفصال متحدة في النتيجة أو مختلفة فيها. أما إذا كانت نتائج التأليفات واحدة فهو القياس المقسم، و شرطه أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة كلّية مانعة الخلو أو حقيقية، كقولنا : كل (ج) إمّا (ب) وإمّا (د) وإمّا (هـ) وكلّ (بط) وكل (دط) وكل (هـ ط) ينتج كل (ج (ط) لأنه لابد من صدق أحد أجزاء الانفصال والحمليات صادقة في نفس الأمر، فأي جزء يفرض صدقه من أجزاء المنفصلة يصدق ما يشاركه من الحمليات وينتج النتيجة المطلوبة. وأما إذا كانت نتائج التأليفات مختلفة وهو القياس الغير المقسم، فلتكن المنفصلة مانعة الخلو كقولنا : «كلّ (ج) إمّا (ب) وإما (د) وإما (هـ) وكل (ب ج) وكلّ (د ط) وكلّ ( هـ (ز) » ينتج كلّ (ج) إمّا (ج) وإما (ط) وإما (ز) لمامر من وجوب صدق أحد أجزاء المنفصلة مع ما يشاركه من الحمليات. الثاني أن تكون الحمليّات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال، ولنفرض الحملية واحدة والمنفصلة ذات جزأين ومانعة الخلو ومشاركة الحملية مع أحدهما، كقولنا : ماكل (اط) أو كلٌ (ج) ب) وكلّ (ب (د) » ينتج : «إما كل (اط) أوكل (ج د) » لأنّ المنفصلة لما كانت مانعة الخلو وجب صدق أحد جزأيها، فالواقع منهما إما الجزء الغير المشارك – وهو أحد جزأي النتيجة - أو الجزء المشارك، فيصدق مع الحمليّات - وهما مقدمتا التأليف - فيصدق نتيجة التأليف، وهي الجزء الآخر من النتيجة، فالواقع لا يخلو عن جزأيها.
I say: The fourth division is what is composed of categorical and disjunctive [propositions], and it is two types, because the categoricals are either equal in number to the parts of the disjunction or less than them, and this division is not exhaustive because it is possible for them to be more numerous than the parts of the disjunction. First: that the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction, and let us suppose that each one of the categoricals shares one part of the parts of the disjunction, and then the compositions between the categoricals and the parts of the disjunction are either unified in the result or different in it. As for when the results of the compositions are one, it is the divided syllogism, and its condition is that the disjunctive is affirmative universal, preventing emptiness [i.e., exhaustive] or true, as we say: 'Every J is either B or D or H, and every B is T, and every D is T, and every H is T' yields 'Every J is T' because it is necessary that one of the parts of the disjunction is true and the categoricals are true in actual fact, so whichever part of the disjunction is assumed true, what shares with it from the categoricals becomes true and yields the desired result. And as for when the results of the compositions are different, that is the undivided syllogism, so let the disjunctive be preventive of emptiness, as we say: 'Every J is either B or D or H, and every B is J, and every D is T, and every H is Z' yields 'Every J is either J or T or Z' due to the aforementioned necessity of the truth of one part of the disjunction together with what shares with it from the categoricals. Second: that the categoricals are less than the parts of the disjunction, and let us suppose the categorical is one and the disjunctive has two parts and is preventive of emptiness, and the categorical shares with one of them, as we say: 'Either every A is T or every J is B, and every B is D' yields 'Either every A is T or every J is D' because, since the disjunctive is preventive of emptiness, one of its two parts must be true, so what actually occurs from them is either the non-participating part – which is one of the two parts of the result – or the participating part, then it becomes true with the categoricals – which are the two premises of the composition – so the result of the composition becomes true, and it is the other part of the result; thus what actually occurs is not free from its two parts.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage expands on the fourth type of compound syllogism, which combines categorical (ḥamliyya) propositions with a disjunctive (munfaṣila) proposition. The author begins by stating that this type has two main cases: either the number of categoricals equals the number of disjuncts, or it is less. He notes that the division is not exhaustive because one could also have more categoricals than disjuncts, though he does not treat that. For the first case (equal number), he further subdivides: either the compositions yield the same result (divided syllogism, qiyās muqassam) or different results (undivided syllogism, qiyās ghayr muqassam). In the divided syllogism, all categoricals share a common predicate (T) and the disjuncts exhaust the subject (J). The condition is that the disjunctive must be affirmative universal and either exhaustive (māni‘at al-khuluw) or true (ḥaqīqiyya). He gives an example: 'Every J is either B or D or H; every B is T; every D is T; every H is T' yields 'Every J is T'. The reasoning: one disjunct must be true; whichever it is, the corresponding categorical applies, so the common predicate T follows. In the undivided syllogism, the categoricals have different predicates (J, T, Z). The example yields 'Every J is either J or T or Z' because the disjuncts apply with their respective categoricals. For the second case (fewer categoricals than disjuncts), he assumes one categorical and a two-part disjunctive that is exhaustive. The categorical shares with one disjunct. Example: 'Either every A is T or every J is B; every B is D' yields 'Either every A is T or every J is D'. Explanation: since the disjunctive is exhaustive, one part is true; if the non-participating part (every A is T) is true, that is part of the result; if the participating part (every J is B) is true, then with the categorical (every B is D) we get (every J is D), the other part of the result. Thus the conclusion is a disjunction of the two. This lesson illustrates the combinatorial possibilities when mixing categorical and disjunctive premises.

Key Terms
الحملية
المنفصلة
القياس المقسم
القياس غير المقسم
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The divided syllogism uses a disjunctive that is preventive of emptiness, while the undivided does not.
B) The compositions in the divided syllogism all yield the same result; in the undivided they yield different results.
C) The divided syllogism requires the categoricals to be universal; the undivided does not.
D) The divided syllogism has exactly two disjuncts; the undivided has three.

Key Takeaway
The key to a valid compound syllogism with categoricals and a disjunctive is that the disjunctive is exhaustive (māni‘at al-khuluw) and the categorical propositions match each disjunct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Compound Syllogisms from Conjunctive and Disjunctive Conditionals
Author introduces the fifth division combining conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate a compound syllogism that combines a conjunctive conditional (muttaṣila) and an exclusive disjunctive conditional (māni‘at al-jam‘).

Core Idea
Combining conditionals yields new disjunctive conclusions only if the disjunction prevents conjunction.

This lesson introduces the fifth division of compound syllogisms, where a conjunctive and a disjunctive premise are combined. The 'natural' form places the conjunctive as the minor premise and the disjunctive as the major. We will walk through an example using the exclusive disjunction 'preventive of conjunction' and check the reasoning, even though the text is cut off mid-argument. Understanding this structure strengthens your ability to construct valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the conjunctive conditional ('كلما كان ا ب ف ج د' = 'Whenever A is B then J is D') and the disjunctive conditional ('دائماً إما كل ج د أو ه ز مانعة الجمع' = 'Always either every J is D or H is Z, preventive of conjunction'). Note that the disjunctive is exclusive: the two parts cannot be true together.
The conjunctive is the minor premise, the disjunctive the major — the 'natural' order specified by the author.
2
Determine the shared element
The term 'ج د' (J is D) appears in both premises: as the consequent of the conjunctive and as the first disjunct of the disjunctive. This is the common part that links the two premises.
The shared part can be a complete part of both premises or incomplete; here it is a complete clause.
3
Set up the conclusion
The conclusion is a new disjunction: 'دائماً إما ا ب أو ه ز مانعة الجمع' = 'Always either A is B or H is Z', also preventive of conjunction. This follows because if A implies J, and J excludes H (they cannot coexist), then A also excludes H.
The text is cut off (ends with 'امتناعه'), but the logical structure is clear.
4
Validate the inference
Assume 'A is B' is true. Then from the conjunctive, 'J is D' is true. But the disjunction says J is D and H is Z cannot both be true. Therefore, 'H is Z' cannot be true. So if A is B, then H is Z is false, meaning the disjunction holds: either A is B or H is Z (exclusive). The reasoning is valid.
This is a form of modus tollens through the disjunction. The full proof would complete the reasoning explicitly.
5
Formalize the argument
Write the argument in logical notation: Premise 1: A → J, Premise 2: J ⊕ H (exclusive or), Conclusion: A ⊕ H. This is valid by the rule that if A implies J and J excludes H, then A excludes H.
Formalization helps see the structure, but always refer back to the original text for authoritative wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحال: القسم الخامس ما يتركب من المتصلة والمنفصلة، والاشتراك إما في جزء تام من المقدمتين أو غير تام منهما - وكيفما كان – فالمطبوع منه ما تكون المتصلة صغرى والمنفصلة كبرى موجبة. مثال الأوّل قولنا: كلما كان (ا (ب) فرج د) ودائماً إما كل (ج د) أو (هـ ز) » مانعة الجمع، ينتج: دائماً إما أن يكون (اب) أو (هـ ز)» مانعة الجمع، لاستلزام امتناع الاجتماع مع اللازم - دائماً أو في الجملة - امتناعه
The state: The fifth division is what is composed of the conjunctive and the disjunctive, and the common part is either in a complete part of the two premises or an incomplete part of them – however it may be – and the natural [form] is that the conjunctive is minor and the disjunctive is major and affirmative. Example of the first: our saying: 'Whenever A is B then J is D, and always either every J is D or H is Z' as a preventive of conjunction, yields: 'Always either A is B or H is Z' as a preventive of conjunction, because the impossibility of conjunction with the necessary – always or sometimes – entails its impossibility.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth type of compound syllogism, which combines a conjunctive conditional (muttaṣila) and a disjunctive conditional (munfaṣila). The author notes that the shared element may be a complete part of both premises or an incomplete part, but the standard or 'natural' form (al-maṭbū‘) is that the conjunctive is the minor premise and the disjunctive is the major premise, and the major is affirmative. He gives an example: 'Whenever A is B then J is D' (conjunctive) and 'Always either every J is D or H is Z' (disjunctive, preventive of conjunction – i.e., exclusive disjunction). The conclusion is 'Always either A is B or H is Z', also exclusive. The reasoning is that if the conjunctive holds (A is B implies J is D), then the disjunctive's first part (every J is D) cannot be true simultaneously with H being Z, so the only remaining possibility is H is Z. But the text is cut off; the full reasoning would complete the explanation. This lesson is incomplete due to the page boundary.

Conditional Conjunctive Syllogism with Fewer Categoricals
Explains the second type of conditional conjunctive syllogism where categoricals are fewer than disjunct parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid conclusion for a conditional conjunctive syllogism when categorical premises are fewer than the disjunct parts, using an exclusive-of-vacuum disjunction.

Core Idea
When categoricals are fewer, the conclusion mirrors the disjunction's structure.

In Islamic logic, syllogisms often combine conditional and categorical premises. This lesson examines a special case where the disjunctive major premise has more parts than the categorical premises. The key is that the disjunction is 'exclusive-of-vacuum' (مانعة الخلو), ensuring at least one part is true. Let's break down how the conclusion follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise and its type
Find the disjunctive (منفصلة) in the passage. Note that it is 'exclusive-of-vacuum' (مانعة الخلو), meaning at least one part must be true. Also count its parts.
In the example, the disjunctive has two parts: 'not every A is' or 'every J is B'.
2
Count the categorical premises
Count how many categorical premises (حمليات) share a term with the disjunctive. Here there is only one categorical: 'every B is D'.
The categoricals are fewer than the disjunct parts (one categorical vs. two disjunct parts).
3
Determine which disjunct part shares with the categorical
Identify which part of the disjunction is linked to the categorical premise via a common term. In the example, 'every J is B' shares 'J' with the categorical's subject? Actually the categorical is 'every B is D', and the disjunct part is 'every J is B', so they share 'B'? Wait, the passage says 'our saying: Not every A is, or every J is B, and every B is D'. So the shared part is 'every J is B' since it shares the term B with the categorical.
The shared part is the one that connects with the categorical premise.
4
Apply the exclusive-of-vacuum rule
Since the disjunction is exclusive-of-vacuum, one of its two parts must be true. Consider two cases: either the non-shared part is true or the shared part is true. If the shared part is true, then together with the categorical premise, the other part of the conclusion follows.
The conclusion is a new disjunction combining the non-shared part and the result of the categorical with the shared part.
5
Write the conclusion
Combine the non-shared part with the result of the categorical composition. In the example, the non-shared part is 'not every A is', and the categorical composition yields 'every J is D' (since 'every J is B' and 'every B is D' gives 'every J is D'). So the conclusion is 'Either not every A is or every J is D'.
The conclusion retains the disjunctive form with two parts.

Source Text

الاقترانيات الكائنة من الشرطيات مع ما يشاركه من الحمليات وينتج النتيجة المطلوبة. وأما إذا كانت نتائج التأليفات مختلفة وهو القياس الغير المقسم، فلتكن المنفصلة مانعة الخلو كقولنا : «كلّ (ج) إمّا (ب) وإما (د) وإما (هـ) وكل (ب ج) وكلّ (د ط) وكلّ ( هـ (ز) » ينتج كلّ (ج) إمّا (ج) وإما (ط) وإما (ز) لمامر من وجوب صدق أحد أجزاء المنفصلة مع ما يشاركه من الحمليات. الثاني أن تكون الحمليّات أقل من أجزاء الانفصال، ولنفرض الحملية واحدة والمنفصلة ذات جزأين ومانعة الخلو ومشاركة الحملية مع أحدهما ، كقولنا : ماكل (اط) أو كلٌ (ج) ب) وكلّ (ب (د) » ينتج : : «إما كل (اط) أوكل (ج د) » لأنّ المنفصلة لما كانت مانعة الخلو وجب صدق أحد جزأيها ، فالواقع منهما إما الجزء الغير المشارك – وهو أحد جزأي النتيجة - أو الجزء المشارك، فيصدق مع الحمليّات - وهما مقدمتا التأليف - فيصدق نتيجة التأليف، وهي الجزء الآخر من النتيجة، فالواقع لا يخلو عن جزأيها.
The conjunctive syllogisms composed of conditional propositions with what shares with them from categoricals and produce the required conclusion. As for when the results of the compositions are different and it is the un-divided syllogism, let the disjunctive be of the type exclusive-of-vacuum, as our saying: 'Every J is either B or D or H, and every B is J, and every D is T, and every H is Z' yields 'Every J is either J or T or Z' due to the necessity of truth of one of the parts of the disjunctive with what shares with it from the categoricals. The second: that the categoricals are fewer than the parts of the disjunction. Let us suppose the categorical is one and the disjunctive has two parts and is exclusive-of-vacuum, and the categorical shares with one of them, as our saying: 'Not every A is, or every J is B, and every B is D' yields 'Either not every A is or every J is D' because since the disjunctive is exclusive-of-vacuum, one of its two parts must be true. So what occurs from them is either the non-shared part – which is one of the two parts of the conclusion – or the shared part. Then it is true with the categoricals – which are the two premises of the composition – so the result of the composition is true, which is the other part of the conclusion. Thus what occurs is not free from its two parts.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of conditional conjunctive syllogism (اقترانيات شرطية) when the categorical premises are fewer than the parts of the disjunctive major premise. The author first presents the general case: if the disjunctive is exclusive-of-vacuum (مانعة الخلو) and has three parts, and each part is associated with a categorical premise that shares a middle term, then the conclusion is another disjunctive with the same number of parts but with the middle term eliminated. Then the specific case: when there is only one categorical premise and a two-part disjunctive, the conclusion follows by noting that either the non-shared part is true (giving one part of the conclusion) or the shared part is true, which together with the categorical yields the other part. This mirrors the logic of Islamic jurisprudence where, for example, a ruling must be either obligatory, prohibited, or permissible; if evidence eliminates the prohibited, the remaining possibilities are obligatory or permissible. The author uses the terms 'مانعة الخلو' to emphasize that at least one part must be true, ensuring the conclusion is necessary.

Identifying the Fifth Type: Conditional + Disjunctive Syllogism
Defines the fifth type of conditional syllogism composed of a conditional and a disjunctive premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth type of conditional syllogism and its main subtypes based on the common element between premises.

Core Idea
A conditional and a disjunctive unite through a shared term to produce a new compound judgment.

We have covered four types of conditional syllogisms. The fifth type is the compound of a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila). In this lesson, we learn how to recognize this type, what forms the common element can take, and which arrangement is most natural.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the conditional premise (with 'if-then' structure) and the disjunctive premise (with 'either-or' structure) in the given passage or argument.
2
Determine the common element
Find the term or proposition that appears in both premises. The passage mentions three possibilities: a complete part of both, an incomplete part of both, or a complete part of one and incomplete of the other.
In the standard form, the common element is a complete part of both.
3
Classify the disjunctive type
Is the disjunctive exclusive-of-combination (مانعة الجمع, at most one true) or exclusive-of-vacuum (مانعة الخلو, at least one true)? This affects the conclusion form.
4
Check the natural arrangement
The passage states that the natural (مطبوع) arrangement is when the conditional is the minor premise and the disjunctive is the major premise, both affirmative.
This is the default for analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس من القياسات الشرطية. القسم الخامس ما يتركب من المتصلة والمنفصلة، والاشتراك إما في جزء تام من المقدمتين أو غير تام منهما - وكيفما كان – فالمطبوع منه ما تكون المتصلة صغرى والمنفصلة كبرى موجبة. مثال الأوّل قولنا: كلما كان (ا (ب) فرج د) ودائماً إما كل (ج د) أو (هـ ز) » مانعة الجمع، ينتج: دائماً إما أن يكون (اب) أو (هـ ز)» مانعة الجمع، لاستلزام امتناع الاجتماع مع اللازم - دائماً أو في الجملة - امتناعه مع الملزوم دائماً أو في الجملة. ومانعة الخلق ينتج: «قد يكون إذا لم يكن (ا ب فهـ (ز) لاستلزام نقيض الأوسط للطرفين استلزاماً كلياً، واستلزام ذلك المطلوب من الثالث. ومثال الثاني: كلما كان ا (ب) فرج (د) ودائماً إماكل (د هـ) أو (هـ ز)، مانعة الخلو ينتج: «كلما كان (اب) فإمـا كـل (ج هـ) أو (هـ ز)» والاستقصاء في هذه الأقسام إلى الرسائل التي عملناها في علم المنطق. القول: آخر أقسام الاقترانيات الشرطيّة ما يتركب من المتصلة والمنفصلة، والشركة بينهما إما في جزء تام منهما أو في جزء غيرتام منهما، أو في جزء تام من إحداهما غير تام من الأخرى. فهذه أقسام ثلاثة اقتصر المصنّف على القسمين الأولين، وكل منهما ينقسم إلى قسمين : لأن المتصلة فيهما إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، لكن المطبوع منهما ماتكون المتصلة صغرى والمنفصلة موجبة كبرى. أما الأول - و هو ما يكون الشركة في جزء تام من المقدمتين - فالمنفصلة إما مانعة الجمع أومانعة الخلو، فإن كانت مانعة الجمع كقولنا : كلما كان (ا) ب) فـ (ج) د ودائماً - أو قد يكون – إما (ج د) أو (هـ ز)» مانعة الجمع، ينتج : دائماً - أو قد يكون – إما (( ب) أو (هـ ز) » لأنّ (ج) د) لازم لـ(اب) و (هـ (ز) ممتنع الاجتماع مع (ج د)- كلياً كان أو جزئياً - فيكون ( هـ ) ممتنع الاجتماع مع (اب) كذلك، لأن امتناع الاجتماع مع اللازم دائماً أو في الجملة يستلزم امتناع الاجتماع مع الملزوم دائماً أو في الجملة.
The fifth division of conditional syllogisms. The fifth division is what is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive. The common element is either in a complete part of the two premises or an incomplete part of them – however it may be – and the natural [type] is where the conditional is the minor premise and the disjunctive is the major premise affirmative. Example of the first: our saying: 'Whenever (A is B) then (J is D) and always either every (J is D) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-combination, yields 'Always either (A is B) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-combination, because the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary – always or in general – necessitates the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary ground always or in general. And exclusive-of-vacuum yields: 'It may be that if (A is B) is not then (H is Z)' because the contradictory of the middle entails both extremes universally, and that entails the required from the third. And example of the second: 'Whenever (A is B) then (J is D) and always either every (D is H) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-vacuum yields: 'Whenever (A is B) then either every (J is H) or (H is Z)'. The thorough investigation of these divisions is [found] in the treatises we have composed on logic. The commentator says: The last of the divisions of conditional conjunctive syllogisms is what is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive. The commonality between them is either in a complete part of them or in an incomplete part of them, or in a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other. These are three divisions; the author confined himself to the first two divisions. Each of them divides into two: because the conditional in them is either minor or major, but the natural [type] is where the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is affirmative major. As for the first – which is where the commonality is in a complete part of the two premises – the disjunctive is either exclusive-of-combination or exclusive-of-vacuum. If it is exclusive-of-combination, as our saying: 'Whenever (A is B) then (J is D) and always – or it may be – either (J is D) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-combination, yields: 'Always – or it may be – either (A is B) or (H is Z)' because (J is D) is necessary for (A is B) and (H is Z) is impossible to coexist with (J is D) – universally or partially – so (H is Z) is impossible to coexist with (A is B) similarly, because the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary always or in general necessitates the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary ground always or in general.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the fifth type of conditional syllogism (القياسات الشرطية), which combines a conditional premise (متصلة) and a disjunctive premise (منفصلة). The author first states the general rule: the common element (الاشتراك) between the two premises may be a complete part of both, an incomplete part, or a complete part of one and incomplete of the other. The standard form is when the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is major. Two examples are given: first, with an exclusive-of-combination disjunctive (مانعة الجمع), the conclusion is also exclusive-of-combination; this follows because if the consequent (necessary from the conditional) cannot coexist with another term, then the antecedent also cannot coexist with that term. The second example uses exclusive-of-vacuum (مانعة الخلو) and yields a conditional with a disjunctive consequent. The commentator (القول) elaborates that there are three types of commonality; the author covered only the first two, each dividing into two sub-types based on whether the conditional is minor or major, with the minor being natural. The commentary then delves into the first type (commonality in a complete part) and explains how the reasoning works: if the consequent (J is D) is implied by the antecedent (A is B), and that consequent cannot coexist with another term (H is Z), then the antecedent also cannot coexist with that term. This mirrors arguments in fiqh, e.g., if performing wudu necessitates purity, and purity cannot coexist with impurity, then wudu cannot coexist with impurity. The author mentions that a full treatment is in his other logical treatises.

Key Terms
متصلة (muttasila): A conditional proposition with an 'if-then' structure (i.e., a hypothetical). [Note: Standard term; stable.]
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive proposition. Here subtypes: مانعة الجمع (exclusive-of-combination) and مانعة الخلو (exclusive-of-vacuum). [Note: In some later texts, the subtypes may be referred to as حقيقية and مانعة الخلو etc., but the meaning is consistent.]
مانعة الجمع (māni'at al-jam'): A disjunctive that prevents conjunction of its parts (at most one true). [Note: Also called حصرية (exclusive). Stable.]
مانعة الخلو (māni'at al-khuluw): A disjunctive that prevents both parts from being false (at least one true). [Note: Also called مجموعية (inclusive). Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Both premises disjunctive

Key Takeaway
The fifth type of conditional syllogism combines a conditional and a disjunctive, with the common element determining the sub-type.

The Syllogism

Reasoning with Exclusive-of-Combination Disjunctive in Compound Syllogisms
Defines the fifth type of conditional syllogism composed of a conditional and a disjunctive premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to validate a compound (conditional + exclusive disjunctive) syllogism using the principle of impossibility transfer.

Core Idea
If the consequent cannot coexist with a term, the antecedent cannot either – the key to exclusive disjunctive reasoning.

The passage demonstrates how an exclusive-of-combination disjunctive (مانعة الجمع) interacts with a conditional. The principle is straightforward: if the consequent of the conditional is incompatible with something, then the antecedent is also incompatible with that something. This lesson walks through the reasoning using the variables and examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Extract the conditional implication
Identify the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي) of the conditional premise. In the example: 'If (A is B) then (J is D)'.
2
State the disjunctive premise
Identify the disjunctive and its type. Here: 'Always either (J is D) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-combination, meaning they cannot both be true.
3
Note the incompatibility
Since the disjunctive is exclusive-of-combination, (J is D) and (H is Z) cannot coexist. This is the key relationship.
4
Apply the transfer principle
The passage states: 'Impossibility of coexistence with the necessary (i.e., the consequent) implies impossibility with its ground (i.e., the antecedent)'. Since (J is D) is necessary for (A is B), and (J is D) is incompatible with (H is Z), then (A is B) is also incompatible with (H is Z).
This principle works both universally and in general (جزئياً).
5
Form the conclusion
Therefore, the conclusion is an exclusive-of-combination disjunctive: 'Always either (A is B) or (H is Z)'.

القسم الخامس من القياسات الشرطية. القسم الخامس ما يتركب من المتصلة والمنفصلة، والاشتراك إما في جزء تام من المقدمتين أو غير تام منهما - وكيفما كان – فالمطبوع منه ما تكون المتصلة صغرى والمنفصلة كبرى موجبة. مثال الأوّل قولنا: كلما كان (ا (ب) فرج د) ودائماً إما كل (ج د) أو (هـ ز) » مانعة الجمع، ينتج: دائماً إما أن يكون (اب) أو (هـ ز)» مانعة الجمع، لاستلزام امتناع الاجتماع مع اللازم - دائماً أو في الجملة - امتناعه مع الملزوم دائماً أو في الجملة. ومانعة الخلق ينتج: «قد يكون إذا لم يكن (ا ب فهـ (ز) لاستلزام نقيض الأوسط للطرفين استلزاماً كلياً، واستلزام ذلك المطلوب من الثالث. ومثال الثاني: كلما كان ا (ب) فرج (د) ودائماً إماكل (د هـ) أو (هـ ز)، مانعة الخلو ينتج: «كلما كان (اب) فإمـا كـل (ج هـ) أو (هـ ز)» والاستقصاء في هذه الأقسام إلى الرسائل التي عملناها في علم المنطق. القول: آخر أقسام الاقترانيات الشرطيّة ما يتركب من المتصلة والمنفصلة، والشركة بينهما إما في جزء تام منهما أو في جزء غيرتام منهما، أو في جزء تام من إحداهما غير تام من الأخرى. فهذه أقسام ثلاثة اقتصر المصنّف على القسمين الأولين، وكل منهما ينقسم إلى قسمين : لأن المتصلة فيهما إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، لكن المطبوع منهما ماتكون المتصلة صغرى والمنفصلة موجبة كبرى. أما الأول - و هو ما يكون الشركة في جزء تام من المقدمتين - فالمنفصلة إما مانعة الجمع أومانعة الخلو، فإن كانت مانعة الجمع كقولنا : كلما كان (ا) ب) فـ (ج) د ودائماً - أو قد يكون – إما (ج د) أو (هـ ز)» مانعة الجمع، ينتج : دائماً - أو قد يكون – إما (( ب) أو (هـ ز) » لأنّ (ج) د) لازم لـ(اب) و (هـ (ز) ممتنع الاجتماع مع (ج د)- كلياً كان أو جزئياً - فيكون ( هـ ) ممتنع الاجتماع مع (اب) كذلك، لأن امتناع الاجتماع مع اللازم دائماً أو في الجملة يستلزم امتناع الاجتماع مع الملزوم دائماً أو في الجملة.
The fifth division of conditional syllogisms. The fifth division is what is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive. The common element is either in a complete part of the two premises or an incomplete part of them – however it may be – and the natural [type] is where the conditional is the minor premise and the disjunctive is the major premise affirmative. Example of the first: our saying: 'Whenever (A is B) then (J is D) and always either every (J is D) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-combination, yields 'Always either (A is B) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-combination, because the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary – always or in general – necessitates the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary ground always or in general. And exclusive-of-vacuum yields: 'It may be that if (A is B) is not then (H is Z)' because the contradictory of the middle entails both extremes universally, and that entails the required from the third. And example of the second: 'Whenever (A is B) then (J is D) and always either every (D is H) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-vacuum yields: 'Whenever (A is B) then either every (J is H) or (H is Z)'. The thorough investigation of these divisions is [found] in the treatises we have composed on logic. The commentator says: The last of the divisions of conditional conjunctive syllogisms is what is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive. The commonality between them is either in a complete part of them or in an incomplete part of them, or in a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other. These are three divisions; the author confined himself to the first two divisions. Each of them divides into two: because the conditional in them is either minor or major, but the natural [type] is where the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is affirmative major. As for the first – which is where the commonality is in a complete part of the two premises – the disjunctive is either exclusive-of-combination or exclusive-of-vacuum. If it is exclusive-of-combination, as our saying: 'Whenever (A is B) then (J is D) and always – or it may be – either (J is D) or (H is Z)' exclusive-of-combination, yields: 'Always – or it may be – either (A is B) or (H is Z)' because (J is D) is necessary for (A is B) and (H is Z) is impossible to coexist with (J is D) – universally or partially – so (H is Z) is impossible to coexist with (A is B) similarly, because the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary always or in general necessitates the impossibility of coexistence with the necessary ground always or in general.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the fifth type of conditional syllogism (القياسات الشرطية), which combines a conditional premise (متصلة) and a disjunctive premise (منفصلة). The author first states the general rule: the common element (الاشتراك) between the two premises may be a complete part of both, an incomplete part, or a complete part of one and incomplete of the other. The standard form is when the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is major. Two examples are given: first, with an exclusive-of-combination disjunctive (مانعة الجمع), the conclusion is also exclusive-of-combination; this follows because if the consequent (necessary from the conditional) cannot coexist with another term, then the antecedent also cannot coexist with that term. The second example uses exclusive-of-vacuum (مانعة الخلو) and yields a conditional with a disjunctive consequent. The commentator (القول) elaborates that there are three types of commonality; the author covered only the first two, each dividing into two sub-types based on whether the conditional is minor or major, with the minor being natural. The commentary then delves into the first type (commonality in a complete part) and explains how the reasoning works: if the consequent (J is D) is implied by the antecedent (A is B), and that consequent cannot coexist with another term (H is Z), then the antecedent also cannot coexist with that term. This mirrors arguments in fiqh, e.g., if performing wudu necessitates purity, and purity cannot coexist with impurity, then wudu cannot coexist with impurity. The author mentions that a full treatment is in his other logical treatises.

Key Terms
متصلة (muttasila): A conditional proposition with an 'if-then' structure (i.e., a hypothetical). [Note: Standard term; stable.]
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive proposition. Here subtypes: مانعة الجمع (exclusive-of-combination) and مانعة الخلو (exclusive-of-vacuum). [Note: In some later texts, the subtypes may be referred to as حقيقية and مانعة الخلو etc., but the meaning is consistent.]
مانعة الجمع (māni'at al-jam'): A disjunctive that prevents conjunction of its parts (at most one true). [Note: Also called حصرية (exclusive). Stable.]
مانعة الخلو (māni'at al-khuluw): A disjunctive that prevents both parts from being false (at least one true). [Note: Also called مجموعية (inclusive). Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What principle justifies the conclusion in the exclusive-of-combination case?
A) The contradiction of the middle term
B) Impossibility of coexistence with the necessary implies impossibility with its ground
C) The disjunctive must be exclusive
D) The conditional must be universal

Key Takeaway
In a compound syllogism with an exclusive disjunctive, incompatibility with the consequent transfers to the antecedent due to the necessity relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a valid compound syllogism using a conditional and an exclusive-of-combination disjunctive from a real fiqh example. For instance: 'If water is pure (طاهر), then it can be used for wudu. And either it can be used for wudu or it is impure (نجس). (Exclusive) Therefore, either water is pure or it is impure.' Verify that the conclusion follows by the transfer principle.

First Division of Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Explains the first division of compound conditionals with a complete shared part and its two subcases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish exclusive and inclusive disjunctions in compound conditional syllogisms and derive the conclusion for each type.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals combine a conditional and a disjunctive; the disjunctive type determines the conclusion.

This passage from the commentary on al-Shamsiyya explains the first division of compound conditional syllogisms, where the shared part is complete. The compound consists of a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila). The disjunctive can be exclusive (man'at al-jam') or inclusive (man'at al-khuluww), leading to different conclusions. We will walk through both cases using the logical variables provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the conditional premise (e.g., 'Whenever A is B, then C is D') and the disjunctive premise (e.g., 'Either C is D or H is Z, exclusively or inclusively'). Note that the shared part is the entire consequent 'C is D'.
2
Determine the type of disjunction
Check if the disjunctive is exclusive (man'at al-jam') — the two disjuncts cannot both be true — or inclusive (man'at al-khuluww) — they cannot both be false.
Exclusive corresponds to XOR; inclusive corresponds to OR.
3
Apply the rule for exclusive disjunction
If exclusive, the conclusion is that the antecedent of the conditional (A is B) is incompatible with the other disjunct (H is Z). Thus, 'Always (or sometimes) either A is B or H is Z'. This follows because C is D is necessary for A is B, and H is Z is incompatible with C is D, so H is Z is also incompatible with A is B.
Incompatibility transfers from the consequent to the antecedent.
4
Apply the rule for inclusive disjunction
If inclusive, the negation of the shared part (not C is D) entails both the negation of the antecedent (not A is B) and the affirmation of the other disjunct (H is Z). From the third figure, we get 'Sometimes if not A is B then H is Z'.
This uses the principle that the negation of an inclusive disjunct implies the other.
5
Verify with Islamic examples
Substitute Islamic content for the variables. For example, 'Whenever prayer is valid (A is B), purification is required (C is D)' and 'Either purification is required (C is D) or intention is not required (H is Z)' exclusive → 'Always either prayer is valid or intention is not required'.
Ensure the example respects the logical form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول: آخر أقسام الاقترانيات الشرطيّة ما يتركب من المتصلة والمنفصلة، والشركة بينهما إما في جزء تام منهما أو في جزء غيرتام منهما، أو في جزء تام من إحداهما غير تام من الأخرى. فهذه أقسام ثلاثة اقتصر المصنّف على القسمين الأولين، وكل منهما ينقسم إلى قسمين : لأن المتصلة فيهما إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى، لكن المطبوع منهما ماتكون المتصلة صغرى والمنفصلة موجبة كبرى. أما الأول - و هو ما يكون الشركة في جزء تام من المقدمتين - فالمنفصلة إما مانعة الجمع أومانعة الخلو، فإن كانت مانعة الجمع كقولنا : كلما كان (ا) ب) فـ (ج) د ودائماً - أو قد يكون – إما (ج د) أو (هـ ز)» مانعة الجمع، ينتج : دائماً - أو قد يكون - إما (( ب) أو (هـ ز) » لأنّ (ج) د) لازم لـ(اب) و (هـ (ز) ممتنع الاجتماع مع (ج د)- كلياً كان أو جزئياً - فيكون ( هـ ) ممتنع الاجتماع مع (اب) كذلك، لأن امتناع الاجتماع مع اللازم دائماً أو في الجملة يستلزم امتناع الاجتماع مع الملزوم دائماً أو في الجملة. وإن كانت مانعة الخلو - كما في المثال المذكور، والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو - ينتج : «قد يكون إذا لم يكن (اب) فرهـ (ز) لأن نقيض الأوسط - وهو نقيض (ج د) - يستلزم طرفي النتيجة، أعني نقيض (اب)؛ وعين (هـ (ز) أما أنه يستلزم نقيض (اب) فلأن نقيض اللازم يستلزم نقيض الملزوم ، وأما أنه يستلزم عين (هـ ز) فلمنع الخلوبين (ج د) و (هـ ز)، وكلّ (ن : فكل أمرين بينهما منع الخلو يستلزم نقيض كل واحد منهما عين الآخر - على ما مر في تلازم الشرطيات - وإذا استلزم نقيض الأوسط للطرفين أنتج من الشكل الثالث أن نقيض (ا) (ب) قد يستلزم عين (هـ ز) وهو المطلوب.
Statement: The last division of conditional conjunctive [syllogisms] is what is composed of a conditional and a disjunctive. The commonality between them is either in a complete part of both, or in an incomplete part of both, or in a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other. These are three divisions; the author confined himself to the first two, and each of them divides into two: because the conditional in them is either minor or major, but the common one is that the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is affirmative major. As for the first—which is when the commonality is in a complete part of the two premises—the disjunctive is either exclusive (man'at al-jam') or inclusive (man'at al-khuluww). If it is exclusive, as we say: 'Whenever A is B then C is D, and always—or sometimes—[it is] either C is D or H is Z' with exclusive disjunction, it yields: 'Always—or sometimes—either A is B or H is Z'. Because C is D is a necessary consequent of A is B, and H is Z is incompatible with C is D—universally or partially—so H is Z is likewise incompatible with A is B, because incompatibility with the necessary consequent always or sometimes entails incompatibility with the necessary antecedent always or sometimes. If it is inclusive—as in the mentioned example, with the disjunctive inclusive—it yields: 'Sometimes, if not A is B then H is Z'. Because the negation of the middle—which is the negation of C is D—entails both sides of the conclusion, namely the negation of A is B and the affirmation of H is Z. As for entailing the negation of A is B, it is because the negation of the consequent entails the negation of the antecedent. As for entailing the affirmation of H is Z, it is due to the non-exclusion between C is D and H is Z, and for any two things between which there is non-exclusion (inclusive), the negation of each entails the affirmation of the other—as passed in the implication of conditionals. And when the negation of the middle entails both sides, it produces from the third figure that the negation of A is B may entail the affirmation of H is Z, which is the desired [conclusion].
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Shamsiyya explains the first division of compound conditional syllogisms, where the shared part is complete. The author begins by stating that the last division of conditional conjunctive syllogisms is composed of a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila). The sharing between them can be a complete part, an incomplete part, or a mixed case. Only the first two are treated. Each of these two divides into two based on whether the conditional is minor or major; typically the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is major. For the first type (complete share), the disjunctive is either exclusive (man'at al-jam') or inclusive (man'at al-khuluww). The author provides examples using variables: for exclusive, the conclusion is that whenever A is B, then either A is B or H is Z (since C is D is necessary for A is B and incompatible with H is Z). For inclusive, the conclusion is that sometimes if A is not B, then H is Z, because the negation of the middle entails both the negation of the antecedent and the affirmation of the other disjunct. An Islamic example for the exclusive case: if we say 'Whenever prayer is valid, purification is required' (conditional) and 'Either purification is required or intention is not required' (exclusive disjunctive), the conclusion is 'Whenever prayer is valid, either prayer is valid or intention is not required' (which is logically trivial but valid). For the inclusive case: 'Whenever prayer is valid, purification is required' and 'Either purification is required or intention is required' (inclusive), the conclusion is 'Sometimes if prayer is not valid, then intention is required'. The explanation relies on the principles of necessary consequence and incompatibility.

Key Terms
متصلة
منفصلة
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
جزء تام

A) Always or sometimes, either A is B or H is Z.
B) Sometimes, if not A is B then H is Z.
C) Always, both A is B and H is Z.
D) Sometimes, if A is B then not H is Z.

Key Takeaway
The type of disjunction (exclusive vs inclusive) in a compound conditional syllogism determines whether the conclusion is a simple disjunction or a conditional derived from the negation of the shared part.

Compound Conditionals with Incomplete Shared Part
Explains the second division of compound conditionals with an incomplete shared part and its rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a compound conditional syllogism where the shared part is incomplete.

Core Idea
An incomplete shared part yields a conditional conclusion with a disjunctive consequent.

In the second division of compound conditionals, the two premises share only an incomplete part (e.g., a predicate). The author shows how to combine a conditional with an inclusive disjunctive to produce a valid conclusion. This lesson unpacks the derivation step by step, using the same variable example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the conditional premise (qadiyya shartiyya) and the disjunctive premise (qadiyya munfasila). In the example, the conditional is 'Whenever A is B, then all C is D' and the disjunctive is 'Always either all D is H or H is Z'.
The disjunctive is inclusive (man'at al-khuluw), meaning at least one disjunct is true.
2
Spot the shared part
The shared part is 'D' (the subject of the disjunctive) which appears only partially in the conditional: it is the predicate of the conditional's consequent. Thus the sharing is incomplete (juz' ghayr tamm).
3
Apply the derivation
Assume the antecedent of the conditional (A is B). Then the consequent (all C is D) holds. Now consider the two cases from the disjunctive: (i) all D is H, or (ii) H is Z. Case (i): from 'all C is D' and 'all D is H' we get 'all C is H' via transitivity. Case (ii): H is Z directly gives the second disjunct. Thus on the assumption, we have 'either all C is H or H is Z'.
This is a proof by cases.
4
Formulate the conclusion
Since the assumption leads to that disjunction, the conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent: 'Whenever A is B, then either all C is H or H is Z'.
The antecedent remains unchanged; only the consequent becomes a disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني - وهو ما يكون الشركة في جزء غير تام - من المقدمتين ولتكن المنفصلة مانعة الخلو، فكقولنا : «كلما كان (اب) فكل (ج د) ودائماً إما كل (د) هـ) أو (هـ ز » ينتج كلّما كان (اب) فإما كل (ج هـ) ز) أو ( هـ ز ) » لأنه كلما فرض (اب) كان (ج) د) فالواقع حينئذ من المنفصلة إماكل (دهـ) أو (هـ (ز) فإن كان ( دهـ) فالواقع على تقدير (اب) كل (ج د) وكلّ (دهـ) وهما يستلزمان كلّ (ج) هـ)، وإن كان (هـ ز) فعلى تقدير (اب) يكون الواقع إما كلّ ( ج هـ) أو (هـ ز) وهو المطلوب. هذا كلام إجمالي في الاقترانيات الشرطية. وأما بيان تفاصيلها فهو تما لا يليق بالمختصرات.
As for the second—which is when the commonality is in an incomplete part—of the two premises, and let the disjunctive be inclusive (man'at al-khuluww). So like our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then all C is D, and always either all D is H or H is Z' yields: 'Whenever A is B, then either all C is H or H is Z'. Because whenever A is B is assumed, C is D is the case, so what occurs then from the disjunctive is either all D is H or H is Z. If it is all D is H, then on the assumption of A is B, [we have] all C is D and all D is H, and they both entail all C is H. And if it is H is Z, then on the assumption of A is B, what occurs is either all C is H or H is Z, which is the desired [conclusion]. This is a summary account of conditional conjunctive [syllogisms]. As for the explanation of its details, it is a complete [subject] that is not suitable for compendia.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage covers the second division of compound conditionals, where the shared part is incomplete (juz' ghayr tamm). The author specifies that the disjunctive is inclusive (man'at al-khuluww). The example uses conditionals with universal quantification: 'Whenever A is B, then all C is D' and 'Always either all D is H or H is Z'. The conclusion is 'Whenever A is B, then either all C is H or H is Z'. The proof: when A is B holds, C is D holds. Then from the disjunctive, either all D is H or H is Z. If all D is H, then from all C is D and all D is H, we get all C is H by transitivity. If H is Z, then the conclusion 'either all C is H or H is Z' is satisfied by the second disjunct. Thus the conclusion follows. An Islamic example: suppose 'Whenever a prayer is valid, all purification (wudu) is required' and 'Always either all required purification is cleanliness or intention is valid'. Then the conclusion: 'Whenever prayer is valid, then either all purification is cleanliness or intention is valid'. The passage ends with a note that this is a summary; detailed explanations belong to larger works.

جزء غير تام
مانعة الخلو

Key Takeaway
When the shared part between a conditional and an inclusive disjunctive is incomplete, the conclusion is a conditional whose consequent is a disjunction of the two possible outcomes.

Conjunctive Syllogisms from Conditional and Disjunctive Premises
Explains rules for conjunctive syllogisms built from conditional and disjunctive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a conjunctive syllogism formed from a disjunctive premise that is exclusive of emptiness and trace how the contradictory of the middle term entails both sides of the conclusion.

Core Idea
In conjunctive syllogisms from conditionals, the contradictory of the middle term links both sides of the conclusion when the disjunction is exclusive of emptiness.

We have studied categorical syllogisms and their figures. Now we examine a more complex type: conjunctive syllogisms where the premises are conditional or disjunctive. This passage from the logical tradition explains how such syllogisms can be reduced to categorical form using the third figure, provided the disjunction is exclusive of emptiness. Understanding these reductions sharpens your ability to handle compound premises in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Determine whether the disjunctive premise is exclusive of emptiness (māni‘at al-khuluw) or exclusive of both. In this passage, it is the former: the two disjuncts cannot both be false together.
2
Locate the middle term and its contradictory
The middle term is the proposition (c-d). Its contradictory is the negation of (c-d). This contradictory is used to bridge the two premises.
3
Trace the entailments of the contradictory
The contradictory of (c-d) entails two things: (1) the contradictory of (ab) – because the contrapositive of the conditional 'if (ab) then (c-d)' gives 'if not (c-d) then not (ab)'; (2) the very (h-z) – because the exclusivity of emptiness between (c-d) and (h-z) means that if one is false, the other must be true.
This step uses the principle that the contradictory of the necessary entails the contradictory of the necessitated.
4
Apply the third figure of categorical syllogism
Since the contradictory of the middle term entails both the contradictory of (ab) and (h-z), we have two premises with the same subject (the contradictory of (c-d)). This matches the third figure, allowing us to conclude that the contradictory of (ab) may entail (h-z).
The third figure requires the middle term to be subject in both premises, which holds here.
5
Handle the second case with an incomplete common part
When the common part between the premises is incomplete (i.e., only part of the middle term is shared), as in the example 'Whenever (ab) is, then every (c-d); and always either every (d-h) or (h-z)', the conclusion becomes a conditional with a disjunctive consequent: 'Whenever (ab) is, then either every (c-h-z) or (h-z).' The derivation uses case analysis on the disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقترانيات الكائنة من الشرطيات وإن كانت مانعة الخلو - كما في المثال المذكور، والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو - ينتج : «قد يكون إذا لم يكن (اب) فرهـ (ز) لأن نقيض الأوسط - وهو نقيض (ج د) - يستلزم طرفي النتيجة، أعني نقيض (اب)؛ وعين (هـ (ز) أما أنه يستلزم نقيض (اب) فلأن نقيض اللازم يستلزم نقيض الملزوم ، وأما أنه يستلزم عين (هـ ز) فلمنع الخلوبين (ج د) و (هـ ز)، وكلّ (ن : فكل أمرين بينهما منع الخلو يستلزم نقيض كل واحد منهما عين الآخر - على ما مر في تلازم الشرطيات - وإذا استلزم نقيض الأوسط للطرفين أنتج من الشكل الثالث أن نقيض (ا) (ب) قد يستلزم عين (هـ ز) وهو المطلوب. وأما الثاني - وهو ما يكون الشركة في جزء غير تام - من المقدمتين ولتكن المنفصلة مانعة الخلو، فكقولنا : «كلما كان (اب) فكل (ج د) ودائماً إما كل (د) هـ) أو (هـ ز » ينتج كلّما كان (اب) فإما كل (ج هـ) ز) أو ( هـ ز ) » لأنه كلما فرض (اب) كان (ج) د) فالواقع حينئذ من المنفصلة إماكل (دهـ) أو (هـ (ز) فإن كان ( دهـ) فالواقع على تقدير (اب) كل (ج د) وكلّ (دهـ) وهما يستلزمان كلّ (ج) هـ)، وإن كان (هـ ز) فعلى تقدير (اب) يكون الواقع إما كلّ ( ج هـ) أو (هـ ز) وهو المطلوب. هذا كلام إجمالي في الاقترانيات الشرطية. وأما بيان تفاصيلها فهو تما لا يليق بالمختصرات.
Conjunctive syllogisms composed of conditionals. If the disjunctive is exclusive of emptiness—as in the mentioned example, and the disjunctive is exclusive of emptiness—then the conclusion is: 'It may be that if (ab) is not, then (h-z) is the case' because the contradictory of the middle term—which is the contradictory of (c-d)—entails the two parts of the conclusion, namely the contradictory of (ab) and the very (h-z). As for its entailing the contradictory of (ab), that is because the contradictory of the necessary entails the contradictory of the necessitated. As for its entailing the very (h-z), that is because of the exclusivity of emptiness between (c-d) and (h-z). And every two things between which there is exclusivity of emptiness, the contradictory of each one entails the very other—as has preceded in the entailment of conditionals. And if the contradictory of the middle term entails both parts, then from the third figure it follows that the contradictory of (a)(b) may entail the very (h-z), which is what was sought. As for the second—which is when the commonality is in a part that is incomplete—from the two premises, taking the disjunctive as exclusive of emptiness, such as our saying: 'Whenever (ab) is, then every (c-d) is; and always either every (d-h) or (h-z) is'—the conclusion is: 'Whenever (ab) is, then either every (c-h-z) or (h-z) is.' Because whenever (ab) is assumed, (c-d) is; so what occurs at that time from the disjunctive is either every (d-h) or (h-z). If it is (d-h), then what occurs on the assumption (ab) is every (c-d) and every (d-h), and both entail every (c-h). And if it is (h-z), then on the assumption (ab) what occurs is either every (c-h) or (h-z), which is what was sought. This is a summary discourse on conditional conjunctive syllogisms. As for the elaboration of their details, that is not suitable for compendiums.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a technical explanation of how conjunctive syllogisms (al-iqtirāniyyāt) can be formed from conditional premises, specifically disjunctive ones that are exclusive of emptiness (māni‘at al-khuluw). The author first considers the case where the disjunction is exclusive of emptiness and shows that the contradictory of the middle term yields both sides of the conclusion. This is demonstrated using letter variables: (ab), (c-d), (h-z) as placeholders for propositions. The argument follows the pattern of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. Then the author considers a second case where the common part between the two premises is incomplete, and gives an example in which a categorical conditional premise is combined with a disjunctive premise to produce a conclusion that is also a conditional with a disjunctive consequent. The passage ends with the remark that these are just summary points, and the detailed exposition is beyond the scope of concise works.
