The Exceptional Syllogism: Definition and Valid Forms
Defines the exceptional syllogism and its valid forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of an exceptional syllogism and construct valid modus ponens and modus tollens arguments using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
An exceptional syllogism uses a conditional premise and a 'positional' premise to affirm or deny a part.

The exceptional syllogism is a fundamental tool in Islamic logic, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam. In this lesson, we examine its definition and the two valid inference patterns: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). The passage from Qutbi lays out the structure with conditions for validity. We will break down these conditions and apply them to arguments from Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Look for one premise that is a conditional sentence (e.g., 'If P then Q') and another that either affirms or denies one part of that conditional. The passage calls the second premise 'al-muqaddima al-waḍ‘iyya' (the positional premise).
2
Classify the conditional type
Determine if the conditional is conjunctive (luzūmiyya muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). The passage requires that the conjunctive be necessary (luzūmiyya) and the disjunctive be oppositional (‘unādiyya). For this lesson, focus on the conjunctive type.
3
Check the universality condition
The passage states that either the conditional must be universal, or the affirmation/negation in the positional premise must be universal, unless the time of connection/disjunction is exactly the time of the positional act. This ensures the conclusion follows necessarily.
4
Apply the two valid forms
Using a concrete Islamic example, construct modus ponens (affirm antecedent to conclude consequent) and modus tollens (deny consequent to conclude denial of antecedent). For instance: 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a creator; the world is temporally originated; therefore it has a creator' (modus ponens).
5
Verify the conclusion follows
Check that the conclusion is not already present as a premise (circular) and that the conditions of necessity and universality are satisfied. The passage explicitly rules out the conclusion being a premise itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: الفضيل الرابع في القياس الاستثنائي وهو مركب من مقدمتين: إحداهما شرطية والأخرى وضع لأحد جزايهـا أو رفعه ليلزم وضع الآخر أو رفعه. ويجب إيجاب الشرطية ولزومية المتصلة وعناديّة المنفصلة وكليّتها أو كلية الوضع أو الرفع إن لم يكن وقت الاتصال والانفصال هو بعينه وقت الوضع والرفع. القول: قدمر أن القياس الاستثنائي ما يكون عين النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل، فالمذكور فيه من النتيجة أو نقيضها إما مقدمة من مقدماته - وهو محال، وإلا لزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه أو بنقيضه أو جزء من مقدمتيه - والمقدّمة التي جزؤها قضية تكون شرطية والأخرى وضعية؛ فالقياس الاستثنائي ما يكون مركباً من مقدمتين : إحداهما شرطية والأخرى وضعيّة - أي إثبات لأحد جزأيها أو رفعه – أي نفيه – ليلزم وضع الجزء الآخر أو رفعه. كقولنا : كلّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة» ينتج «أن النهار موجود». ولكن النهار ليس بموجود» ينتج الشمس ليست بطالعة.
He said: The fourth chapter on the exceptional syllogism. It is composed of two premises: one of them is conditional and the other is an affirmation of one of its parts or a negation of it, such that the affirmation or negation of the other part follows. It is necessary that the conditional be affirmative and that the conjunctive be necessary and the disjunctive be oppositional, and that it be universal, or that the affirmation or negation be universal if the time of the connection and disjunction is not exactly the time of the affirmation and negation. The statement: It has preceded that the exceptional syllogism is that in which the very conclusion or its contradictory is actually mentioned. So what is mentioned of the conclusion or its contradictory is either a premise of its premises—which is impossible, otherwise it would entail proving a thing by itself or by its contradictory—or a part of its two premises. And the premise of which a part is a proposition is conditional, and the other is affirmative. So the exceptional syllogism is that which is composed of two premises: one of them conditional and the other affirmative—i.e., an affirmation of one of its parts or a negation of it—such that the affirmation of the other part or its negation follows. Like our saying: 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists; but the sun is shining' yields 'daytime exists'. And 'daytime does not exist' yields 'the sun is not shining'.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptional syllogism (qiyās istithnā’ī). It opens by stating that it is composed of two premises: one conditional (sharṭiyya) and the other 'affirmative' (waḍ‘iyya), which means either affirming or negating one part of the conditional. From this, the affirmation or negation of the other part follows. The author then introduces conditions: the conditional must be affirmative and necessary (luzūmiyya) for conjunctives, and oppositional (‘unādiyya) for disjunctives. Additionally, either the conditional must be universal or the affirmation/negation must be universal, unless the time of connection/disjunction is exactly the time of affirmation/negation. The explanation (al-qawl) recalls that in an exceptional syllogism, the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in the premises. It cannot be a premise itself, as that would be circular, so it must be part of the premises. Thus, the syllogism has one conditional premise and one 'affirmative' premise that posits or removes a part, leading to the conclusion. Two examples from daily life are given: affirming the antecedent of a conditional to conclude the consequent, and denying the consequent to conclude the denial of the antecedent.

المقدمة الوضعية
لزومية المتصلة

An exceptional syllogism uses a conditional premise and a positional premise to yield a conclusion that is a part of the conditional, and it must satisfy the conditions of necessity and universality.

Structure of the Conditional Syllogism
Defines conditional syllogism and gives examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two premises of a conditional syllogism and determine whether the inference is modus ponens, modus tollens, or the disjunctive pattern.

Core Idea
The conditional syllogism explicitly states its conclusion in one premise.

In the previous lesson, we studied categorical syllogisms where the conclusion is implicit. Now we turn to the conditional syllogism, where the conclusion or its contradictory is directly mentioned in a premise. This lesson defines its structure and demonstrates its two forms: the connective (if-then) and the disjunctive (either-or).

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Look for the conditional statement (shartiyya) which is either connective ('if...then') or disjunctive ('either...or'). This premise sets up the logical relationship.
The conditional must be affirmative and essential for validity, but we check that later.
2
Identify the positional premise
Find the premise that affirms or denies one part of the conditional. This is called the wad'iyya (positional premise). It is the 'exceptional' premise that triggers the inference.
3
Determine the inference pattern
For connective conditionals: affirming antecedent yields consequent (modus ponens); denying consequent yields denying antecedent (modus tollens). For disjunctive conditionals: affirming one part yields denial of the other; denying one part yields affirmation of the other.
Memorise the patterns: in connectives, 'positive positive' and 'negative negative'; in disjunctives, 'positive negative' and vice versa.
4
Apply to the sun/day example
The conditional 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' is connective. The positional premise 'the sun is rising' affirms the antecedent, so we conclude 'daytime exists' (modus ponens). If the positional premise denies the consequent ('daytime does not exist'), we conclude 'the sun is not rising' (modus tollens).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في القياس الاستثنائي
وهو مركب من مقدمتين: إحداهما شرطية والأخرى وضع لأحد جزأيها أو رفعه ليلزم وضع الآخر أو رفعه.
ويجب إيجاب الشرطية ولزومية المتصلة وعناديّة المنفصلة وكليّتها أو كلية الوضع أو الرفع إن لم يكن وقت الاتصال والانفصال هو بعينه وقت الوضع والرفع.
القول: قد مر أن القياس الاستثنائي ما يكون عين النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل، فالمذكور فيه من النتيجة أو نقيضها إما مقدمة من مقدماته - وهو محال، وإلا لزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه أو بنقيضه أو جزء من مقدمتيه - والمقدّمة التي جزؤها قضية تكون شرطية والأخرى وضعية؛ فالقياس الاستثنائي ما يكون مركباً من مقدمتين : إحداهما شرطية والأخرى وضعيّة - أي إثبات لأحد جزأيها أو رفعه – أي نفيه – ليلزم وضع الجزء الآخر أو رفعه.
كقولنا : «كلّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة» ينتج «أن النهار موجود». ولكنه «النهار ليس بموجود» ينتج الشمس ليست بطالعة.
وكقولنا : «دائما إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً، لكن هذا العدد زوج» ينتج «أنه ليس بفرد». ولكنه ليس بزوج» ينتج «أنه فرد».
ففي المتصلات ينتج الوضع الوضع والرفع الرفع، وفي المنفصلات ينتج الوضع الرفع وبالعكس.
شرائط الانتاج في القياسات الاستثنائية:
ويعتبر في إنتاج هذا القياس شرائط :
أحدها أن تكون الشرطية موجبة، فإنّها لو كانت سالبة لم تنتج شيئاً لا الوضع ولا الرفع - فإن معنى الشرطية السالبة سلب اللزوم والعناد، وإذا لم يكن بين الأمرين لزوم أو عناد لم يلزم من وجود أحدهما أو عدمه وجود الآخر أو عدمه.
وثانيها : أن تكون الشرطيّة لزومية - إن كانت متصلة - وعنادية - إن كانت منفصلة - لا اتفاقيّة، لأنّ العلم بصدق الاتفاقية أو كذبها موقوف على العلم بصدق أحد طرفيها أو كذبه ، فلو استفيد العلم بصدق أحد الطرفين أو كذبه من الاتفاقية يلزم الدور.
وثالثها أحد الأمرين : وهو إما كلّية الشرطية أو كلية الاستثناء - أي كلية الوضع أو الرفع - فإنّه لو انتفى الأمران احتمل أن يكون اللزوم أو العناد على بعض الأوضاع والاستثناء على وضع آخر، فلا يلزم من إثبات أحد جزئي الشرطية أو نفيه ثبوت الآخر أو انتفاؤه. اللهم إلا إذا كان وقت الاتصال والانفصال ووضعها هو بعينه وقت الاستثناء ووضعه، فإنه ينتج.
The fourth chapter on the conditional syllogism. It is composed of two premises: one of them is conditional and the other is an affirmation of one of its two parts or a negation of it, in order to necessitate the affirmation of the other part or its negation. It is necessary that the conditional be affirmative, and if it is connective it must be essential, and if disjunctive it must be exclusive, and that it be universal, or that the affirmation or negation be universal, unless the time of connection or disconnection is exactly the time of the affirmation or negation. Statement: It has already passed that the conditional syllogism is that in which the very conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned explicitly. So what is mentioned of the conclusion or its contradictory is either a premise of its premises — which is impossible, otherwise it would entail proving a thing by itself or by its contradictory or by a part of its two premises — and the premise whose part is a proposition is conditional, and the other is positional. So the conditional syllogism is composed of two premises: one conditional and the other positional — i.e., the affirmation of one of its two parts or its negation — i.e., its denial — in order to necessitate the affirmation of the other part or its negation. Like our statement: 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but the sun is rising' yields 'that daytime exists.' And 'but daytime does not exist' yields 'that the sun is not rising.' And like our statement: 'Always, either this number is even or odd; but this number is even' yields 'that it is not odd.' And 'but it is not even' yields 'that it is odd.' So in connectives, affirming the antecedent affirms the consequent and denying the antecedent denies the consequent, and in disjunctives, affirming one part denies the other and vice versa. Conditions for production in conditional syllogisms: In the production of this syllogism, conditions are considered: First, that the conditional be affirmative; for if it were negative, it would not produce anything, neither affirmation nor negation — for the meaning of a negative conditional is the negation of implicative relation or incompatibility, and if there is no implication or incompatibility between the two matters, it does not follow from the existence of one or its non-existence the existence of the other or its non-existence. Second, that the conditional be implicative if it is connective, and exclusive if it is disjunctive, not accidental; because knowledge of the truth or falsity of the accidental conditional depends on knowledge of the truth of one of its two parts or its falsity; so if knowledge of the truth of one of the two parts or its falsity were derived from the accidental conditional, circularity would ensue. Third, one of two things: either the universality of the conditional or the universality of the exception — i.e., the universality of the affirmation or negation — for if both are absent, it is possible that the implication or incompatibility holds for some states and the exception for another state; so the affirmation of one of the two parts of the conditional or its negation does not entail the affirmation of the other or its negation. Unless the time of connection or disconnection and its state is exactly the time of the exception and its state, then it produces.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i) and lists its necessary conditions for valid inference. The author first distinguishes it from the categorical syllogism: here the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated in one of the premises. That premise is called the 'positional' premise (al-muqaddima al-wad'iyya), which affirms or denies one part of the conditional. The other premise is the conditional itself. The argument proceeds: if the conditional connects A and B (e.g., 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists'), then affirming A yields B, and denying B yields not-A. The author gives examples using the sun/day and even/odd number, which are standard logical examples. For disjunctives (e.g., 'Either the number is even or odd'), affirming one part necessitates negating the other, and negating one necessitates affirming the other. The author then lists three conditions for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion: (1) The conditional must be affirmative; a negative conditional denies any implication or incompatibility, so no inference is possible. (2) The conditional must be essential (lizumiyya) for connectives and exclusive ('inadiyya) for disjunctives, not accidental (ittifaqiyya); otherwise, we would need prior knowledge of one part, leading to circularity (dawr). (3) Either the conditional itself must be universal, or the exception (the positional premise) must be universal; otherwise, the implication might hold only for some cases while the exception applies to a different case, breaking the inference. An exception is noted: if the time or state of the connection/disconnection is exactly the same as the time/state of the exception, then a particular conditional can still yield a conclusion. This precision is crucial for applying conditional syllogisms in legal reasoning (e.g., conditional contracts or obligations).

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
شرطية
وضعية
لزومية
عنادية
اتفاقية

A) Daytime does not exist.
B) The sun is not rising.
C) Daytime exists.
D) No conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The conditional syllogism is built from a conditional premise and a positional premise; the connection must be necessary for a valid inference.

The Syllogism

Three Conditions for Valid Conditional Syllogisms
Enumerates three conditions for conditional syllogism validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to examine any conditional syllogism and determine whether it satisfies the conditions of affirmation, essential connection, and universality (or temporal coincidence).

Core Idea
A valid conditional syllogism requires the conditional to be affirmative, essential, and universal (or the exception to be universal and simultaneous).

Not every conditional argument yields a valid conclusion. The master logician lays down three conditions that must hold for the syllogism to produce certainty. These conditions prevent fallacies such as circularity and scope mismatches. In this lesson, we unpack each condition and apply them to examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Check if the conditional is affirmative
The conditional premise must be positive (mujiba). A negative conditional (saliba) denies any necessary relation between the parts, so no inference can be drawn from affirming or denying one part.
Example: 'If the sun rises, then daytime does not exist' is negative; from 'sun rises' we cannot conclude about daytime.
2
Check if the conditional is essential, not accidental
For connective conditionals, it must be lizumiyya (essential implication); for disjunctive, it must be 'inadiyya (true exclusion). Accidental conditionals (ittifaqiyya) are invalid because they lead to circular reasoning: knowledge of the truth of the conditional depends on knowledge of one part, which we are trying to derive from the conditional.
Example: 'If Zaid enters, Amr enters' (accidental) cannot be used to conclude anything.
3
Check for universality or temporal coincidence
Either the conditional itself must be universal (covering all cases) or the positional premise must be universal. If both are particular, the implication might hold for some cases but not the specific one. An exception: if the time/state of the conditional is exactly the same as the time/state of the positional premise, then a particular conditional can still yield a conclusion.
Example: 'Sometimes when it rains, the ground is wet' (particular) cannot be used to conclude that the ground is wet from a particular rain, unless we specify the same time.
4
Apply conditions to a kalam example
Consider the argument: 'If the world is created, then it has a Creator. The world is created. Therefore it has a Creator.' Conditional is affirmative and essential (creation necessarily implies a creator). It is universal (all created things have a creator). So the conclusion follows validly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في القياس الاستثنائي
وهو مركب من مقدمتين: إحداهما شرطية والأخرى وضع لأحد جزأيها أو رفعه ليلزم وضع الآخر أو رفعه.
ويجب إيجاب الشرطية ولزومية المتصلة وعناديّة المنفصلة وكليّتها أو كلية الوضع أو الرفع إن لم يكن وقت الاتصال والانفصال هو بعينه وقت الوضع والرفع.
القول: قد مر أن القياس الاستثنائي ما يكون عين النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل، فالمذكور فيه من النتيجة أو نقيضها إما مقدمة من مقدماته - وهو محال، وإلا لزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه أو بنقيضه أو جزء من مقدمتيه - والمقدّمة التي جزؤها قضية تكون شرطية والأخرى وضعية؛ فالقياس الاستثنائي ما يكون مركباً من مقدمتين : إحداهما شرطية والأخرى وضعيّة - أي إثبات لأحد جزأيها أو رفعه – أي نفيه – ليلزم وضع الجزء الآخر أو رفعه.
كقولنا : «كلّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة» ينتج «أن النهار موجود». ولكنه «النهار ليس بموجود» ينتج الشمس ليست بطالعة.
وكقولنا : «دائما إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً، لكن هذا العدد زوج» ينتج «أنه ليس بفرد». ولكنه ليس بزوج» ينتج «أنه فرد».
ففي المتصلات ينتج الوضع الوضع والرفع الرفع، وفي المنفصلات ينتج الوضع الرفع وبالعكس.
شرائط الانتاج في القياسات الاستثنائية:
ويعتبر في إنتاج هذا القياس شرائط :
أحدها أن تكون الشرطية موجبة، فإنّها لو كانت سالبة لم تنتج شيئاً لا الوضع ولا الرفع - فإن معنى الشرطية السالبة سلب اللزوم والعناد، وإذا لم يكن بين الأمرين لزوم أو عناد لم يلزم من وجود أحدهما أو عدمه وجود الآخر أو عدمه.
وثانيها : أن تكون الشرطيّة لزومية - إن كانت متصلة - وعنادية - إن كانت منفصلة - لا اتفاقيّة، لأنّ العلم بصدق الاتفاقية أو كذبها موقوف على العلم بصدق أحد طرفيها أو كذبه ، فلو استفيد العلم بصدق أحد الطرفين أو كذبه من الاتفاقية يلزم الدور.
وثالثها أحد الأمرين : وهو إما كلّية الشرطية أو كلية الاستثناء - أي كلية الوضع أو الرفع - فإنّه لو انتفى الأمران احتمل أن يكون اللزوم أو العناد على بعض الأوضاع والاستثناء على وضع آخر، فلا يلزم من إثبات أحد جزئي الشرطية أو نفيه ثبوت الآخر أو انتفاؤه. اللهم إلا إذا كان وقت الاتصال والانفصال ووضعها هو بعينه وقت الاستثناء ووضعه، فإنه ينتج.
The fourth chapter on the conditional syllogism. It is composed of two premises: one of them is conditional and the other is an affirmation of one of its two parts or a negation of it, in order to necessitate the affirmation of the other part or its negation. It is necessary that the conditional be affirmative, and if it is connective it must be essential, and if disjunctive it must be exclusive, and that it be universal, or that the affirmation or negation be universal, unless the time of connection or disconnection is exactly the time of the affirmation or negation. Statement: It has already passed that the conditional syllogism is that in which the very conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned explicitly. So what is mentioned of the conclusion or its contradictory is either a premise of its premises — which is impossible, otherwise it would entail proving a thing by itself or by its contradictory or by a part of its two premises — and the premise whose part is a proposition is conditional, and the other is positional. So the conditional syllogism is composed of two premises: one conditional and the other positional — i.e., the affirmation of one of its two parts or its negation — i.e., its denial — in order to necessitate the affirmation of the other part or its negation. Like our statement: 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but the sun is rising' yields 'that daytime exists.' And 'but daytime does not exist' yields 'that the sun is not rising.' And like our statement: 'Always, either this number is even or odd; but this number is even' yields 'that it is not odd.' And 'but it is not even' yields 'that it is odd.' So in connectives, affirming the antecedent affirms the consequent and denying the antecedent denies the consequent, and in disjunctives, affirming one part denies the other and vice versa. Conditions for production in conditional syllogisms: In the production of this syllogism, conditions are considered: First, that the conditional be affirmative; for if it were negative, it would not produce anything, neither affirmation nor negation — for the meaning of a negative conditional is the negation of implicative relation or incompatibility, and if there is no implication or incompatibility between the two matters, it does not follow from the existence of one or its non-existence the existence of the other or its non-existence. Second, that the conditional be implicative if it is connective, and exclusive if it is disjunctive, not accidental; because knowledge of the truth or falsity of the accidental conditional depends on knowledge of the truth of one of its two parts or its falsity; so if knowledge of the truth of one of the two parts or its falsity were derived from the accidental conditional, circularity would ensue. Third, one of two things: either the universality of the conditional or the universality of the exception — i.e., the universality of the affirmation or negation — for if both are absent, it is possible that the implication or incompatibility holds for some states and the exception for another state; so the affirmation of one of the two parts of the conditional or its negation does not entail the affirmation of the other or its negation. Unless the time of connection or disconnection and its state is exactly the time of the exception and its state, then it produces.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i) and lists its necessary conditions for valid inference. The author first distinguishes it from the categorical syllogism: here the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated in one of the premises. That premise is called the 'positional' premise (al-muqaddima al-wad'iyya), which affirms or denies one part of the conditional. The other premise is the conditional itself. The argument proceeds: if the conditional connects A and B (e.g., 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists'), then affirming A yields B, and denying B yields not-A. The author gives examples using the sun/day and even/odd number, which are standard logical examples. For disjunctives (e.g., 'Either the number is even or odd'), affirming one part necessitates negating the other, and negating one necessitates affirming the other. The author then lists three conditions for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion: (1) The conditional must be affirmative; a negative conditional denies any implication or incompatibility, so no inference is possible. (2) The conditional must be essential (lizumiyya) for connectives and exclusive ('inadiyya) for disjunctives, not accidental (ittifaqiyya); otherwise, we would need prior knowledge of one part, leading to circularity (dawr). (3) Either the conditional itself must be universal, or the exception (the positional premise) must be universal; otherwise, the implication might hold only for some cases while the exception applies to a different case, breaking the inference. An exception is noted: if the time or state of the connection/disconnection is exactly the same as the time/state of the exception, then a particular conditional can still yield a conclusion. This precision is crucial for applying conditional syllogisms in legal reasoning (e.g., conditional contracts or obligations).

Key Takeaway
For a conditional syllogism to be valid, the conditional must be affirmative, essential (not accidental), and either universal or the exception universal (or temporally coincident).

The Syllogism

Muttasil Conditional Syllogisms: Modus Ponens and Tollens
Defines muttasil syllogism rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the production rules of muttasil syllogisms (affirming antecedent affirms consequent; denying consequent denies antecedent) to derive valid conclusions from conditional premises in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
In a muttasil syllogism, affirming the antecedent affirms the consequent; denying the consequent denies the antecedent.

Conditional arguments are the backbone of many legal and theological inferences in Islam. Understanding the muttasil syllogism ensures you know which moves are valid when dealing with 'if...then' statements. This lesson extracts the rule from the text and shows you how to apply it in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the major premise that is a muttasil (implicational) statement of the form 'if A then B'. For example, 'If the water is najis, then wudu is invalid.'
Ensure the connective expresses a logical implication, not just temporal sequence.
2
Determine the exceptional premise
The second premise either affirms the antecedent (al-muqaddam) or denies the consequent (al-tali). In the text: 'wad' al-muqaddam yantij wad' al-tali' (affirmation of antecedent yields affirmation of consequent) and 'raf' al-tali yantij raf' al-muqaddam' (negation of consequent yields negation of antecedent).
3
Apply modus ponens if antecedent is affirmed
If the second premise affirms the antecedent, you may validly conclude the consequent. Example: 'It is najis, therefore wudu is invalid.'
This is wad' al-muqaddam.
4
Apply modus tollens if consequent is denied
If the second premise denies the consequent, you may validly conclude the negation of the antecedent. Example: 'Wudu is not invalid, therefore the water is not najis.'
This is raf' al-tali.
5
Derive the conclusion
State the conclusion clearly. Verify that the reasoning follows one of the two valid forms.
Invalid forms include denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الاستثنائي وكقولنا: «دائما إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً، لكن هذا العدد زوج» ينتج «أنه ليس بفرد». ولكنه ليس بزوج» ينتج «أنه فرد». وفي المتصلات ينتج الوضع الوضع والرفع الرفع، وفي المنفصلات ينتج الوضع الرفع وبالعكس.
The conditional syllogism. And like our saying: 'Always either this number is even or odd, but this number is even' produces 'that it is not odd.' 'But it is not even' produces 'that it is odd.' In implicationals, affirmation produces affirmation and negation produces negation; in disjunctives, affirmation produces negation and vice versa.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and its two types: muttasil (implicational/conditional) and munfasil (disjunctive). The author gives an example of the disjunctive syllogism: 'Either this number is even or odd; it is even, therefore it is not odd.' And 'it is not even, therefore it is odd.' Then the rule: In implicational conditionals, affirming the antecedent affirms the consequent, and denying the consequent denies the antecedent (the modus ponens and modus tollens common in logic). In disjunctive syllogisms, affirming one alternative denies the other, and denying one affirms the other. The author uses the terms 'wad' (affirmation) and 'raf' (negation) to refer to the position or removal of one of the parts. For example, in fiqh: 'Either this act is wajib or it is mubah; it is not wajib, therefore it is mubah' follows the disjunctive rule.

Key Takeaway

Munfasil Disjunctive Syllogisms: Affirmation Denies, Negation Affirms
Defines munfasil syllogism rule with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the production rules of munfasil syllogisms (affirming one disjunct denies the other; denying one affirms the other) to derive valid conclusions from disjunctive premises in Islamic legal classifications.

Core Idea
In a munfasil syllogism, affirming one alternative denies the other; denying one affirms the other.

Disjunctive reasoning is essential when dealing with mutually exclusive categories, such as halal/haram or wajib/mubah. This lesson unpacks the rule for munfasil (disjunctive) syllogisms from the text and demonstrates its application in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find the major premise that is a munfasil (disjunctive) statement of the form 'either A or B', where the disjuncts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive. Example: 'Either this act is wajib or it is mubah.'
The disjunction must be exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) for the rule to apply straightforwardly.
2
Determine the exceptional premise
The second premise either affirms one disjunct (wad' ahadihima) or denies one disjunct (raf' ahadihima). From the text: 'في المنفصلات ينتج الوضع الرفع وبالعكس' meaning affirmation yields negation and vice versa.
3
Apply affirmation rule
If the second premise affirms one disjunct, you may validly conclude the negation of the other disjunct. Example: 'It is wajib' yields 'It is not mubah'.
This is wad' ahadihima yantij raf' al-akhar.
4
Apply negation rule
If the second premise denies one disjunct, you may validly conclude the affirmation of the other disjunct. Example: 'It is not wajib' yields 'It is mubah'.
This is raf' ahadihima yantij wad' al-akhar.
5
Derive the conclusion
State the conclusion clearly. Verify that the disjuncts were truly exclusive.
If the disjunction is not exclusive (e.g., 'either A or B or both'), the rule may not hold.

Three Conditions for Valid Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates three conditions for conditional syllogisms to produce a valid conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate a conditional syllogism in Islamic logic by checking if it satisfies the three conditions of affirmation, necessity, and universality.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism proves a conclusion only when its conditional premise is affirmative, necessary, and adequately universal.

The author of this passage expands on the standard conditions for a conditional syllogism to produce a valid conclusion. These conditions prevent fallacious inferences where the connection between antecedent and consequent is weak or coincidental. By mastering these criteria, you will be able to critique theological and legal arguments that rely on conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise and the exception
Find the 'if...then' statement (the conditional) and the second premise that affirms or denies one part (the exception). For example, in 'If Zayd came at Dhuhr with Amr, I honor him; but he came at that time, so I honored him.'
The exception is often indicated by 'لكن' or 'فـ'.
2
Check the conditional's quality
Ensure the conditional is affirmative. If it is negative (e.g., 'It is not the case that if...'), no conclusion can be drawn because the implication or opposition is denied.
A negative conditional denies any connection between the parts.
3
Check the conditional's modality
For a connected conditional (muttaṣil), it must be necessary (luzūmiyya); for a disjunctive (munfaṣil), it must be oppositional (ʿinādiyya). Accidental conditionals (ittifāqiyya) are invalid because they lead to circular reasoning.
An accidental conditional has no real logical connection.
4
Check universality
Verify that either the conditional is universal (applies to all positions) or the exception is universal (covers all relevant positions). If neither is universal, the conclusion does not follow. The author adds that universality must consider all positions not contradictory to the antecedent.
Permanence in time alone is insufficient; all non-contradictory positions must be covered.
5
Apply the conditions to a real argument
Test a conditional syllogism from Islamic theology or jurisprudence against the three conditions. For instance, 'If the world is created, it has a Creator; it is created; therefore it has a Creator.'
If any condition fails, the argument is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إن كانت متصلة - وعنادية - إن كانت منفصلة - لا اتفاقية، لأن العلم بصدق الاتفاقية أو كذبها موقوف على العلم بصدق أحد طرفيها أو كذبه، فلو استفيد العلم بصدق أحد الطرفين أو كذبه من الاتفاقية يلزم الدور. وثالثها أحد الأمرين: وهو إما كلية الشرطية أو كلية الاستثناء - أي كلية الوضع أو الرفع - فإنه لو انتفى الأمران احتمل أن يكون اللزوم أو العناد على بعض الأوضاع والاستثناء على وضع آخر، فلا يلزم من إثبات أحد جزئي الشرطية أو نفيه ثبوت الآخر أو انتفاؤه. اللهم إلا إذا كان وقت الاتصال والانفصال ووضعها هو بعينه وقت الاستثناء ووضعه، فإنه ينتج القياس حينئذ ضرورة كقولنا: إن قدم زيد في وقت الظهر مع عمرو أكرمته، لكنه قدم مع عمرو في ذلك الوقت فأكرمته. والمراد بكلية الاستثناء ليس تحققه في جميع الأزمنة فقط، بل مع جميع الأوضاع التي لا تنافي وضع المقدم. فإذا قلنا: قد يكون إذا كان (اب) ف (ج د) وكان (اب) واقعاً دائماً، لم يلزم بمجرد ذلك تحقق (ج د) في الجملة، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان (اب) كما هو واقع دائماً كان واقعاً مع جميع الأوضاع التي لا تنافي (اب)، وليس يلزم من وقوعه دائماً وقوعه مع جميع الأوضاع غير المتنافية، الجواز أن يكون له وضع غير مناف ولا يكون له تحقق أصلاً. والمذكور في بعض الكتب «أن دوام الوضع والرفع منتج»، وهو إنما يصح لو فسرنا الشرطية الكلية بما يكون اللزوم أو العناد فيه متحققاً مع الأوضاع المتحققة في نفس الأمر، حتى يلزم من دوام الوضع أو الرفع تحققه مع جميع الأوضاع المعتبرة. وليس كذلك، بل هي مفسرة بتحقق اللزوم أو العناد على الأوضاع غير المنافية للمقدم، فيجوز أن يكون اللزوم في الجزئية له شرط لا يوجد أبداً مع وجود الملزوم دائماً، وحينئذ لا يلزم وجود اللازم لعدم تحقق وضع الملزوم مع اللازم وشرطه لانتفائهما دائماً، كما يصدق قولنا: «قد يكون إذا كان الواجب موجوداً كان الجزء موجوداً - من الشكل الثالث - والواجب موجود دائماً»، ولا يلزم منه أن يكون الجزء موجوداً في الجملة، لأن اللزوم هاهنا إنما هو على وضع اجتماع الواجب والجزء في الوجود، وهو ليس بواقع أصلاً.
Conditions for production in conditional syllogisms. And conditions are considered in the production of this syllogism: First, that the conditional be affirmative; for if it were negative, it would not produce anything, neither affirmation nor negation - for the meaning of a negative conditional is the negation of implication and opposition; and if there is no implication or opposition between the two matters, then from the existence or non-existence of one does not follow the existence or non-existence of the other. Second, that the conditional be implicational if it is connected, and oppositional if it is disjunctive, not accidental; because knowledge of the truth or falsity of an accidental conditional depends on knowledge of the truth or falsity of one of its two parts; if knowledge of the truth or falsity of one part is derived from the accidental conditional, circularity follows. Third, one of two things: either the universality of the conditional or the universality of the exception - i.e., universality of affirmation or negation; for if both are lacking, it is possible that the implication or opposition is with respect to some positions and the exception with respect to another position, so from establishing or negating one of the two parts of the conditional, the establishment or negation of the other does not follow. Unless the time of connection/disconnection and its position is exactly the time of the exception and its position; then the syllogism necessarily produces, as in our saying: 'If Zayd came at the time of Dhuhr with Amr, I honor him; but he came with Amr at that time, so I honored him.' And by universality of the exception is meant not just its realization in all times, but with all positions that do not contradict the position of the antecedent. So if we say: 'It may be that if A is B then C is D, and A is B always,' it does not follow from that alone that C is D at all; rather that would follow if A is B as it is always realized, it is realized with all positions that do not contradict A is B; and from its always occurring it does not follow that it occurs with all non-contradictory positions, for it is possible that it has a non-contradictory position but has no realization at all. And what is mentioned in some books that 'the permanence of affirmation and negation is productive' is only valid if we interpret the universal conditional as that in which implication or opposition is realized with the positions realized in actuality, such that from the permanence of affirmation or negation its realization with all considered positions follows. But it is not so; rather it is interpreted as the realization of implication or opposition with positions that do not contradict the antecedent. So it is possible that the implication in the particular has a condition that never exists with the existence of the unqualified always; and then the existence of the consequent does not follow due to the non-realization of the position of the unqualified with the consequent and its condition, because both are always non-existent. As is true of our saying: 'It may be that if the Necessary Existent exists, the part exists - from the third figure - and the Necessary Existent always exists,' and it does not follow from that that the part exists at all, because the implication here is only upon the position of the conjunction of the Necessary Existent and the part in existence, which never actually occurs.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage enumerates three conditions for a conditional syllogism to produce a valid conclusion. The author is expanding on the matn (original text) which likely stated these conditions briefly. The first condition is that the conditional premise must be affirmative. If it is negative, it denies any implication or opposition, so nothing follows. For example, a negative conditional like 'It is not the case that if it rains, the ground is wet' does not give us any inference. The second condition is that the conditional must be necessary (luzumiyya) for muttasil or oppositional (inadiyya) for munfasil, not accidental (ittifaqiyya). An accidental conditional is like 'If the sky is clear, then the muezzin called the adhan' where there is no real connection. Using it would lead to circularity because to know its truth we need prior knowledge of one part. The third condition is that either the conditional itself is universal (covering all cases) or the exception (affirmation/negation) is universal. If neither is universal, the conclusion does not follow. The author then elaborates on what universality of the exception means, correcting a view from some books that 'permanence of affirmation/negation is productive.' He explains that it must hold for all positions that do not contradict the antecedent. He gives a metaphysical example: 'If the Necessary Existent exists, the part exists' in the third figure; though the Necessary Existent always exists, the part does not necessarily exist because the implication is conditional on a specific conjunction that never occurs. This shows the nuance of conditional syllogism theory.

Key Terms
شرطية موجبة
شرطية لزومية
اتفاقية
كلية
استثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid condition for a conditional syllogism to produce a conclusion?
A) The conditional must be negative.
B) The conditional must be accidental.
C) The conditional must be affirmative and necessary.
D) The exception must be particular.

Key Takeaway
The three conditions (affirmative, necessary, universal) guard against fallacious inferences in conditional arguments used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a conditional argument from a work of usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'If the revealed text is general, it applies to all cases; this text is general; therefore it applies to all cases') and test it against the three conditions. Explain which conditions are satisfied and which (if any) are not.
?
Consider the following argument from Kalam: 'If the world were eternal, it would not require a cause; but it does require a cause; therefore it is not eternal.' Check the conditions. Is this a valid conditional syllogism? Why or why not?

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
The True Condition for Universality in Conditional Syllogisms
Author refutes perpetual occurrence suffices for universal conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a universal conditional proposition requires perpetual occurrence or universality across all non-contradictory situations.

Core Idea
Universality requires implication across all non-contradictory situations, not just perpetual occurrence of the antecedent.

A common claim in logic is that if the antecedent of a conditional occurs perpetually, then the conditional is universal and yields a conclusion. The author here critically refines this claim, showing that perpetual occurrence is insufficient. Instead, the implication must hold in all situations that do not contradict the antecedent. This lesson unpacks the argument and its implications for conditional syllogisms, especially in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Refuted
Find the claim that 'perpetuity of placement and removal is productive' (دوام الوضع والرفع منتج). This is the target of the author's critique.
The claim is mentioned as found in some books.
2
Understand the Example
The author gives an example: 'If Zayd came at Dhuhr with Amr, I honored him.' The conditional is universal only if it holds for all situations that do not contradict Zayd's coming with Amr at Dhuhr.
Perpetual occurrence of Zayd's coming does not alone guarantee the conditional is universal.
3
Distinguish Two Interpretations
One interpretation: universal conditional covers all actual situations (then perpetual occurrence suffices). The author's interpretation: it covers all non-contradictory situations, which is stricter.
This distinction is the crux of the critique.
4
Analyze the Third-Figure Counterexample
The author gives a kalam example: 'If the Necessary Existent exists, then the part exists' in the third figure, with the Necessary Existent existing perpetually. Yet the part does not exist because implication depends on the combination, which never occurs.
This shows that perpetual occurrence does not guarantee the consequent.
5
Formulate the Correct Condition
Conclude that for a universal conditional, the implication must hold upon all situations that do not contradict the antecedent, not merely upon its perpetual occurrence.
This refines the standard definition of universal conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس حينئذ ضرورة كقولنا : إن قدم زيد في وقت الظهر مع عمرو أكرمته، لكنه قدم مع عمرو في ذلك الوقت فأكرمته»، والمراد بكلية الاستثناء ليس تحققة في جميع الأزمنة فقط، بل مع جميع الأوضاع التي لاتنا في وضع المقدّم ، فإذا قلنا : قد يكون إذا كان (اب) فرج (د) » وكان (اب) واقعاً دائماً، لم يلزم بمجرد ذلك تحقق (ج د) في الجملة، وإنما يلزم ذلك لوكان (اب) كما هو اقع دائماً كان واقعاً مع جميع الأوضاع التي لاتنافي (اب)، وليس يلزم من وقوعه دائماً وقوعه مع جميع الأوضاع الغير المتنافية، الجواز أن يكون له وضع غير مناف ولا يكون له تحقق أصلاً. والمذكور في بعض الكتب «أن دوام الوضع والرفع منتج»، وهو إنما يصح لوفسرنا الشرطية الكلية بما يكون اللزوم أو العناد فيه متحققاً مع الأوضاع المتحققة في نفس الأمر ، حتّى يلزم من دوام الوضع أو الرفع تحققه مع جميع الأوضاع المعتبرة. وليس كذلك، بل هي مفسرة بتحقق اللزوم أو العناد على الأوضاع الغير المنافية للمقدّم، فيجوز أن يكون اللزوم في الجزئية له شرط لا يوجد أبداً مع وجود الملزوم دائماً، وحينئذ لا يلزم وجود اللازم لعدم تحقق وضع الملزوم مع اللازم وشرطه لانتفائهما دائماً، كما يصدق قولنا : «قد يكون إذا كان الواجب موجوداً كان الجزء موجوداً - من الشكل الثالث - والواجب موجود دائماً»، ولا يلزم منه أن يكون الجزء موجوداً في الجملة، لأن اللزوم هاهنا إنما هو على وضع اجتماع الواجب والجزء في الوجود، وهو ليس بواقع أصلاً.
The syllogism then necessarily, as we say: 'If Zayd came at the time of Dhuhr with 'Amr, I honored him. But he came with 'Amr at that time, so I honored him.' What is meant by universality of the exception is not its actuality in all times only, but with all situations that do not contradict the situation of the antecedent. So if we say: 'It may be that if (A B) then (C D),' and (A B) is always actual, it does not follow from that alone that (C D) is actual at all, rather it follows only if (A B), being always actual, is actual with all situations that do not contradict (A B). It does not follow from its being always actual that it is actual with all non-contradictory situations, because it is possible that it has a situation that is non-contradictory and it has no actuality at all. And it is mentioned in some books that 'perpetuity of placement and removal is productive.' This is valid only if we interpret the universal conditional as one in which the implication or opposition is actualized with the situations that are actual in themselves, so that from perpetuity of placement or removal, its actuality with all considered situations follows. It is not like that; rather it is interpreted as the actualization of implication or opposition upon situations that are non-contradictory to the antecedent. So it is possible that the implication in the particular has a condition that never exists with the existence of the implicans always, and then the existence of the implicatum does not follow because the situation of the implicans with the implicatum and its condition is not realized, due to their perpetual non-existence. As we say: 'It may be that if the Necessary Existent exists, then the part exists—in the third figure—and the Necessary Existent exists always,' yet it does not follow that the part exists at all, because the implication here is only upon the situation of the combination of the Necessary Existent and the part in existence, which is not actual at all.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a claim found in some books about the condition for universality in conditional propositions used in exceptional syllogisms. The author begins by giving an example of a conditional syllogism: 'If Zayd came at Dhuhr with 'Amr, I honored him; he came with 'Amr at that time, so I honored him.' He then explains that the universality of the exception (the conditional premise) does not mean merely that the condition occurs at all times; rather, it must occur with all situations that do not contradict the antecedent. He then critizes the claim that perpetual occurrence of the antecedent or its negation is sufficient to produce a conclusion. He argues that if we interpret the universal conditional as requiring implication or opposition to hold in all actual situations, then perpetuity would be sufficient. But the proper interpretation is that implication holds for situations non-contradictory to the antecedent. Therefore, it is possible that the implication has a condition that never occurs, even if the antecedent is always true. He gives an example from the third figure: 'If the Necessary Existent exists, then the part exists; and the Necessary Existent exists always' – but the part does not necessarily exist because the implication depends on the combination of both, which never occurs. Thus, the claim that perpetual occurrence is sufficient is shown to be false.

Key Terms
الشرطية الكلية (al-shartiyya al-kulliyya): A conditional proposition where the implication holds for all situations that do not contradict the antecedent. [Note: In some texts, it is defined as covering all times, but here it is refined to include all non-contradictory conditions.]
دوام الوضع والرفع (dawām al-waḍʿ wa al-rafʿ): The perpetual occurrence of the placement or removal of the antecedent or consequent.
اللزوم (al-luzūm): Implication; the necessary connection between antecedent and consequent in a conditional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the author's third-figure example, why does the part not exist despite the Necessary Existent always existing?
A) Because the conditional is false.
B) Because the implication requires a condition (combination of both) that never occurs.
C) Because the Necessary Existent does not exist.
D) Because the part is impossible.

Key Takeaway
A universal conditional requires implication to hold across all non-contradictory situations, not merely across all actual situations or times.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Consider the conditional proposition: 'If the world is temporally originated (حادث), then it has a creator (محدث).' The antecedent 'the world is temporally originated' is perpetually true (the world is always temporally originated). According to the author's refined condition, does this conditional qualify as universal? Justify your answer by considering whether the implication holds for all non-contradictory situations.
?
Task: Find or construct a conditional from a kalam text (e.g., about divine attributes) and test it against the author's criterion. Identify a situation that is non-contradictory to the antecedent but where the consequent might not hold if the condition is not properly universal.

Rules for Connected Conditional Syllogisms
Author states valid and invalid inferences for connected conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of modus ponens and modus tollens in connected conditional syllogisms and identify the invalid converse forms.

Core Idea
In a connected conditional, affirming antecedent or denying consequent yields valid conclusions.

This passage from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's lexicon presents fundamental rules for exceptional syllogisms. We focus on connected conditionals (al-shartiyya al-muttasila), where implication (luzūm) between antecedent and consequent is central. Mastering these rules is essential for sound reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the connected conditional
Look for an 'if...then' structure. For example: 'If this is wine, then it is forbidden.' The antecedent is 'this is wine', consequent 'it is forbidden'.
The passage calls the antecedent 'muqaddam' and consequent 'tāli'.
2
Apply modus ponens (istithnā' ‘ayn al-muqaddam)
Affirm the antecedent in the exception to obtain the consequent. Example: 'It is wine, therefore it is forbidden.' This is valid because otherwise the implication would break.
The author says: 'excepting the very antecedent produces the very consequent.'
3
Apply modus tollens (istithnā' naqīḍ al-tālī)
Deny the consequent in the exception to obtain the contradictory of the antecedent. Example: 'It is not forbidden, therefore it is not wine.' This is valid.
The author says: 'excepting the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent.'
4
Recognize invalid converse forms
Affirming the consequent (e.g., 'it is forbidden, therefore it is wine') or denying the antecedent (e.g., 'it is not wine, therefore it is not forbidden') are not valid. Reason: the consequent may be broader (other intoxicants are also forbidden).
The passage explicitly says 'not the converse in either of them.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الاستثنائي
١١٣- نتائج القياسات الاستثنائية
قال والشرطية الموضوعة فيه إن كانت متصلة فاستثناء عين المقدم ينتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم، وإلا لبطل اللزوم دون العكس في شيء منهما لاحتمال كون التالي أعم من المقدم. وإن كانت منفصلة: فإن كانت حقيقية فاستثناء عين أي جزء كان ينتج نقيض الآخر - لاستحالة الجمع - واستثناء نقيض أي جزء كان ينتج عين الآخر – لاستحالة الخلو. وإن كانت مانعة الجمع ينتج القسم الأوّل فقط لامتناع الاجتماع دون الخلق. وإن كانت مانعة الخلو ينتج القسم الثاني فقط لامتناع الخلو دون الجمع.
القول: الشرطية التي هي جزء القياس الاستثنائي إما متصلة أو منفصلة :
فإن كانت متصلة:
ينتج استثناء عين مقدّمها عين التالي - والا لزم انفكاك اللازم عن الملزوم فيبطل اللزوم - واستثناء نقيض تاليها نقيض المقدم - وإلا لزم وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم فيبطل اللزوم أيضاً، دون العكس في شيء منهما، أي لا ينتج استثناء عين التالي عين المقدم، ولا استثناء نقيض المقدّم نقيض التالي، لجواز أن يكون التالي أعم من عين المقدم، فلا يلزم من وجود اللازم وجود الملزوم ، ولا من عدم الملزوم عدم اللازم.
The exceptional syllogism. 113- Results of exceptional syllogisms. He said: And the conditional placed in it—if it is connected, then excepting the very antecedent produces the very consequent, and excepting the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent; otherwise the implication would be invalid. But not the converse in either of them, because of the possibility that the consequent is more general than the antecedent. And if it is disconnected: if it is real, then excepting the very any part produces the contradictory of the other—due to the impossibility of combination—and excepting the contradictory of any part produces the very other—due to the impossibility of emptiness. And if it is preventing combination, it produces only the first part—due to the prevention of combination, not of emptiness. And if it is preventing emptiness, it produces only the second part—due to the prevention of emptiness, not of combination. Statement: The conditional that is part of the exceptional syllogism is either connected or disconnected. If it is connected: excepting the very antecedent produces the very consequent—otherwise the separation of the implicatum from the implicans would be necessary, invalidating the implication—and excepting the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent—otherwise the existence of the implicans without the implicatum would follow, also invalidating the implication. But not the converse in either of them, i.e., excepting the very consequent does not produce the very antecedent, nor does excepting the contradictory of the antecedent produce the contradictory of the consequent, because it is possible that the consequent is more general than the very antecedent; so from the existence of the implicatum the existence of the implicans does not follow, nor from the non-existence of the implicans does the non-existence of the implicatum follow.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage enumerates the rules for drawing conclusions in exceptional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī). The author first gives a concise statement: For a connected conditional (muttaṣila), excepting (i.e., affirming) the very antecedent yields the very consequent, and excepting the contradictory of the consequent yields the contradictory of the antecedent. These are valid because otherwise the implication would be broken. The converse is not valid: affirming the consequent does not affirm the antecedent, nor does denying the antecedent deny the consequent, because the consequent may be broader. For a disconnected conditional (munfaṣila), three types are distinguished. (1) Real disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya): affirming one part yields the contradictory of the other (since both cannot be true), and denying one part yields the other (since they cannot both be false). (2) Preventing combination (māni‘at al-jam‘): only affirming one yields the contradictory of the other (since combination is impossible, but emptiness is possible). (3) Preventing emptiness (māni‘at al-khuluw): only denying one yields the other (since emptiness is impossible, but combination is possible). The author then repeats the rules in a more detailed statement, adding explanatory justifications: in the connected case, if affirming the antecedent did not produce the consequent, the implicatum would be separate from the implicans, destroying the implication; similarly, if denying the consequent did not produce denial of the antecedent, the implicans would exist without the implicatum. The converse fails due to possible breadth of the consequent. For disconnected, the same as before. For Islamic examples: In fiqh, 'If this is wine, it is forbidden' (connected). Affirm 'it is wine' leads to 'it is forbidden'. Deny 'it is forbidden' leads to 'it is not wine'. But from 'it is forbidden' we cannot conclude it is wine, as other intoxicants are also forbidden. For disconnected real: 'Either this act is obligatory or it is forbidden' – if it is obligatory, it is not forbidden; if it is not obligatory, it is forbidden (if no third option).

Key Terms
الشرطية المتصلة
المقدم
التالي
استثناء عين المقدم
استثناء نقيض التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a connected conditional, which of the following is a valid inference?
A) Affirming the antecedent yields the consequent.
B) Affirming the consequent yields the antecedent.
C) Denying the antecedent yields the denial of the consequent.
D) Denying the consequent yields the antecedent.

Key Takeaway
Connected conditional syllogisms allow only modus ponens and modus tollens; affirming consequent or denying antecedent are fallacies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid modus ponens argument on a fiqh topic. For example: 'If an animal is not slaughtered properly, it is carrion. This animal was not slaughtered properly, therefore it is carrion.'
?
Identify the invalid converse forms in the following: 'If the prayer is valid, then the wudu is intact. The prayer is valid. Therefore the wudu is intact. Is this valid? Why or why not?'

Rules for Disconnected Conditional Syllogisms
Author states inference rules for three types of disconnected conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disconnected conditional and apply the correct inference rules for each.

Core Idea
In disconnected conditionals, inference rules depend on whether disjunction prevents combination, emptiness, or both.

We now turn to disconnected conditionals (al-shartiyya al-munfaṣila), where the parts are in opposition. The passage distinguishes three types: real disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya), preventing combination (māni‘at al-jam‘), and preventing emptiness (māni‘at al-khuluw). Each has its own rule for valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Based on the source's description: real disjunction prevents both combination and emptiness; preventing combination only allows both to be false; preventing emptiness only allows both to be true.
The passage uses terms: حقيقية, مانعة الجمع, مانعة الخلو.
2
Apply rules for real disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya)
Affirming one part yields the contradictory of the other; denying one part yields the other. Example: 'Either this act is obligatory or forbidden.' If it is obligatory, it is not forbidden. If it is not obligatory, it is forbidden.
The author says: 'excepting the very any part produces the contradictory of the other...and excepting the contradictory of any part produces the very other.'
3
Apply rules for preventing combination (māni‘at al-jam‘)
Only affirming one part yields the contradictory of the other. Denying one part does not yield the other because both could be false. Example: 'Either the witness is truthful or lying (they cannot both be true).' If truthful, not lying. But if not truthful, they could be lying or not? Not necessarily, so invalid.
The author says: 'it produces only the first part' meaning only the inference from affirming one to denying the other.
4
Apply rules for preventing emptiness (māni‘at al-khuluw)
Only denying one part yields the other. Affirming one does not deny the other because both could be true. Example: 'Either the patient has disease X or Y (they cannot both be false).' If not X, then Y. But if X, Y may also be present? So not valid.
The author says: 'it produces only the second part' meaning only the inference from denying one to affirming the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الاستثنائي
١١٣- نتائج القياسات الاستثنائية
قال والشرطية الموضوعة فيه إن كانت متصلة فاستثناء عين المقدم ينتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم، وإلا لبطل اللزوم دون العكس في شيء منهما لاحتمال كون التالي أعم من المقدم. وإن كانت منفصلة: فإن كانت حقيقية فاستثناء عين أي جزء كان ينتج نقيض الآخر - لاستحالة الجمع - واستثناء نقيض أي جزء كان ينتج عين الآخر – لاستحالة الخلو. وإن كانت مانعة الجمع ينتج القسم الأوّل فقط لامتناع الاجتماع دون الخلق. وإن كانت مانعة الخلو ينتج القسم الثاني فقط لامتناع الخلو دون الجمع.
القول: الشرطية التي هي جزء القياس الاستثنائي إما متصلة أو منفصلة :
فإن كانت متصلة:
ينتج استثناء عين مقدّمها عين التالي - والا لزم انفكاك اللازم عن الملزوم فيبطل اللزوم - واستثناء نقيض تاليها نقيض المقدم - وإلا لزم وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم فيبطل اللزوم أيضاً، دون العكس في شيء منهما، أي لا ينتج استثناء عين التالي عين المقدم، ولا استثناء نقيض المقدّم نقيض التالي، لجواز أن يكون التالي أعم من عين المقدم، فلا يلزم من وجود اللازم وجود الملزوم ، ولا من عدم الملزوم عدم اللازم.
The exceptional syllogism. 113- Results of exceptional syllogisms. He said: And the conditional placed in it—if it is connected, then excepting the very antecedent produces the very consequent, and excepting the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent; otherwise the implication would be invalid. But not the converse in either of them, because of the possibility that the consequent is more general than the antecedent. And if it is disconnected: if it is real, then excepting the very any part produces the contradictory of the other—due to the impossibility of combination—and excepting the contradictory of any part produces the very other—due to the impossibility of emptiness. And if it is preventing combination, it produces only the first part—due to the prevention of combination, not of emptiness. And if it is preventing emptiness, it produces only the second part—due to the prevention of emptiness, not of combination. Statement: The conditional that is part of the exceptional syllogism is either connected or disconnected. If it is connected: excepting the very antecedent produces the very consequent—otherwise the separation of the implicatum from the implicans would be necessary, invalidating the implication—and excepting the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent—otherwise the existence of the implicans without the implicatum would follow, also invalidating the implication. But not the converse in either of them, i.e., excepting the very consequent does not produce the very antecedent, nor does excepting the contradictory of the antecedent produce the contradictory of the consequent, because it is possible that the consequent is more general than the very antecedent; so from the existence of the implicatum the existence of the implicans does not follow, nor from the non-existence of the implicans does the non-existence of the implicatum follow.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage enumerates the rules for drawing conclusions in exceptional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī). The author first gives a concise statement: For a connected conditional (muttaṣila), excepting (i.e., affirming) the very antecedent yields the very consequent, and excepting the contradictory of the consequent yields the contradictory of the antecedent. These are valid because otherwise the implication would be broken. The converse is not valid: affirming the consequent does not affirm the antecedent, nor does denying the antecedent deny the consequent, because the consequent may be broader. For a disconnected conditional (munfaṣila), three types are distinguished. (1) Real disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya): affirming one part yields the contradictory of the other (since both cannot be true), and denying one part yields the other (since they cannot both be false). (2) Preventing combination (māni‘at al-jam‘): only affirming one yields the contradictory of the other (since combination is impossible, but emptiness is possible). (3) Preventing emptiness (māni‘at al-khuluw): only denying one yields the other (since emptiness is impossible, but combination is possible). The author then repeats the rules in a more detailed statement, adding explanatory justifications: in the connected case, if affirming the antecedent did not produce the consequent, the implicatum would be separate from the implicans, destroying the implication; similarly, if denying the consequent did not produce denial of the antecedent, the implicans would exist without the implicatum. The converse fails due to possible breadth of the consequent. For disconnected, the same as before. For Islamic examples: In fiqh, 'If this is wine, it is forbidden' (connected). Affirm 'it is wine' leads to 'it is forbidden'. Deny 'it is forbidden' leads to 'it is not wine'. But from 'it is forbidden' we cannot conclude it is wine, as other intoxicants are also forbidden. For disconnected real: 'Either this act is obligatory or it is forbidden' – if it is obligatory, it is not forbidden; if it is not obligatory, it is forbidden (if no third option).

Checkpoint
In a man'at al-khuluw disjunction, which inference is valid?
A) Affirming one part yields the contradictory of the other.
B) Denying one part yields the other part.
C) Both A and B.
D) Neither A nor B.

Key Takeaway
Valid inference in disconnected conditionals depends on the type: real allows both affirming and denying; mani'at al-jam' allows only affirming; mani'at al-khuluw allows only denying.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each type of disconnected conditional, construct a valid argument using an Islamic example. For real: 'Either this act is sunnah or bid'ah. It is sunnah, so it is not bid'ah.' For mani'at al-jam': 'Either the witness is truthful or lying (they cannot both be true). If he is truthful, he is not lying.' For mani'at al-khuluw: 'Either the patient has disease X or Y (they cannot both be false). If he does not have X, he has Y.'
?
Classify the following disjunction and state which inferences are valid: 'Either the prayer is valid or invalid.' (Real, so both affirming one and denying one yield the other's contradictory/affirmation.)
